Causality and Created Things

The First Type: Considering a Thing with Its Cause
Enumeration of the first type (four causes) with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of causes (efficient, receptive, formal, final) and how a thing can be considered in relation to each, with specific examples.

Core Idea
Four causes define a thing: agent, matter, form, and purpose.

In this lesson, we examine the first type of 'consideration' (اعتبار) — how a concept can be defined by its relation to various causes. The author provides clear examples for each of the four Aristotelian causes, showing how the meaning of a term like 'giving' inherently points to the giver. Understanding these causal relations is foundational to analyzing theological terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the examples settle before interpreting.
Notice that each cause is introduced with 'مع' (with).
2
Identify each cause and its example
List the four causes: efficient (فاعل), receptive (قابل), formal (صورة), final (غاية). For each, identify the given example and explain how the example relates to its cause.
The example for formal cause is 'الأفطس' (flat-nosed), which is a form inhering in the nose.
3
Reflect on the relational nature
Consider why each example is defined by its cause. For instance, 'giving' (العطاء) only makes sense with a giver. How does this help in analyzing broader theological concepts?
This connects to the idea that many attributes are relational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والأول ) ، وهو المعتبر بالقياس إلى العلة : ( إما ) معتبر ( مع الفاعل، نحو العطاء ) فإنه اسم لفائدة اعتبرت إضافتها إلى الفاعل ( أو ) مع القابل نحو الفطوسة، وهي الذي في الأنف اعتبر فيها الشيء بالإضافة إلى قابله ( أو ) مع (الصورة نحو الأفطس ) (أو) مع (الغاية نحو الخاتم؛ فإنه حلقة يتزين بها في الإصبع، وذلك التزين هو الغاية المقصودة من تلك الحلقة ( والثاني ) : وهو المعتبر بالنسبة إلى المعلول (نحو الخالق) والرازق وأمثالهما ، مما اعتبر فيه الشيء مقيساً إلى معلوله .
And the first is what is considered in relation to the cause: it is either considered with the efficient cause, like 'giving' (العطاء) – for it is a name for a benefit whose addition to the agent is considered – or with the receptive cause (قابل), like 'flatness' (الفطوسة), which is the concavity in the nose; the thing is considered in it by addition to its receiver, or with the formal cause (صورة), like 'flat-nosed' (الأفطس), or with the final cause (غاية), like 'ring' (الخاتم); for it is a ring that is adorned on the finger, and that adornment is the intended goal of that ring. And the second is what is considered in relation to the effect, like 'Creator' (الخالق) and 'Provider' (الرازق) and similar terms, in which the thing is considered measured to its effect.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples for the first two types of consideration. For the first type (consideration with a cause), the author lists the four Aristotelian causes: efficient (فاعل), material/receptive (قابل/مادة), formal (صورة), and final (غاية). Each example illustrates how the thing is defined by its relation to that cause. For instance, 'giving' (العطاء) is a concept that inherently includes reference to the giver (الفاعل). 'Flatness' (الفطوسة) refers to a concavity in the nose, which is considered in relation to the material that receives it (the nose). 'Flat-nosed' (الأفطس) is a form inhering in the nose. 'Ring' (الخاتم) is defined by its purpose of adornment. For the second type (consideration with an effect), examples like 'Creator' and 'Provider' are terms that refer to the agent in relation to the created or provided entity. This demonstrates the relational nature of these concepts.

Key Terms
فاعل (efficient cause)
قابل (receptive cause)
صورة (formal cause)
غاية (final cause)
عطاء (giving)
فطوسة (flatness)
أفطس (flat-nosed)
خاتم (ring)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Giving is just an action without reference to a giver.
Correction: The term 'giving' (العطاء) is defined in relation to the efficient cause (the giver); it inherently includes that relation.

Checkpoint
Which cause is exemplified by 'ring' (الخاتم)?
A) Efficient cause
B) Receptive cause
C) Formal cause
D) Final cause

Key Takeaway
Concepts like 'giving' and 'ring' receive their meaning through their relation to efficient, receptive, formal, or final causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own actions, do you consider the purpose (final cause) for which you do them, or do you act without reflecting on the goal? Take a moment to identify one action today and ask: what is the true purpose behind it?
?
This lesson shows that words like 'giving' gain meaning by relation to the giver. How often do you define your own identity by the causes that shape you—such as your Creator, your materials, your form, and your purpose? Which of these four is most neglected in your self-understanding?
?
If the ring's meaning lies in its intended adornment, then what does your existence point to as its final goal? Are you living in a way that fulfills that purpose, or have you reduced yourself to a mere object without a cause? Write one concrete change you will make today to align with your purpose.

Causality and Created Things

The Second Type: Considering a Thing with Its Effect
Enumeration of the second type (consideration with effect) with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second type of consideration, where a thing is considered in relation to its effect, with examples such as 'Creator' and 'Provider'.

Core Idea
Some terms like 'Creator' only make sense in relation to what is created.

Having seen how terms can be defined by their causes, we now turn to the second type: consideration in relation to the effect. The examples 'Creator' and 'Provider' highlight that these Divine attributes are relational—they inherently point to the creation and provision of creatures. This relational dimension is essential for understanding how we speak about Allah's actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second type
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the passage explicitly states the second type is 'what is considered in relation to the effect' (المعتبر بالنسبة إلى المعلول).
2
Identify the examples and their relation
The examples are 'Creator' (الخالق) and 'Provider' (الرازق). Explain how each term necessarily points to an effect (the created thing and the provided thing).
These terms are among the names of Allah that show His actions.
3
Compare with the first type
Consider the difference: the first type relates a thing to its cause, while the second type relates it to its effect. How does this distinction help in analyzing theological language?
This distinction is crucial for understanding attributes of action like 'Khaliq'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والأول ) ، وهو المعتبر بالقياس إلى العلة : ( إما ) معتبر ( مع الفاعل، نحو العطاء ) فإنه اسم لفائدة اعتبرت إضافتها إلى الفاعل ( أو ) مع القابل نحو الفطوسة، وهي الذي في الأنف اعتبر فيها الشيء بالإضافة إلى قابله ( أو ) مع (الصورة نحو الأفطس ) (أو) مع (الغاية نحو الخاتم؛ فإنه حلقة يتزين بها في الإصبع، وذلك التزين هو الغاية المقصودة من تلك الحلقة ( والثاني ) : وهو المعتبر بالنسبة إلى المعلول (نحو الخالق) والرازق وأمثالهما ، مما اعتبر فيه الشيء مقيساً إلى معلوله .
And the first is what is considered in relation to the cause: it is either considered with the efficient cause, like 'giving' (العطاء) – for it is a name for a benefit whose addition to the agent is considered – or with the receptive cause (قابل), like 'flatness' (الفطوسة), which is the concavity in the nose; the thing is considered in it by addition to its receiver, or with the formal cause (صورة), like 'flat-nosed' (الأفطس), or with the final cause (غاية), like 'ring' (الخاتم); for it is a ring that is adorned on the finger, and that adornment is the intended goal of that ring. And the second is what is considered in relation to the effect, like 'Creator' (الخالق) and 'Provider' (الرازق) and similar terms, in which the thing is considered measured to its effect.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples for the first two types of consideration. For the first type (consideration with a cause), the author lists the four Aristotelian causes: efficient (فاعل), material/receptive (قابل/مادة), formal (صورة), and final (غاية). Each example illustrates how the thing is defined by its relation to that cause. For instance, 'giving' (العطاء) is a concept that inherently includes reference to the giver (الفاعل). 'Flatness' (الفطوسة) refers to a concavity in the nose, which is considered in relation to the material that receives it (the nose). 'Flat-nosed' (الأفطس) is a form inhering in the nose. 'Ring' (الخاتم) is defined by its purpose of adornment. For the second type (consideration with an effect), examples like 'Creator' and 'Provider' are terms that refer to the agent in relation to the created or provided entity. This demonstrates the relational nature of these concepts.

Key Terms
معلول (effect)
خالق (Creator)
رازق (Provider)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the term 'Creator' (الخالق) is considered in relation to what?
A) Its cause
B) Its effect
C) Its form
D) Its purpose

Key Takeaway
Terms like 'Creator' and 'Provider' derive their meaning from their relation to the effect (the created or provided entity).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Al-Khāliq' (The Creator), do you truly sense that you are in a constant state of being created? How would your awareness change if you realized that His creative act is not just in the past but ongoing every moment?
?
If 'Provider' only has meaning because of what it provides, then your next breath, your next meal, your every need is a direct manifestation of that name. Are you living with that gratitude and dependence, or have you become numb to His provision?
?
This lesson shows that some of Allah's names only make sense when connected to His creation. Does that make Him dependent on creation? No. But it forces you to see that your existence is not accidental—it is the very reason those names are known. How does that truth demand a response from you today?

The Three Types of Parts in Causal Relations
The author enumerates the three types of parts and explains the first two with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of parts into three categories based on their relation to cause and effect, with detailed examples for the first two types.

Core Idea
Parts are classified by their relationship to a cause, an effect, or neither.

In this lesson, we explore the classification of parts (أجزاء) that the author presents as the fifth objective. The classification is based on whether the part is considered with respect to its cause, its effect, or something without causal relation. We focus on the first two types and the general definition of the third, leaving the subdivisions for the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the main classification
Read the Arabic passage that lists the three types of parts. Notice the structure: first type with cause, second with effect, third without cause or effect.
The first type includes form and end.
2
Analyze the first type's example
The example of the nose with concavity illustrates a part considered with its form. The ring's adornment illustrates a part considered with its final cause. Understand why these are 'parts' in the causal sense.
3
Analyze the second type's example
The Creator and Provider are examples of parts considered with respect to their effect. Here, the part is defined by its relationship to what it produces.
This category includes agents, matter, form, and ends as per commentary.
4
Review the commentary's clarification
The commentary explains that the first type achieves the actual flat-nose, and the second type involves the addition of agent to patient. It also generalizes the four causes.
Note the phrase 'المراد بالعلل الأربع أعم'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس: في تقسيم الأجزاء وهو الأنف الذي فيه تقعير وهو يجري مجرى الصورة من الأنف، (أو) مع (الغاية نحو الخاتم؛ فإنه حلقة يتزين بها في الإصبع، وذلك التزين هو الغاية المقصودة من تلك الحلقة ( والثاني ) : وهو المعتبر بالنسبة إلى المعلول (نحو الخالق) والرازق وأمثالهما ، مما اعتبر فيه الشيء مقيساً إلى معلوله . ( والثالث ) : وهو الذي اعتبر مع ما ليس علة ولا معلولاً، إما متشابهة) في الماهية (نحو أجزاء العشرة) الوحدات المتوافقة الحقيقة ( أو متخالفة في الماهية وهي (إما) متمايزة (عقلاً) لا حساً، ( كالجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة، فإن أجزاءه متخالفة متمايزة في العقل دون الحس، وكالعدالة المركبة من الحكمة والعفة والشجاعة أو خارجاً) أي حساً كأعضاء البدن، وعلى هذا ففي قوله : (نحو) الإنسان المركب من النفس والبدن ) نظر، فإن النفس الناطقة والبدن لا يتمايزان حسا، وإن أريد بالخارج ما يقابل الذهن، كانت الهيولى والصورة من الأجزاء الخارجية دون العقلية، (و) نحو الخلقة المركبة من اللون والشكل المتمايزين في الحس، فإن الهيئات الشكلية محسوسة تبعاً، ونحو البلقة المركبة من السواد والبياض المحسوسين بالذات . قوله : ( وهو يجري مجرى إلخ) في أنه يحصل به الأفطس بالفعل ومن هذا ظهر أن المراد بالعلل الأربع أعم من أن يكون حقيقة أو شبيهة بها . قوله : ( نحو الخالق إلخ) فإنه اعتبر فيه إضافة الفاعل إلى مفعوله . قوله : ( وأمثالهما إلخ) إشارة إلى أن ذلك الشيء أعم من أن يكون فاعلاً أو مادة أو صورة أو غاية . قوله : (إما متشابهة في الماهية أي متفقة في الماهية النوعية، والتمايز بينها بالتشخصات، فلا يكون التمايز بينها عقلا؛ إذ العقل لا يدرك الجزئيات، فلذا لم يقسمها إلى ما قسم إليه المتخالفة، فمعنى قوله : إما متشابهة أي أجزاؤه إما متشابهة . قوله : (إما متمايزة إلخ) لما لم يكن التخالف في الماهية مدركاً إلا بالعقل قدر متمايزة؛ ليصح التقسيم، ومعنى التمايز العقلي أن يحكم العقل بتغايرهما في الوجود، سواء كان بالضرورة أو بالبرهان . قوله : ( كالجسم المركب إلخ) أي كأجزاء الجسم، أو من حيث إنه مركب منهما . قوله : ( خارجاً أي حساً فسر إلخارج بالحس متابعة لما ذكره الإمام في المباحث المشرقية وغيره من قسمة الأجزاء إلى المعقولة والمحسوسة . قوله : ( فإن النفس الناطقة إلخ ) لأن التمايز الحسي يقتضي أن يكون كل منهما محسوساً نقل عنه ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنه يكفي في التمايز الحسي كون البدن محسوساً دون النفس الناطقة انتهى . قوله : ( وإن أريد إلخ ) أورده بطريق الاحتمال لما عرفت أن المذكور هو السابق . قوله : ( من الأجزاء الخارجية لتمايزها بالوجود في الخارج ولذا لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر. ( قوله دون العقلية) بالمعنى المراد هاهنا أعني التمايز في العقل فقط دون الخارج بقرينة . قوله : ( محسوسة تبعاً فلا ينافي ذلك كون الشكل من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات . ينبغي أن يكون المراد من تركب الشيء مع غير علله ومعلولاته أن يؤخذ هو من حيث عرضت له الإضافة إلى ذلك الغير، وليس الأمر كذلك في الإنسان والعشرة ونحوهما. قوله : ( من الحكمة والعفة والشجاعة قد سبق تفاسيرها في أواخر شرح الديباجة فلا نعيده . قوله: (فإن النفس الناطقة إلخ) نقل عن الشارح أنه يمكن أن يجاب : بأنه يكفي في التمايز الحسي كون البدن محسوساً دون النفس الناطقة، وقريب منه ما يقال في الجواب : يكفي في التمايز الحسي أن يحس أحدهما مع عدم الآخر، فالفرق ظاهر؛ لأن البدن بلا نفس قد يحس كما في الميت، وأما الهيولى والصورة فلا تحس إحداهما بدون الأخرى قطعاً. فإن قلت : ما ذكره الشارح إنما يرد إذا حمل النفس على الجوهر المجرد ، وأما إذا حمل على غيره فلا . قلت : إن بني التمثيل على مذهب الفلاسفة فقد عرفت حاله، وإن بني على مذهب المتكلمين فالنفس عندهم الهيكل المحسوس، فلا تمايز بينها وبين البدن أيضاً وقول النظام النفس هي السارية في البدن سريان ماء الورد في الورد لا يفيد التمايز الحسي أيضاً؛ لأن الورد مجموع الماء ومحله .
The second observation - the fifth objective: On the division of parts. And it is the nose which has concavity and it runs in the course of the form from the nose, or with the end (ghāya) like the ring; for it is a ring that is adorned with on the finger, and that adornment is the intended end from that ring. And the second: that which is considered in relation to the effect (maʿlūl) (like the Creator, the Provider and their likes), from that in which the thing is measured to its effect. And the third: that which is considered with what is neither cause nor effect, either similar in essence (māhiyya) like the parts of ten, the units agreeing in reality, or different in essence, and they are either distinguished intellectually not sensibly, like the body composed of matter and form, for its parts are different and distinguished in the intellect without sensation, and like justice composed of wisdom, chastity, and bravery; or externally, i.e. sensibly, like the limbs of the body. On this, in his saying: 'like man composed of soul and body' there is consideration, for the rational soul and the body are not distinguished sensibly, and if external is meant what is opposite the mind, then matter and form are among external parts not intellectual. And like the complexion composed of color and shape distinguished in sensation, for the shapes are perceived secondarily, and like the piebald composed of black and white perceived essentially. His saying (wa huwa yajrī majrā etc.): in that the flat-nosed is produced actually. From this it appears that the intended by the four causes is more general than being real or similar to them. His saying (naḥwa al-khāliq etc.): for he considered in it the addition of the agent to its patient. His saying (wa amthālihimā etc.): an indication that that thing is more general than being an agent, matter, form, or end. His saying (immā mutashābiha fī al-māhiyya): i.e. agreeing in specific essence, and the distinction between them is by individuations, so there is no intellectual distinction between them, since the intellect does not perceive particulars. Therefore he did not divide them into what he divided the different. So the meaning of his saying immā mutashābiha is that its parts are similar. His saying (immā mutamāyiza etc.): since the difference in essence is only perceived by the intellect, he said 'distinguished' to make the division valid, and the meaning of intellectual distinction is that the intellect judges them to be different in existence, whether by necessity or proof. His saying (ka al-jism al-murakkab etc.): i.e. like the parts of the body, or insofar as it is composed of them. His saying (khārijan ay ḥissan): he interpreted 'external' by sensation following what the Imām mentioned in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya and others of the division of parts into intellectual and sensible. His saying (fa-inna al-nafs al-nāṭiqa etc.): because sensible distinction requires that each of them be sensible; it is transmitted from him and it can be answered that it suffices in sensible distinction that the body be sensible without the rational soul. End. His saying (wa in urīda etc.): he brought it by way of possibility, for you have known that what is mentioned is the former. His saying (mina al-ajzāʾ al-khārijiyya): due to their distinction by existence in the external, and therefore one is not predicated of the other. His saying (dūna al-ʿaqliyya): in the meaning intended here, i.e. distinction in the intellect only without the external, by the evidence. His saying (maḥsūsa tabʿan): that does not contradict that shape is among the qualities specific to quantities. It should be that the intended by the composition of the thing with other than its causes and effects is that it is taken insofar as it has an addition to that other, and the matter is not so in man and ten and the like. His saying (mina al-ḥikma wa al-ʿiffa wa al-shajāʿa): their explanations have preceded in the end of the commentary on the Introduction, so we do not repeat it. His saying (fa-inna al-nafs al-nāṭiqa etc.): transmitted from the commentator that it can be answered: that it suffices in sensible distinction that the body be sensible without the rational soul, and similar to it is what is said in the answer: it suffices in sensible distinction that one of them is sensed with the absence of the other, so the difference is clear, because the body without soul may be sensed as in the dead, but matter and form are not sensed one without the other definitely. If you say: what the commentator mentioned applies only if soul is taken as the immaterial substance, but if taken as other, then no. I say: if the example is based on the philosophers' school, you know its state, and if based on the theologians' school, then soul for them is the sensible structure, so there is no distinction between it and the body either, and the statement of al-Naẓẓām that the soul permeates the body like the permeation of rosewater in the rose does not produce sensible distinction either, because the rose is the sum of water and its place.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the division of parts (اجزاء) in the context of causal relations. The author begins by stating the fifth objective: on the division of parts. He then presents the first type of parts: those that are considered with respect to the form (or end), giving the example of the nose with concavity as analogous to form, and the ring's adornment as the final cause. The second type is that which is considered in relation to the effect (معلول), exemplified by 'Creator' and 'Provider' where the thing is measured to its effect. The third type is that which is considered with neither cause nor effect, subdivided into parts that are similar in essence (like the units of ten) and those different in essence. For the latter, they are further divided into those distinguished intellectually (like body from matter and form) and those distinguished sensibly (like limbs of the body). The author then discusses the example of 'man composed of soul and body', noting the issue that rational soul and body are not sensibly distinct unless interpreted differently. The commentary (قوله sections) explains each part: the first type achieves the flat-nose actually; the second type involves the addition of agent to patient; the third type's subdivisions are clarified regarding similarity and difference in essence, intellectual vs. sensible distinction, and the conditions for these distinctions. The commentary also addresses objections, such as whether bodily sensation alone suffices for sensible distinction, and discusses the different schools' views on the soul.

Key Terms
أجزاء
علة
معلول
غاية
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the three types are mutually exclusive and exhaustive only in a physical sense.
Correction: In fact, they are based on the relational context, and the third type includes many subdivisions, not just one.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a part considered in relation to its effect (معلول)?
A) The nose with concavity
B) The ring's adornment
C) The Creator (الخالق)
D) The units of ten

Key Takeaway
The three types of parts are distinguished by whether they are considered with a cause, an effect, or neither.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions, do you tend to focus on what caused them (your intentions) or what they effect (their outcomes)? Which of the three categories would you say your self-reflection most often neglects, and how might that imbalance distort your self-understanding?
?
The passage uses 'Creator' as an example of a part considered with its effect. In your daily life, do you experience your relationship with Allah as a 'part' that has effects on your character and decisions? If not, what would change if you consciously viewed Him as the cause that shapes every part of your existence?
?
The third type of parts are those considered with neither cause nor effect—like agent, matter, form, and end. Consider something in your life you treat as an end in itself. Is it truly without cause or effect? How does this category challenge you to examine whether you have mistaken a means for an end?

Subdivisions of the Third Type: Parts Without Causal Relation
The author details the subdivision of the third type of parts and explains the criteria of similarity/difference and intellectual/sensible distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subdivisions of parts that are neither cause nor effect, including similar vs. different in essence, and intellectually vs. sensibly distinct.

Core Idea
Parts without causal relation are further divided by similarity or difference in essence, and by mode of distinction.

This lesson focuses on the third type of parts: those considered with neither cause nor effect. The author subdivides this type into parts that are similar in essence (like units of ten) and those that are different in essence. The different ones are further divided into intellectually distinct (like body from matter and form) and sensibly distinct (like limbs). The commentary clarifies these distinctions and addresses potential objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the third type's subdivisions
Read the Arabic passage that describes the two main subdivisions: similar in essence and different in essence. Then the different are further divided into intellectual and sensible distinction.
Note the examples: units of ten, body from matter and form, justice from virtues, limbs, color and shape, etc.
2
Understand 'similar in essence'
Parts that share the same specific essence but are distinguished by individuation, not intellect. Example: the units of ten are all units.
The commentary says intellect does not perceive particulars.
3
Understand 'different in essence' and its two types
Different in essence means the parts have distinct essences. They are either intellectually distinct (only the intellect can tell them apart, like matter and form) or sensibly distinct (senses can tell them apart, like limbs or colors).
The commentary discusses the example of man composed of soul and body, noting that soul and body are not sensibly distinct unless interpreted.
4
Review the commentary's clarifications
The commentary explains that intellectual distinction means the intellect judges them as different in existence, either by necessity or proof. Sensible distinction requires direct sensory perception.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس: في تقسيم الأجزاء وهو الأنف الذي فيه تقعير وهو يجري مجرى الصورة من الأنف، (أو) مع (الغاية نحو الخاتم؛ فإنه حلقة يتزين بها في الإصبع، وذلك التزين هو الغاية المقصودة من تلك الحلقة ( والثاني ) : وهو المعتبر بالنسبة إلى المعلول (نحو الخالق) والرازق وأمثالهما ، مما اعتبر فيه الشيء مقيساً إلى معلوله . ( والثالث ) : وهو الذي اعتبر مع ما ليس علة ولا معلولاً، إما متشابهة) في الماهية (نحو أجزاء العشرة) الوحدات المتوافقة الحقيقة ( أو متخالفة في الماهية وهي (إما) متمايزة (عقلاً) لا حساً، ( كالجسم المركب من الهيولى والصورة، فإن أجزاءه متخالفة متمايزة في العقل دون الحس، وكالعدالة المركبة من الحكمة والعفة والشجاعة أو خارجاً) أي حساً كأعضاء البدن، وعلى هذا ففي قوله : (نحو) الإنسان المركب من النفس والبدن ) نظر، فإن النفس الناطقة والبدن لا يتمايزان حسا، وإن أريد بالخارج ما يقابل الذهن، كانت الهيولى والصورة من الأجزاء الخارجية دون العقلية، (و) نحو الخلقة المركبة من اللون والشكل المتمايزين في الحس، فإن الهيئات الشكلية محسوسة تبعاً، ونحو البلقة المركبة من السواد والبياض المحسوسين بالذات . قوله : ( وهو يجري مجرى إلخ) في أنه يحصل به الأفطس بالفعل ومن هذا ظهر أن المراد بالعلل الأربع أعم من أن يكون حقيقة أو شبيهة بها . قوله : ( نحو الخالق إلخ) فإنه اعتبر فيه إضافة الفاعل إلى مفعوله . قوله : ( وأمثالهما إلخ) إشارة إلى أن ذلك الشيء أعم من أن يكون فاعلاً أو مادة أو صورة أو غاية . قوله : (إما متشابهة في الماهية أي متفقة في الماهية النوعية، والتمايز بينها بالتشخصات، فلا يكون التمايز بينها عقلا؛ إذ العقل لا يدرك الجزئيات، فلذا لم يقسمها إلى ما قسم إليه المتخالفة، فمعنى قوله : إما متشابهة أي أجزاؤه إما متشابهة . قوله : (إما متمايزة إلخ) لما لم يكن التخالف في الماهية مدركاً إلا بالعقل قدر متمايزة؛ ليصح التقسيم، ومعنى التمايز العقلي أن يحكم العقل بتغايرهما في الوجود، سواء كان بالضرورة أو بالبرهان . قوله : ( كالجسم المركب إلخ) أي كأجزاء الجسم، أو من حيث إنه مركب منهما . قوله : ( خارجاً أي حساً فسر إلخارج بالحس متابعة لما ذكره الإمام في المباحث المشرقية وغيره من قسمة الأجزاء إلى المعقولة والمحسوسة . قوله : ( فإن النفس الناطقة إلخ ) لأن التمايز الحسي يقتضي أن يكون كل منهما محسوساً نقل عنه ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنه يكفي في التمايز الحسي كون البدن محسوساً دون النفس الناطقة انتهى . قوله : ( وإن أريد إلخ ) أورده بطريق الاحتمال لما عرفت أن المذكور هو السابق . قوله : ( من الأجزاء الخارجية لتمايزها بالوجود في الخارج ولذا لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر. ( قوله دون العقلية) بالمعنى المراد هاهنا أعني التمايز في العقل فقط دون الخارج بقرينة . قوله : ( محسوسة تبعاً فلا ينافي ذلك كون الشكل من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات . ينبغي أن يكون المراد من تركب الشيء مع غير علله ومعلولاته أن يؤخذ هو من حيث عرضت له الإضافة إلى ذلك الغير، وليس الأمر كذلك في الإنسان والعشرة ونحوهما. قوله : ( من الحكمة والعفة والشجاعة قد سبق تفاسيرها في أواخر شرح الديباجة فلا نعيده . قوله: (فإن النفس الناطقة إلخ) نقل عن الشارح أنه يمكن أن يجاب : بأنه يكفي في التمايز الحسي كون البدن محسوساً دون النفس الناطقة، وقريب منه ما يقال في الجواب : يكفي في التمايز الحسي أن يحس أحدهما مع عدم الآخر، فالفرق ظاهر؛ لأن البدن بلا نفس قد يحس كما في الميت، وأما الهيولى والصورة فلا تحس إحداهما بدون الأخرى قطعاً. فإن قلت : ما ذكره الشارح إنما يرد إذا حمل النفس على الجوهر المجرد ، وأما إذا حمل على غيره فلا . قلت : إن بني التمثيل على مذهب الفلاسفة فقد عرفت حاله، وإن بني على مذهب المتكلمين فالنفس عندهم الهيكل المحسوس، فلا تمايز بينها وبين البدن أيضاً وقول النظام النفس هي السارية في البدن سريان ماء الورد في الورد لا يفيد التمايز الحسي أيضاً؛ لأن الورد مجموع الماء ومحله .
The second observation - the fifth objective: On the division of parts. And it is the nose which has concavity and it runs in the course of the form from the nose, or with the end (ghāya) like the ring; for it is a ring that is adorned with on the finger, and that adornment is the intended end from that ring. And the second: that which is considered in relation to the effect (maʿlūl) (like the Creator, the Provider and their likes), from that in which the thing is measured to its effect. And the third: that which is considered with what is neither cause nor effect, either similar in essence (māhiyya) like the parts of ten, the units agreeing in reality, or different in essence, and they are either distinguished intellectually not sensibly, like the body composed of matter and form, for its parts are different and distinguished in the intellect without sensation, and like justice composed of wisdom, chastity, and bravery; or externally, i.e. sensibly, like the limbs of the body. On this, in his saying: 'like man composed of soul and body' there is consideration, for the rational soul and the body are not distinguished sensibly, and if external is meant what is opposite the mind, then matter and form are among external parts not intellectual. And like the complexion composed of color and shape distinguished in sensation, for the shapes are perceived secondarily, and like the piebald composed of black and white perceived essentially. His saying (wa huwa yajrī majrā etc.): in that the flat-nosed is produced actually. From this it appears that the intended by the four causes is more general than being real or similar to them. His saying (naḥwa al-khāliq etc.): for he considered in it the addition of the agent to its patient. His saying (wa amthālihimā etc.): an indication that that thing is more general than being an agent, matter, form, or end. His saying (immā mutashābiha fī al-māhiyya): i.e. agreeing in specific essence, and the distinction between them is by individuations, so there is no intellectual distinction between them, since the intellect does not perceive particulars. Therefore he did not divide them into what he divided the different. So the meaning of his saying immā mutashābiha is that its parts are similar. His saying (immā mutamāyiza etc.): since the difference in essence is only perceived by the intellect, he said 'distinguished' to make the division valid, and the meaning of intellectual distinction is that the intellect judges them to be different in existence, whether by necessity or proof. His saying (ka al-jism al-murakkab etc.): i.e. like the parts of the body, or insofar as it is composed of them. His saying (khārijan ay ḥissan): he interpreted 'external' by sensation following what the Imām mentioned in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya and others of the division of parts into intellectual and sensible. His saying (fa-inna al-nafs al-nāṭiqa etc.): because sensible distinction requires that each of them be sensible; it is transmitted from him and it can be answered that it suffices in sensible distinction that the body be sensible without the rational soul. End. His saying (wa in urīda etc.): he brought it by way of possibility, for you have known that what is mentioned is the former. His saying (mina al-ajzāʾ al-khārijiyya): due to their distinction by existence in the external, and therefore one is not predicated of the other. His saying (dūna al-ʿaqliyya): in the meaning intended here, i.e. distinction in the intellect only without the external, by the evidence. His saying (maḥsūsa tabʿan): that does not contradict that shape is among the qualities specific to quantities. It should be that the intended by the composition of the thing with other than its causes and effects is that it is taken insofar as it has an addition to that other, and the matter is not so in man and ten and the like. His saying (mina al-ḥikma wa al-ʿiffa wa al-shajāʿa): their explanations have preceded in the end of the commentary on the Introduction, so we do not repeat it. His saying (fa-inna al-nafs al-nāṭiqa etc.): transmitted from the commentator that it can be answered: that it suffices in sensible distinction that the body be sensible without the rational soul, and similar to it is what is said in the answer: it suffices in sensible distinction that one of them is sensed with the absence of the other, so the difference is clear, because the body without soul may be sensed as in the dead, but matter and form are not sensed one without the other definitely. If you say: what the commentator mentioned applies only if soul is taken as the immaterial substance, but if taken as other, then no. I say: if the example is based on the philosophers' school, you know its state, and if based on the theologians' school, then soul for them is the sensible structure, so there is no distinction between it and the body either, and the statement of al-Naẓẓām that the soul permeates the body like the permeation of rosewater in the rose does not produce sensible distinction either, because the rose is the sum of water and its place.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the division of parts (اجزاء) in the context of causal relations. The author begins by stating the fifth objective: on the division of parts. He then presents the first type of parts: those that are considered with respect to the form (or end), giving the example of the nose with concavity as analogous to form, and the ring's adornment as the final cause. The second type is that which is considered in relation to the effect (معلول), exemplified by 'Creator' and 'Provider' where the thing is measured to its effect. The third type is that which is considered with neither cause nor effect, subdivided into parts that are similar in essence (like the units of ten) and those different in essence. For the latter, they are further divided into those distinguished intellectually (like body from matter and form) and those distinguished sensibly (like limbs of the body). The author then discusses the example of 'man composed of soul and body', noting the issue that rational soul and body are not sensibly distinct unless interpreted differently. The commentary (قوله sections) explains each part: the first type achieves the flat-nose actually; the second type involves the addition of agent to patient; the third type's subdivisions are clarified regarding similarity and difference in essence, intellectual vs. sensible distinction, and the conditions for these distinctions. The commentary also addresses objections, such as whether bodily sensation alone suffices for sensible distinction, and discusses the different schools' views on the soul.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that parts that are similar in essence have no distinction at all.
Correction: They are distinct by individuation (تشخصات), but the intellect does not perceive this distinction; they are only sensibly distinct as individuals.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is an example of parts that are distinguished intellectually (عقلاً)?
A) The units of ten
B) The limbs of the body
C) Body composed of matter and form
D) Black and white

Key Takeaway
The third type of parts divides into similar in essence (distinguished by individuation) and different in essence (distinguished intellectually or sensibly).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the world, do you often mistake superficial differences for essential ones? This lesson teaches that some things differ only by individuation while others differ in essence. Reflect on a belief you hold where you might have conflated a non-essential difference with a fundamental one. What can you do today to rectify your understanding?
?
The passage distinguishes between what the intellect discerns and what the senses discern. Have you ever trusted your senses over your intellect in matters of faith? In what area of your life are you most prone to rely on sensory evidence rather than rational conviction? Commit to one specific practice this week that engages your intellect in worshipping God.
?
Consider the example of the soul and body: they are distinct yet united. How do you perceive your own existence—are you more identified with your body or your soul? This lesson challenges you to see the distinction correctly. What changes can you make in your daily habits to reflect the truth that your soul is the essence of your being?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
5 min
The Second Madhhab: Essences Are Made
Al-Jurjani presents the proof for the second madhhab that essences are made.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that all essences are made by the Maker, and why denying this leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
If no essence were made, even existence would be independent of the Maker.

Al-Jurjani now presents the second major school of thought on whether essences are made. He provides a concise reductio argument: if no essence whatsoever were made, then the essence of any possible thing, its existence, and its being qualified by existence would all be unmade, implying that the possible is independent of the Agent. Since this conclusion is unacceptable to any rational person, the premise must be false. This passage establishes the core reasoning behind the view that essences are absolutely made.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the logical structure of the reductio.
2
Identify the reductio structure
Notice the conditional: if no essence is made, then the possible would be independent. This leads to an absurdity.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Jurjani states that no rational person would accept the independence of the possible from the agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب ( الثاني : أنها مجعولة مطلقاً) أي في الجملة؛ إذ لو لم تكن الماهية ) أي شيء من الماهيات ( مجعولة ) أصلاً (ارتفع المجعولية مطلقاً) أي بالكلية؛ لأن ما فرض كونه مجعولاً من وجود أو موصوفية الماهية (به أي بالوجود، (فهو أيضاً ماهية) في نفسه والمقدر أن لا شيء من الماهيات بمجعولة، فلا تكون حينئذ ماهية الممكن ولا وجودها ولا اتصافها بالوجود مجعولة بجعل الجاعل فيلزم استغناء الممكن عن المؤثر، وذلك مما لا يقول به عاقل. هذا ما يقتضيه تقرير الكتاب قوله : ( هو الإيجاب المعدول، فإنه يقتضي وجود الموضوع، فيلزم انتفاء الشيء حال ثبوته . قوله : ( أي شيء من الماهيات على أن اللام في الماهية للجنس. قوله هذا ما يقتضيه إلخ) أي كون مطلقا بمعنى في الجملة مخالفته لقوله : مطلقاً مع السابق، وجعل المدعى موجبة جزئية ما يقتضيه تقرير الكتاب للدليل؛ لأن ارتفاع المجعولية بالكلية إنما يلزم أن لو لم يكن شيء من الجزئيات مجعولة، وهو سالبة كلية، فكذبها يكون مستلزماً لصدق الموجبة الجزئية والمشهور الموافق لما حرره المصنف أن أحد المذاهب الموجبة الكلية، فإن روعي موافقة الدليل يلزم مخالفة المشهور، وإن روعي موافقة المشهور يلزم مخالفة التقرير فإحدى المخالفتين ،لازمة فلا يرد كان الأولى أن يحمل الشارح قدس سره قوله : مطلقاً على العموم ويجعل المدعى الموجبة الكلية كما هو المشهور، ويعترض على الدليل بمنع الملازمة أقول : ويمكن تقرير الكتاب بحيث يثبت الموجبة الكلية بأن يقال : الماهيات كلها مجعولة لأنه كلما كانت الماهية من حيث الصدق مجعولة كانت الماهيات كلها مجعولة، لكن المقدم حق فالتالي ،مثله أما الملازمة فظاهرة؛ لعدم اختصاص صدقها بفرد دون فرد وأما حقية المقدم ؛ فلأنه لو لم تكن الماهية من حيث الصدق مجعولة ارتفع المجعولية؛ لأن كل ما فرض أنه مجعول يصدق عليه أنه ،ماهية فتكون الماهية من حيث الصدق مجعولة، وفيه تأمل، وفي إفراد لفظ الماهية إشارة إلى ما ذكرنا وقيل في تقريره : إن الماهيات كلها مجعولة لأن ماهية ما مجعولة وإلا ارتفع المجعولية بالكلية، وإذا كانت ماهية ما مجعولة كانت الماهيات كلها مجعولة؛ لاستوائها في الإمكان الذي هو علة المجعولية. ولا يخفى ما فيه، أما أولاً فلان قوله : ( فهو أيضاً ماهية في نفسه إلخ) فيه بحث؛ لأن الوجود والموصوفية من المعقولات الثانية الممتنعة الوجود في الخارج، والكلام في الممكنات الوجود فيه، فشيء منهما لا يندرج فيما قدر عدم مجعوليته، ثم إن تعلق الجعل بالممتنع لا بالإيجاد غير ممتنع فتأمل. قوله : (هذا ما يقتضيه تقرير الكتاب إلخ) قيل : الظاهر أن مراد المصنف أن الماهية كلها مجعولة كما ذكره في تحرير المسألة، إذ لا نزاع في أن للواجب تعالى جعلاً وتأثيراً في الممكن
The second school (of thought: that they are made absolutely), i.e., in some sense. For if the essence (i.e., something of the essences) were not made at all, then the made-ness would be removed absolutely, i.e., entirely. Because whatever is assumed to be made, whether existence or the attribution of the essence to it, i.e., to existence, it is also an essence in itself. And it is assumed that no essence is made. Then the essence of the possible, its existence, and its being qualified by existence would not be made by the maker's act. Thus the independence of the possible from the agent would follow, and that is something no rational person affirms. This is what the exposition of the book requires. His saying: 'It is the modified positive judgment, for it requires the existence of the subject, so the negation of the thing while it is established follows.' His saying: 'i.e., something of the essences' such that the lam in al-māhiyya is for the genus. His saying: 'This is what the exposition requires, etc.' – i.e., the meaning of 'absolutely' (muṭlaqan) as 'in some sense' contradicts his earlier use of 'absolutely', and making the claim a particular affirmative is what the exposition of the book for the argument requires. For the removal of made-ness entirely would follow only if no particular thing were made, which is a universal negative. Its falsity entails the truth of the particular affirmative. But the well-known view, in agreement with what the author (al-Ījī) wrote, is that one of the schools is the universal affirmative. So if conformity to the argument is observed, contradiction of the well-known view follows; and if conformity to the well-known view is observed, contradiction of the exposition follows. So one of the two contradictions is necessary. So it is not valid. It would have been better for the commentator (al-Jurjani), may his secret be sanctified, to interpret his saying 'absolutely' as universal and make the claim the universal affirmative, as is well-known, and object to the argument by denying the entailment. I say: It is possible to present the book such that the universal affirmative is established by saying: All essences are made because whenever the essence in terms of truth (min ḥaythu al-ṣidq) is made, then all essences are made. But the premise is true, so the consequent is likewise. As for the entailment, it is obvious because its truth is not restricted to one individual without another. As for the truth of the premise, because if the essence in terms of truth were not made, made-ness would be removed entirely; because whatever is assumed to be made, it is true of it that it is an essence, so the essence in terms of truth is made. And there is reflection (taʾammul) in it. And the singular expression of the word 'essence' indicates what we mentioned. And it was said in its exposition: All essences are made because some essence is made; otherwise made-ness would be removed entirely. And if some essence is made, then all essences are made because they are equal in possibility (imkān) which is the cause of made-ness. And it is not hidden what is in it. Firstly, because his saying: 'It is also an essence in itself, etc.' has discussion; for existence and the attribute of being qualified are among the secondary intelligibles that are impossible of existence in external reality, and the discussion concerns possible existents in external reality, so neither of them falls under what is assumed not to be made. Moreover, the attachment of making (jaʿl) to the impossible, not to bringing into existence, is not impossible. So consider. His saying: 'This is what the exposition of the book requires, etc.' – it is said: It is apparent that the author intended that all essences are made, as he mentioned in the formulation of the problem, for there is no dispute that the Necessary Existent (al-wājib taʿālā) has making and influence upon the possible.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the second madhhab in the debate: that essences (māhiyyāt) are made (majʿūla) absolutely, i.e., in some sense. Al-Jurjani gives a proof: if no essence at all were made, then the made-ness of everything that could be considered made—such as the existence of a possible or its being qualified by existence—would also be removed, because those things are themselves essences. This would imply that the possible is independent of the agent (muʾaththir), which is absurd. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: denying that any essence is made leads to the conclusion that nothing about the possible is made, thus denying the need for a maker. The commentary then discusses a nuance: the phrase 'absolutely' (muṭlaqan) in the exposition might be interpreted as 'in some sense' (fī al-jumla), which would make the claim a particular affirmative, but the well-known position is that the claim is a universal affirmative. Al-Jurjani explains that either interpretation leads to a contradiction—either with the argument or with the well-known view. He suggests that it would have been better to interpret 'absolutely' universally and deny the entailment in the proof. He then offers an alternative formulation to establish the universal affirmative: if the essence as such (min ḥaythu al-ṣidq) is made, then all essences are made; and that essence as such is indeed made because otherwise made-ness would be entirely removed. However, he notes that there is reflection (taʾammul) in this reasoning. Another argument is presented: some essence is made, otherwise made-ness would be entirely removed; and if some essence is made, then all essences are made because they share the same possibility (imkān) which is the cause of made-ness. This too is criticized: existence and qualification by existence are secondary intelligibles that do not exist in external reality, so they are not included in the scope of the assumption. Al-Jurjani concludes by noting that the author's intent was likely the universal affirmative, as there is no dispute that God has making and influence upon the possible.

Key Terms
مجعولة
ماهية
ممكن
مؤثر

Checkpoint

A) The possible would be self-sufficient and independent of the agent.
B) The maker would be unnecessary only for essences.
C) The possible would cease to exist.
D) Only existence would be made.

Key Takeaway
The second madhhab argues that essences must be made because otherwise the possible would be independent of the Maker, which is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever live as if anything about you—your personality, your strengths, your very existence—is self-caused or independent of Allah? What would it mean to fully acknowledge that even your essence is made by Him?
?
If the possible is completely dependent on the Maker for its essence and existence, why do you still sometimes act as if you have autonomous control over your own life? Where does that illusion come from, and how does this lesson expose it?

What would change today if you truly internalized that you are entirely made—from your very essence to your every attribute?

Universal vs. Particular: Nuances in the Proof
Al-Jurjani discusses the logical implications of the term 'absolutely' and proposes alternative proofs for the universal claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical tension between the proof and the well-known universal affirmative interpretation of the claim.

Core Idea
The proof only works for a particular claim, but the well-known position is universal.

After presenting the proof, the commentary delves into a subtle logical issue: does the claim 'essences are made absolutely' mean universally or particularly? The proof as stated seems to support only a particular affirmative, but the well-known position is the universal affirmative. Al-Jurjani explores this tension and offers alternative formulations to establish the universal claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical tension
The term 'absolutely' could mean either 'in some sense' (particular) or 'universally'. The proof seems to require the particular interpretation, but the well-known position is universal.
2
Examine the alternative proof
Al-Jurjani offers a different argument to establish the universal claim, using the notion of 'essence in terms of truth'.
3
Consider the criticisms
He notes that there is reflection (ta'ammul) in the alternative proof, and that existence and qualification are secondary intelligibles.

The second school (of thought: that they are made absolutely), i.e., in some sense. For if the essence (i.e., something of the essences) were not made at all, then the made-ness would be removed absolutely, i.e., entirely. Because whatever is assumed to be made, whether existence or the attribution of the essence to it, i.e., to existence, it is also an essence in itself. And it is assumed that no essence is made. Then the essence of the possible, its existence, and its being qualified by existence would not be made by the maker's act. Thus the independence of the possible from the agent would follow, and that is something no rational person affirms. This is what the exposition of the book requires. His saying: 'It is the modified positive judgment, for it requires the existence of the subject, so the negation of the thing while it is established follows.' His saying: 'i.e., something of the essences' such that the lam in al-māhiyya is for the genus. His saying: 'This is what the exposition requires, etc.' – i.e., the meaning of 'absolutely' (muṭlaqan) as 'in some sense' contradicts his earlier use of 'absolutely', and making the claim a particular affirmative is what the exposition of the book for the argument requires. For the removal of made-ness entirely would follow only if no particular thing were made, which is a universal negative. Its falsity entails the truth of the particular affirmative. But the well-known view, in agreement with what the author (al-Ījī) wrote, is that one of the schools is the universal affirmative. So if conformity to the argument is observed, contradiction of the well-known view follows; and if conformity to the well-known view is observed, contradiction of the exposition follows. So one of the two contradictions is necessary. So it is not valid. It would have been better for the commentator (al-Jurjani), may his secret be sanctified, to interpret his saying 'absolutely' as universal and make the claim the universal affirmative, as is well-known, and object to the argument by denying the entailment. I say: It is possible to present the book such that the universal affirmative is established by saying: All essences are made because whenever the essence in terms of truth (min ḥaythu al-ṣidq) is made, then all essences are made. But the premise is true, so the consequent is likewise. As for the entailment, it is obvious because its truth is not restricted to one individual without another. As for the truth of the premise, because if the essence in terms of truth were not made, made-ness would be removed entirely; because whatever is assumed to be made, it is true of it that it is an essence, so the essence in terms of truth is made. And there is reflection (taʾammul) in it. And the singular expression of the word 'essence' indicates what we mentioned. And it was said in its exposition: All essences are made because some essence is made; otherwise made-ness would be removed entirely. And if some essence is made, then all essences are made because they are equal in possibility (imkān) which is the cause of made-ness. And it is not hidden what is in it. Firstly, because his saying: 'It is also an essence in itself, etc.' has discussion; for existence and the attribute of being qualified are among the secondary intelligibles that are impossible of existence in external reality, and the discussion concerns possible existents in external reality, so neither of them falls under what is assumed not to be made. Moreover, the attachment of making (jaʿl) to the impossible, not to bringing into existence, is not impossible. So consider. His saying: 'This is what the exposition of the book requires, etc.' – it is said: It is apparent that the author intended that all essences are made, as he mentioned in the formulation of the problem, for there is no dispute that the Necessary Existent (al-wājib taʿālā) has making and influence upon the possible.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the second madhhab in the debate: that essences (māhiyyāt) are made (majʿūla) absolutely, i.e., in some sense. Al-Jurjani gives a proof: if no essence at all were made, then the made-ness of everything that could be considered made—such as the existence of a possible or its being qualified by existence—would also be removed, because those things are themselves essences. This would imply that the possible is independent of the agent (muʾaththir), which is absurd. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: denying that any essence is made leads to the conclusion that nothing about the possible is made, thus denying the need for a maker. The commentary then discusses a nuance: the phrase 'absolutely' (muṭlaqan) in the exposition might be interpreted as 'in some sense' (fī al-jumla), which would make the claim a particular affirmative, but the well-known position is that the claim is a universal affirmative. Al-Jurjani explains that either interpretation leads to a contradiction—either with the argument or with the well-known view. He suggests that it would have been better to interpret 'absolutely' universally and deny the entailment in the proof. He then offers an alternative formulation to establish the universal affirmative: if the essence as such (min ḥaythu al-ṣidq) is made, then all essences are made; and that essence as such is indeed made because otherwise made-ness would be entirely removed. However, he notes that there is reflection (taʾammul) in this reasoning. Another argument is presented: some essence is made, otherwise made-ness would be entirely removed; and if some essence is made, then all essences are made because they share the same possibility (imkān) which is the cause of made-ness. This too is criticized: existence and qualification by existence are secondary intelligibles that do not exist in external reality, so they are not included in the scope of the assumption. Al-Jurjani concludes by noting that the author's intent was likely the universal affirmative, as there is no dispute that God has making and influence upon the possible.

إمكان
إيجاب معدول
سالبة كلية
موجبة جزئية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'absolutely' always means universally, but the argument shows it can mean 'in some sense'.
Correction: The term 'absolutely' is ambiguous and must be interpreted in context; here the proof requires the particular meaning.

Checkpoint

B) The proof requires a particular claim but the position is universal.

D) There is no tension.

Key Takeaway
The claim that essences are made can be defended as a universal affirmative through careful logical formulation, despite the proof initially suggesting a particular interpretation.

The Objection to Simple Quiddities
Objection to creation of simple quiddities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if simple quiddities cannot be created, then no quiddity can be created, leading to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If simples aren't created, nothing is created — an infinite regress arises.

The commentator introduces an objection against the claim that simple quiddities are not created. The objector argues that every quiddity is either simple or composite. If simples are not created, then composites, being composed of simples, also cannot be truly created. This leads to the conclusion that nothing is created, which is absurd. The objection is presented as a counterargument (mu'arada) rather than a direct refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once. It presents the objection clearly: every quiddity is either simple or composite, and if simples are not created, then nothing is created.
Notice the phrase 'fall' of creation.
2
Identify the dilemma
Identify the two options: either the quiddity is simple (and not created) or composite (and the discussion recurs).
3
Understand the regress
If simples are not created, composites cannot be created either, because they are made of simples. This leads to an infinite regress or the conclusion that nothing is created.
This is the heart of the objection.
4
Recognize the type of argument
The commentator states that this objection is a counterargument (mu'arada), not a refutation. Note the difference.
We will explore this distinction in a later lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول : (ذلك الذي ذكرتموه من الانضمام أو الوجود ) أيضاً له ماهية، فهي إما بسيطة فلا تكون مجعولة على ذلك التقدير ( أو مركبة فيعود الكلام فيه، وفي أجزائه البسيطة حتى يظهر ارتفاع المجعولية مطلقاً، والاعتراض المذكور معارضة، (والحل) هو : أن البسيط له ماهية ،ووجود فلعل الإمكان يعرض (للماهية ) البسيطة ( بالنسبة إلى الوجود)، فالإمكان يقتضي شيئين لا جزءين حتى يستحيل عروضه للبسيط. ( ) واعلم أن هذه المسألة من المداحض التي تزلق فيها أقدام الأذهان، ( وإنا نريد أن نثبت أقدامك في هذه المسألة ( بإشارة خفية إلى تحرير محل النزاع ومنشأ المذاهب والحق لا يحتجب عن طالبه بعد ذلك ( التحرير ( فنقول : الحكماء لما

قوله : ( والحل أن البسيط (إلخ لا يخفى أن اللازم منه أن يكون البسيط مجعولاً باعتبار الوجود، ولا نزاع فيه .

قوله : ( بإشارة خفية إلخ وهو ما أشار إليه بقوله : إلا ما ينسب إلى المعتزلة، فإنه إشارة إلى تحرير معنى يمكن النزاع فيه، وأما ما قبله فهو بيان لمنشأ المذاهب الثلاثة وأنها كلها حقة قوله : ( لما قسموا الوجود إلخ) وأما النافون للوجود الذهني فيقولون : إن كل ما يعرض للشيء فإنما يعرض له في الخارج ونفس الأمر، والمعدوم مسلوب عنه كل شيء حتى نفسه، إلا أن من العوارض ما يعرضه بشرط الوجود وهو عوارض الهوية، ومنها ما يعرضه في الوجود وهو عوارض الماهية، وعوارض الوجود الذهني داخلة عندهم في عوارض ،الهوية ومنها ما يعرضه في الوجود وهو عوارض الماهية وعوارض الوجود الذهني داخلة عندهم في عوارض الماهية فلا يرد ما قيل: إنه يلزمهم أن لا يقولوا بنحو الذاتية والعرضية والكلية والجزئية، ولا شك أن إنكارها مكابرة .

قوله : ( لأنا) نقول : ذلك الذي ذكرتموه إلخ إن قلت : لعله يقول بمجعولية هوية الانضمام مثلاً قلت: بعد تسليم تحقق الهوية الانضمامية تلك الهوية إن كانت بسيطة لم يتعلق بها الجعل، وإن كانت مركبة كان المجعول هوية الانضمامية، وننقل الكلام إليها فيتسلسل بمعنى أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد يمكن تعلق الجعل به .

قوله : ( والاعتراض المذكور (معارضة لا نقض إجمالي كما ذهب إليه الشارح الأبهري؛ إذ لا يمكن إجراء الدليل المذكور بعينه في المركبات كما نقل الشارح وفيه تأمل، لأن النقض الإجمالي علي وجهين الأول جريان الدليل في موضع مع تخلف الحكم عنه، الثاني استلزام تمامه محذوراً، والمنفي هاهنا هو الأول لا الثاني، فليتأمل.
We say: That which you mentioned of composition or existence also has a quiddity. It is either simple, so it is not created on that estimation, or composite, so the discussion returns to it and to its simple parts until the absence of creation appears absolutely. The mentioned objection is a counterargument. The solution is that the simple has a quiddity and existence, so perhaps possibility (imkan) attaches to the simple quiddity in relation to existence. Possibility requires two things, not two parts, so its attaching to the simple is not impossible. Know that this problem is one of the slippery slopes where the feet of minds slip. We want to establish your feet in this problem by a subtle indication to the precise statement of the point of dispute and the origin of the schools; the truth is not veiled from its seeker after that precisification. So we say: The philosophers, when...

His statement (the mentioned objection is a counterargument, not a general refutation as was thought, because the mentioned proof for the non-creation of simples does not apply to composites and does not entail impossibility; rather, what entails impossibility is the claim, namely the non-creation of simples. So the mentioned objection establishes the opposite of the claim, thus it is a counterargument).

His statement (and the solution is that the simple... It is not hidden that the necessary consequence is that the simple is created in respect of existence, and there is no dispute about that).

His statement (by a subtle indication etc.) is what he pointed to by his saying 'except what is attributed to the Mu'tazila', for it is an indication to the precise statement of a meaning in which dispute is possible. As for what precedes it, it is an explanation of the origin of the three schools and that they are all true. His statement (when they divided existence etc.) As for those who deny mental existence, they say: Everything that attaches to a thing attaches to it in external existence and in the actual state; the non-existent is stripped of everything, even itself, except that among the accidents there are those that attach to it under the condition of existence, and they are accidents of identity (huwiyya), and among them are those that attach to it in existence, and they are accidents of quiddity (mahiyya). According to them, the accidents of mental existence are included among the accidents of identity. And among them are those that attach to it in existence, and they are accidents of quiddity; the accidents of mental existence are included among the accidents of quiddity. So what was said does not follow: that it necessarily follows that they do not affirm categories such as essentiality, accidentality, universality, and particularity. There is no doubt that denying them is obstinacy).

His statement (for we say: that which you mentioned etc.) If you say: Perhaps he holds the creation of the identity of composition, for example? I say: After conceding the realization of the identity of composition, if that identity is simple, no making (ja'l) attaches to it; if it is composite, then what is made is the identity of composition. We transfer the discussion to it, and so it goes on ad infinitum, meaning that it does not end at a limit where the attaching of making is possible.

His statement (and the mentioned objection is a counterargument, not a general refutation as al-Ābharī held, because it is not possible to apply the mentioned proof itself to composites as the commentator reported, and there is consideration in it, for the general refutation is of two kinds: the first is the application of the proof in a place while the judgment is absent; the second is its completion entailing a problem. What is negated here is the first, not the second. So let one consider).
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer set within a commentary on the problem of whether quiddities are created. The commentator begins by presenting an objection: if every quiddity is either simple or composite, and simple quiddities cannot be created (since creation is thought to require composition), then no quiddity can be created, leading to a regress. The solution is that possibility (imkan) can attach to a simple quiddity in relation to existence without requiring two parts; it requires two 'things' (the quiddity and existence) not two 'parts' of a composite. The commentator then clarifies that the objection is a counterargument (mu'arada) that establishes the opposite of the claim, not a general refutation (naqd ijmalī). He explains that the proof against the creation of simples does not apply to composites, so it is not a refutation. He also addresses the denial of mental existence by some, noting that for them accidents are divided into those that require the condition of existence (accidents of identity) and those that occur within existence (accidents of quiddity), and mental accidents are included in one of these categories. The commentary then discusses a potential regress in the case of the identity of composition, concluding that if the identity is simple, no making attaches; if composite, the discussion transfers. Finally, the commentator refines the notion of general refutation, stating that here the first type (applying the proof in a place where the judgment is absent) is not possible, only the second type (entailing a problem) might apply, requiring careful consideration.

Key Terms
مجعولة
بسيط
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the objection is a direct refutation (naqd) of the philosopher's proof in the same terms.
Correction: The commentator explicitly says it is a counterargument (mu'arada), not a refutation, because it does not apply the same proof to composites.

Checkpoint
What does the objection conclude if simple quiddities are not created?
A) Only composites are created.
B) No quiddity can be created at all.
C) Simple quiddities are created through existence.
D) The philosophers' position is false.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses a regress argument to show that denying creation of simples leads to no creation at all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's creation, do you limit it to things that are complex and visibly compounded? Where in your heart have you made an exception for the 'simple' or the 'natural'? How does this objection force you to acknowledge God's hand in every particle?
?
This argument shows that denying God's creation of the simplest realities leads to a logical dead end. Are there areas of your life where you assume some part of your existence is not under His direct control—your habits, your thoughts, your very essence?
?
The objector calls his argument a mu‘āraḍa, a counterargument, not a refutation. It does not prove the philosopher wrong but shows the opposite conclusion is also valid. Are you willing to accept that your own certainty about God's power might be challenged by logical rigor? Will you let this argument deepen your reliance on Him?

The Solution: Possibility and the Simple Quiddity
Solution using possibility for simple quiddities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of possibility (imkan) resolves the problem of creation of simple quiddities by relating the simple quiddity to existence.

Core Idea
Possibility requires two things, not two parts — a simple quiddity can be created through its relation to existence.

The commentator presents the solution to the objection. He states that the simple quiddity can receive the attribute of possibility in relation to existence. Possibility does not require the quiddity to have parts; it requires two things: the quiddity itself and its existence. Thus, a simple quiddity can be considered created in respect of its existence, which is not disputed.

Guided Path
1
Read the solution passage
Read the Arabic passage that presents the solution. It explains that the simple has both quiddity and existence.
The word 'imkan' (possibility) is key.
2
Understand possibility's relation
Possibility attaches to the simple quiddity in relation to existence. It requires two 'things' (thay'ayn): the quiddity and existence, not two 'parts' (juz'ayn) of the quiddity itself.
This avoids the need for composition.
3
Why this solves the problem
Since possibility does not require the quiddity to be composite, a simple quiddity can be considered created (maj'ul) in terms of its existence without violating its simplicity.
The creation is in respect of existence, not the quiddity's intrinsic structure.
4
Note the scope of creation
The commentator adds that the creation of simples in respect of existence is not disputed. The real dispute is about quiddity being created.
This narrows down the point of disagreement.

We say: That which you mentioned of composition or existence also has a quiddity. It is either simple, so it is not created on that estimation, or composite, so the discussion returns to it and to its simple parts until the absence of creation appears absolutely. The mentioned objection is a counterargument. The solution is that the simple has a quiddity and existence, so perhaps possibility (imkan) attaches to the simple quiddity in relation to existence. Possibility requires two things, not two parts, so its attaching to the simple is not impossible. Know that this problem is one of the slippery slopes where the feet of minds slip. We want to establish your feet in this problem by a subtle indication to the precise statement of the point of dispute and the origin of the schools; the truth is not veiled from its seeker after that precisification. So we say: The philosophers, when...

His statement (the mentioned objection is a counterargument, not a general refutation as was thought, because the mentioned proof for the non-creation of simples does not apply to composites and does not entail impossibility; rather, what entails impossibility is the claim, namely the non-creation of simples. So the mentioned objection establishes the opposite of the claim, thus it is a counterargument).

His statement (and the solution is that the simple... It is not hidden that the necessary consequence is that the simple is created in respect of existence, and there is no dispute about that).

His statement (by a subtle indication etc.) is what he pointed to by his saying 'except what is attributed to the Mu'tazila', for it is an indication to the precise statement of a meaning in which dispute is possible. As for what precedes it, it is an explanation of the origin of the three schools and that they are all true. His statement (when they divided existence etc.) As for those who deny mental existence, they say: Everything that attaches to a thing attaches to it in external existence and in the actual state; the non-existent is stripped of everything, even itself, except that among the accidents there are those that attach to it under the condition of existence, and they are accidents of identity (huwiyya), and among them are those that attach to it in existence, and they are accidents of quiddity (mahiyya). According to them, the accidents of mental existence are included among the accidents of identity. And among them are those that attach to it in existence, and they are accidents of quiddity; the accidents of mental existence are included among the accidents of quiddity. So what was said does not follow: that it necessarily follows that they do not affirm categories such as essentiality, accidentality, universality, and particularity. There is no doubt that denying them is obstinacy).

His statement (for we say: that which you mentioned etc.) If you say: Perhaps he holds the creation of the identity of composition, for example? I say: After conceding the realization of the identity of composition, if that identity is simple, no making (ja'l) attaches to it; if it is composite, then what is made is the identity of composition. We transfer the discussion to it, and so it goes on ad infinitum, meaning that it does not end at a limit where the attaching of making is possible.

His statement (and the mentioned objection is a counterargument, not a general refutation as al-Ābharī held, because it is not possible to apply the mentioned proof itself to composites as the commentator reported, and there is consideration in it, for the general refutation is of two kinds: the first is the application of the proof in a place while the judgment is absent; the second is its completion entailing a problem. What is negated here is the first, not the second. So let one consider).
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer set within a commentary on the problem of whether quiddities are created. The commentator begins by presenting an objection: if every quiddity is either simple or composite, and simple quiddities cannot be created (since creation is thought to require composition), then no quiddity can be created, leading to a regress. The solution is that possibility (imkan) can attach to a simple quiddity in relation to existence without requiring two parts; it requires two 'things' (the quiddity and existence) not two 'parts' of a composite. The commentator then clarifies that the objection is a counterargument (mu'arada) that establishes the opposite of the claim, not a general refutation (naqd ijmalī). He explains that the proof against the creation of simples does not apply to composites, so it is not a refutation. He also addresses the denial of mental existence by some, noting that for them accidents are divided into those that require the condition of existence (accidents of identity) and those that occur within existence (accidents of quiddity), and mental accidents are included in one of these categories. The commentary then discusses a potential regress in the case of the identity of composition, concluding that if the identity is simple, no making attaches; if composite, the discussion transfers. Finally, the commentator refines the notion of general refutation, stating that here the first type (applying the proof in a place where the judgment is absent) is not possible, only the second type (entailing a problem) might apply, requiring careful consideration.

Key Terms
بسيط
ماهية
وجود
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that possibility (imkan) requires the quiddity to have composition or parts.
Correction: Possibility requires two things (quiddity and existence), not two parts of the quiddity, so it can attach to a simple quiddity.

Checkpoint
Why doesn't possibility require the simple quiddity to have parts?
A) Because possibility is a mental attribute.
B) Because it requires two things (quiddity and existence), not two parts.
C) Because simples have no composition.
D) Because existence is simple.

Key Takeaway
The creation of simples is understood through the relation of possibility to existence, not through composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your own existence as a relation to your Creator, or do you feel you exist independently? How does this passage challenge your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
The simple quiddity has no parts, yet its existence is given. In what area of your life have you been trying to add parts to yourself to earn existence before Allah?
?
What does it mean for you that your very possibility is a relation, not a property? Can you live today aware that each moment of your existence is a gift from Him, not a given?

Counterargument vs. General Refutation
Distinction between counterargument and general refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a counterargument (mu'arada) and a general refutation (naqd ijmalī) in the context of logical argumentation.

Core Idea
A counterargument establishes the opposite claim; a general refutation shows the same proof fails elsewhere.

The commentator clarifies that the objection raised against the philosophers is not a general refutation (naqd ijmalī) as some thought, but a counterargument (mu'arada). He explains that a general refutation would require the same proof to be applied to composites and fail, but that does not happen here. Instead, the objection simply establishes the contradictory of the claim. The commentator further distinguishes two types of general refutation: one where the proof applies but the judgment is absent, and another where the proof's completion entails a problem. Here, only the second type might be relevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on mu'arada vs. naqd
Read the Arabic passage that distinguishes the objection from a general refutation. Note the key terms.
The commentator repeats this distinction twice.
2
Understand mu'arada
A counterargument (mu'arada) establishes the opposite claim of the original thesis. Here, the thesis was that simples are not created; the objection argues that they are created.
It does not use the same proof.
3
Understand naqd ijmalī
A general refutation (naqd ijmalī) would show that the same proof, when applied to another case, fails. The commentator says this is not happening here because the proof for simples does not apply to composites.
4
Learn the two types of naqd ijmalī
The first type: the proof applies but the judgment is absent. The second: the proof's completion entails a problem. Here, only the second might apply, but it is not the case.
This nuance is important for precise logical analysis.
