The Three Schools and the Point of Dispute
Importance of precise dispute formulation and three schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of precisely defining the point of dispute regarding the creation of simple quiddities and recognize that multiple schools have valid positions.

Core Idea
The truth is not hidden once the point of dispute is clarified — all three schools have valid insights.

The commentator emphasizes that this problem is a slippery slope. He intends to establish the reader's position by offering a subtle indication to the precise formulation of the point of dispute and the origin of the three schools. He notes that all three schools are correct when properly understood. This lesson explores that precise delineation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the slippery slope
Read the Arabic passage that warns about the difficulty of this problem and the intention to fix the reader's stance.
The phrase 'tazillu fīhā aqdām al-adhhān' vividly portrays the intellectual danger.
2
Understand the need for precise formulation
The commentator stresses that a subtle indication (ishārat khafiyya) to the precise site of dispute (taḥrīr maḥall al-nizā‘) is necessary to clarify the truth.
Precision is key to avoiding error.
3
The three schools
The passage mentions the origin of the three schools (madhāhib) and that they are all true (ḥaqqa). The schools are: those who deny creation of simples, those who affirm it in existence, and the Mu'tazila position.
The commentator uses the phrase 'illa mā yunsabu ilā al-Mu‘tazila' to point to the precise issue.
4
Conclusion: truth after precisification
Once the dispute is precisely defined, the truth becomes evident and each school's position can be seen as valid in its own respect.

نقول : (ذلك الذي ذكرتموه من الانضمام أو الوجود ) أيضاً له ماهية، فهي إما بسيطة فلا تكون مجعولة على ذلك التقدير ( أو مركبة فيعود الكلام فيه، وفي أجزائه البسيطة حتى يظهر ارتفاع المجعولية مطلقاً، والاعتراض المذكور معارضة، (والحل) هو : أن البسيط له ماهية ،ووجود فلعل الإمكان يعرض (للماهية ) البسيطة ( بالنسبة إلى الوجود)، فالإمكان يقتضي شيئين لا جزءين حتى يستحيل عروضه للبسيط. ( ) واعلم أن هذه المسألة من المداحض التي تزلق فيها أقدام الأذهان، ( وإنا نريد أن نثبت أقدامك في هذه المسألة ( بإشارة خفية إلى تحرير محل النزاع ومنشأ المذاهب والحق لا يحتجب عن طالبه بعد ذلك ( التحرير ( فنقول : الحكماء لما

قوله : ( والاعتراض المذكور معارضة وليس نقضاً إجمالياً على ما توهم، إذ الدليل المذكور لعدم مجعولية البسائط لا يجري في المركبات ولا يستلزم محالاً، إنما المستلزم للمحال هو المدعى، أعني عدم مجعولية البسائط، فيكون الاعتراض المذكور مثبتاً لنقيض المدعى، فيكون معارضة .

قوله : ( والحل أن البسيط (إلخ لا يخفى أن اللازم منه أن يكون البسيط مجعولاً باعتبار الوجود، ولا نزاع فيه .

قوله : ( بإشارة خفية إلخ وهو ما أشار إليه بقوله : إلا ما ينسب إلى المعتزلة، فإنه إشارة إلى تحرير معنى يمكن النزاع فيه، وأما ما قبله فهو بيان لمنشأ المذاهب الثلاثة وأنها كلها حقة قوله : ( لما قسموا الوجود إلخ) وأما النافون للوجود الذهني فيقولون : إن كل ما يعرض للشيء فإنما يعرض له في الخارج ونفس الأمر، والمعدوم مسلوب عنه كل شيء حتى نفسه، إلا أن من العوارض ما يعرضه بشرط الوجود وهو عوارض الهوية، ومنها ما يعرضه في الوجود وهو عوارض الماهية، وعوارض الوجود الذهني داخلة عندهم في عوارض ،الهوية ومنها ما يعرضه في الوجود وهو عوارض الماهية وعوارض الوجود الذهني داخلة عندهم في عوارض الماهية فلا يرد ما قيل: إنه يلزمهم أن لا يقولوا بنحو الذاتية والعرضية والكلية والجزئية، ولا شك أن إنكارها مكابرة .

قوله : ( لأنا) نقول : ذلك الذي ذكرتموه إلخ إن قلت : لعله يقول بمجعولية هوية الانضمام مثلاً قلت: بعد تسليم تحقق الهوية الانضمامية تلك الهوية إن كانت بسيطة لم يتعلق بها الجعل، وإن كانت مركبة كان المجعول هوية الانضمامية، وننقل الكلام إليها فيتسلسل بمعنى أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد يمكن تعلق الجعل به .

قوله : ( والاعتراض المذكور (معارضة لا نقض إجمالي كما ذهب إليه الشارح الأبهري؛ إذ لا يمكن إجراء الدليل المذكور بعينه في المركبات كما نقل الشارح وفيه تأمل، لأن النقض الإجمالي علي وجهين الأول جريان الدليل في موضع مع تخلف الحكم عنه، الثاني استلزام تمامه محذوراً، والمنفي هاهنا هو الأول لا الثاني، فليتأمل.
We say: That which you mentioned of composition or existence also has a quiddity. It is either simple, so it is not created on that estimation, or composite, so the discussion returns to it and to its simple parts until the absence of creation appears absolutely. The mentioned objection is a counterargument. The solution is that the simple has a quiddity and existence, so perhaps possibility (imkan) attaches to the simple quiddity in relation to existence. Possibility requires two things, not two parts, so its attaching to the simple is not impossible. Know that this problem is one of the slippery slopes where the feet of minds slip. We want to establish your feet in this problem by a subtle indication to the precise statement of the point of dispute and the origin of the schools; the truth is not veiled from its seeker after that precisification. So we say: The philosophers, when...

His statement (the mentioned objection is a counterargument, not a general refutation as was thought, because the mentioned proof for the non-creation of simples does not apply to composites and does not entail impossibility; rather, what entails impossibility is the claim, namely the non-creation of simples. So the mentioned objection establishes the opposite of the claim, thus it is a counterargument).

His statement (and the solution is that the simple... It is not hidden that the necessary consequence is that the simple is created in respect of existence, and there is no dispute about that).

His statement (by a subtle indication etc.) is what he pointed to by his saying 'except what is attributed to the Mu'tazila', for it is an indication to the precise statement of a meaning in which dispute is possible. As for what precedes it, it is an explanation of the origin of the three schools and that they are all true. His statement (when they divided existence etc.) As for those who deny mental existence, they say: Everything that attaches to a thing attaches to it in external existence and in the actual state; the non-existent is stripped of everything, even itself, except that among the accidents there are those that attach to it under the condition of existence, and they are accidents of identity (huwiyya), and among them are those that attach to it in existence, and they are accidents of quiddity (mahiyya). According to them, the accidents of mental existence are included among the accidents of identity. And among them are those that attach to it in existence, and they are accidents of quiddity; the accidents of mental existence are included among the accidents of quiddity. So what was said does not follow: that it necessarily follows that they do not affirm categories such as essentiality, accidentality, universality, and particularity. There is no doubt that denying them is obstinacy).

His statement (for we say: that which you mentioned etc.) If you say: Perhaps he holds the creation of the identity of composition, for example? I say: After conceding the realization of the identity of composition, if that identity is simple, no making (ja'l) attaches to it; if it is composite, then what is made is the identity of composition. We transfer the discussion to it, and so it goes on ad infinitum, meaning that it does not end at a limit where the attaching of making is possible.

His statement (and the mentioned objection is a counterargument, not a general refutation as al-Ābharī held, because it is not possible to apply the mentioned proof itself to composites as the commentator reported, and there is consideration in it, for the general refutation is of two kinds: the first is the application of the proof in a place while the judgment is absent; the second is its completion entailing a problem. What is negated here is the first, not the second. So let one consider).
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer set within a commentary on the problem of whether quiddities are created. The commentator begins by presenting an objection: if every quiddity is either simple or composite, and simple quiddities cannot be created (since creation is thought to require composition), then no quiddity can be created, leading to a regress. The solution is that possibility (imkan) can attach to a simple quiddity in relation to existence without requiring two parts; it requires two 'things' (the quiddity and existence) not two 'parts' of a composite. The commentator then clarifies that the objection is a counterargument (mu'arada) that establishes the opposite of the claim, not a general refutation (naqd ijmalī). He explains that the proof against the creation of simples does not apply to composites, so it is not a refutation. He also addresses the denial of mental existence by some, noting that for them accidents are divided into those that require the condition of existence (accidents of identity) and those that occur within existence (accidents of quiddity), and mental accidents are included in one of these categories. The commentary then discusses a potential regress in the case of the identity of composition, concluding that if the identity is simple, no making attaches; if composite, the discussion transfers. Finally, the commentator refines the notion of general refutation, stating that here the first type (applying the proof in a place where the judgment is absent) is not possible, only the second type (entailing a problem) might apply, requiring careful consideration.

Key Terms
معتزلة
مذاهب ثلاث
تحرير محل النزاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the three schools of thought are in direct contradiction and only one can be correct.
Correction: The commentator asserts that all three schools are true (ḥaqqa) when the precise point of dispute is clarified.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the three schools?
A) Only one is correct.
B) They are all true when the point of dispute is clarified.
C) They are all false.
D) Only the philosophers are correct.

Key Takeaway
Clarifying the precise point of dispute reveals that the three schools are not in contradiction; each has a valid aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological disagreement, do you rush to judge one side as wrong, or do you first strive to understand the precise point of dispute? How can you change your approach today to reflect the adab required by this lesson?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly. Have you ever considered that someone who disagrees with you might be 'correct' in a specific sense that you haven't taken the time to understand? What step can you take this week to explore an opposing view with the precision this lesson demands?
?
The commentator's warning about a 'slippery' problem applies not only to theological disputes but to your own inner doubts. Are you willing to honestly articulate your deepest spiritual questions with the same precision, even if it means admitting you don't have all the answers?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Quiddity Is Not Made
Jurjani explains that quiddity is not made and that made-ness attaches to existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why quiddity itself is not considered 'made' and why the need for a maker is a property of existence, not essence.

Core Idea
Quiddity is not made; made-ness is a property of existence.

This passage from Jurjani addresses a subtle but crucial distinction in kalam: the quiddity (essence) of a thing is not directly created by a maker. Instead, being 'made' applies only to the existence that realizes that essence. The author uses a clear example—imagining a human without considering existence—to show that the quiddity itself does not require a maker. Understanding this distinction clarifies the relationship between divine causation and the essences of things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Jurjani builds his argument: first stating the claim, then explaining it, giving an example, and finally clarifying the meaning of 'made-ness'.
The passage is dense; take your time to parse each phrase.
2
Identify the main distinction
Look for the contrast between 'quiddity' (ماهية) and 'existence' (هوية). The key point is that made-ness (مجعولية) is an accident of existence, not of quiddity as such.
Quiddity in itself has no need for a maker.
3
Analyze the example
Consider the example: imagining a human that is 'not made'. Why would that not be a human? Because any imagined human inherently involves existence, so the quiddity alone cannot be a subject for 'made-ness'.
The example shows the logical consequence of separating quiddity from existence.
4
Understand the redefinition of 'made-ness'
Jurjani explicitly states that by 'made-ness' they mean the need for an efficient cause. This need is a concomitant of existence, not of quiddity. Reflect on why this is 'a true statement about which there is no doubt'.
This redefinition is key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنبهوا بقولهم أن الماهية غير مجعولة على أن المجعولية إنما تلحق الهوية لا الماهية، أي هي من عوارض الوجود الخارجي، من عوارض الماهية من حيث هي هي. فلو تصور مثلاً إنسان غير مجعول لم يكن ذلك المتصور لا إنساناً حتى يلزم التناقض وأرادوا يعني هؤلاء النافين بالمجعولية الاحتياج إلى الفاعل الموجد، وهذا كلام حق لا مرية فيه؛ لأن الاحتياج من لوازم الوجود دون الماهية
So they drew attention by their statement that quiddity is not made, to the fact that made-ness attaches only to identity, not to quiddity, i.e., it is among the properties of external existence, not among the properties of quiddity insofar as it is itself. For if one imagines, for example, a human that is not made, that imagined thing would not be a human, so that contradiction follows. And they intended – meaning these negators – by 'made-ness' the need for the efficient cause, and this is a true statement about which there is no doubt, because need is among the necessaries of existence, not of quiddity.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents the argument that quiddity (ماهية) is not made (مجعولة) in itself. The maker's act brings existence, not quiddity. So made-ness is a property of existence. The example: if one imagines a human quiddity without considering its existence, that quiddity is not made. The intended meaning of 'made-ness' is the need for an efficient cause (الفاعل الموجد). This need is a concomitant (لازم) of existence, not of quiddity. Thus the argument establishes that made-ness is not part of the quiddity itself.

Key Terms
ماهية
مجعولية
هوية
الاحتياج إلى الفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that quiddity itself requires a maker, because everything that exists seems to need a cause.
Correction: The text clarifies that the need for a maker is a property of existence, not of quiddity. Quiddity considered in itself has no such need.

Checkpoint
What is meant by 'made-ness' (المجعولية) in this passage?
A) The quiddity having parts
B) The need for an efficient cause
C) Being created in time
D) Being composite

Key Takeaway
The need for a maker attaches to existence, not to quiddity itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are not a necessary being. Your quiddity does not require a cause, but your existence does. Does your daily life reflect the reality that you are constantly dependent on the One who gives you existence, moment by moment? How often do you acknowledge that dependence in your actions?
?
The text says that 'need is among the necessaries of existence.' Your existence is a constant need for God. Are you aware of this need every moment? Or do you fall into the illusion of self-sufficiency? What would change if you truly felt your need for Him at every breath?
?
If you were to imagine yourself as 'not made,' you would cease to be human. Your very humanity is a product of being made. How does this truth shape your humility before your Creator? Are there areas where you resist acknowledging that you are completely dependent on His act of bringing you into existence and sustaining you?

Causality and Created Things

The Second Interpretation of Made-ness: Need for a Constitutive Part
Jurjani presents the second interpretation of made-ness as need for a constitutive part, distinguishing composite from simple quiddities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second interpretation of made-ness as need for a constitutive part, and how it applies to composite quiddities but not simple ones.

Core Idea
Composite quiddities are made because they need their parts; simple ones are not.

In this passage, Jurjani presents an alternative understanding of 'made-ness' (مجعولية). Some scholars expanded it to include not only need for an efficient cause but also need for a constitutive part. This interpretation has important implications for composite quiddities, which by their very essence depend on their parts, unlike simple quiddities. We will explore this distinction and the proof that quiddities are not made in themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation slowly, noting the key concepts.
Pay attention to the definitions: made-ness as need, composite vs. simple.
2
Identify the two kinds of need
The passage distinguishes need for an efficient cause and need for a constitutive part. Notice how made-ness is defined as need for 'the other' encompassing both.
Remember that the second interpretation emphasizes need for a part.
3
Understand the distinction between composite and simple
Composite quiddities (e.g., humanity: animal + rational) have need for their parts intrinsically. Simple quiddities (e.g., intellect) lack this need. Think about why the proof shows quiddities are not made in themselves.
The proof uses humanity as an example: humanity remains humanity whether or not making is considered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم : وقد أرادوا بالمجعولية الاحتياج إلى الغير سواء كان فاعلاً موجداً أو جزءاً مقوماً ، أنها أي المجعولية بهذا المعنى تلحق الماهية المركبة لذاتها، مع قطع النظر عن وجودها، فإن الاحتياج إلى جزئها الداخل في قوامها يلحقها لنفس مفهومها من حيث هو هو قطعاً، فأينما وجدت الماهية المركبة كانت متصفة بالاحتياج إلى الغير، بخلاف البسيطة؛ إذ ليس لها هذا الاحتياج اللازم للماهية وإن اشتركتا في الاحتياج اللازم للوجود . وأرادوا بقولهم: الإمكان لا يعرض للبسيط؛ إذ ليس فيه شيئان، أن الاحتياج العارض والمجعولية، وإلى تطبيق الدليل المذكور سابقاً لعدم المجعولية على هذا المعنى، بأن يراد أنه لو كانت الإنسانية متلبسة بالجعل في نفسها لم تكن الإنسانية عند عدم اعتبار جعل الجاعل معها إنسانية، والتالي باطل لأن الإنسانية إنسانية اعتبر معها الجعل أو لا .

قوله : ( وأرادوا إلخ) أي المجعولية المترتبة على الاحتياج إلى الموجد، وكذلك الكلام فيما سيأتي، لا نفس الاحتياج بطريق التسامح بذكر المسبب وإرادة السبب على ما وهم الاحتياج إلى الموجد متقدم على الإيجاد المتقدم على الوجود، فكيف يكون من عوارض الوجود الخارجي، بل هو من عوارض الوجود الذهني، فإن الماهية الممكنة الموجودة إذا حصلت في العقل انتزع منها الإمكان والاحتياج وكونها موجودة والوجود بخلاف المجعولية، فإنها متأخرة عن وجودها بدليل صحة دخول الفاء بأن يقال الماهية أمكنت فاحتاجت فأوجدت، فوجدت فصارت مجعولة .

قوله : (سواء كان إلخ هذا التعميم بالنظر إلى الواقع لثبوت الاحتياج إلى الموجد لجميع الممكنات لا لأن له مدخلا في كون المركبة مجعولة دون البسيطة إذ بناء الفرق بثبوت الاحتياج إلى الأجزاء المركبة دون البسيطة .

قوله : ( عن وجودها أي خصوصية وجودها الخارجي والذهني.

قوله : (وأرادوا ) تطبيق لدليلهم على هذا المعنى.

قوله : ( بالمجعولية الاحتياج إلى (الفاعل الظاهر أن المجعولية هي الوصف المترتب على الاحتياج، لكن لما كان الفرق باعتبار المبدئية نصوا على الفارق، وهاهنا بحث وهو : أن ظاهر ما سبق من تفصيل العوارض وتقسيمها إلى الثلاثة يدل على أن العوارض المذكورة ما يعرض باعتبار أحد الوجودين مطلقاً، أو بخصوصية أحدهما، فجعل الاحتياج إلى الفاعل من عوارض الوجود الخارجي، أي عارضاً باعتباره وبعده محل تأمل. وإن أراد أن الموصوف به أمر خارجي ولو حال الاتصاف يلزم أن يكون نفس الوجود الخارجي من هذا القسم لا من القسم الثالث أعني المعقولات الثانية مع أنه منها، فتأمل جوابه .
And some said: They intended by made-ness the need for the other, whether it be an efficient cause or a constitutive part. That it [made-ness in this sense] attaches to the composite quiddity for itself, disregarding its existence. For the need for its part that enters into its constitution attaches to it for its own concept insofar as it is itself, absolutely. So wherever the composite quiddity is found, it is qualified by need for the other, unlike the simple quiddity, for it does not have this need that is necessary for the quiddity, even though they share in the need necessary for existence. And they intended by their statement: 'Possibility does not occur to the simple, for there are not two things in it' – that the occurring need and made-ness [apply]. And to apply the previously mentioned proof for the absence of made-ness to this meaning, by intending that if humanity were clothed with making in itself, humanity would not be humanity when assuming not considering the making of the maker along with it; and the consequent is false, because humanity is humanity whether making is considered with it or not.  His saying: 'And they intended etc.' i.e., the made-ness that follows from the need for the efficient cause, and likewise the speech in what follows, not the need itself by way of tolerance by mentioning the effect and intending the cause, as was imagined – the need for the efficient cause precedes the bringing into existence which precedes existence, so how can it be from the properties of external existence? Rather, it is from the properties of mental existence. For the possible existing quiddity, when it obtains in the mind, possibility and need and its being existent and existence are abstracted from it, unlike made-ness, for it is posterior to its existence, evidenced by the correctness of using 'then' such that one says: the quiddity was possible, then it needed, then it was brought into existence, then it existed, then it became made.  His saying: 'Whether it be etc.' This generalization is with regard to reality, because the need for the efficient cause is established for all possibles, not because it has a role in the composite being made without the simple, since the difference is built on the existence of need for the parts for the composite without the simple.  His saying: 'Disregarding its existence' i.e., the particularity of its external and mental existence.  His saying: 'And they intended' – an application of their proof to this meaning.  His saying: 'By made-ness the need for the efficient cause' – it is apparent that made-ness is the description that follows from the need, but since the difference is by consideration of the source (al-mabda'iyya), they specified the differentiator. And here there is a discussion: that the apparent from what preceded of the detail of properties and their division into three indicates that the mentioned properties occur by virtue of either of the two existences absolutely, or by the particularity of one of them. So making the need for the efficient cause from the properties of external existence, i.e., occurring by virtue of it, is a matter for contemplation afterwards. And if one intends that the described thing is an external thing, even at the moment of qualification, it would follow that the external existence itself is of this division, not of the third division, namely the secondary intelligibles, while it is among them. So reflect on its answer.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents a second interpretation of made-ness. Some scholars understood made-ness not only as need for an efficient cause but also as need for a constitutive part (جزء مقوم). Under this interpretation, composite quiddities (like human: animal + rational) are made even without considering existence, because their very quiddity depends on their parts. Simple quiddities (like intellect) do not have this need for parts, though they share the need for an efficient cause at the level of existence. The passage then applies the proof that quiddity is not made to this interpretation: if humanity were made in itself, then without the maker's act, it would not be humanity, but humanity is humanity regardless. The subsequent commentary discusses the relationship between need and made-ness, arguing that need is a property of mental existence, not external existence, because need is abstracted from the quiddity when considered in the mind, while made-ness is posterior to actual existence. The commentator also notes a potential issue with classifying need for the efficient cause as a property of external existence, since it seems to be a secondary intelligible (mental property). He leaves it for further reflection.

Key Terms
مجعولية
ماهية مركبة
جزء مقوم
احتياج
بسيطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all quiddities are made in the same way.
Correction: The passage clarifies that composite quiddities are made in the sense of needing parts, while simple quiddities are not made in that sense; they only share need for an efficient cause.

Checkpoint
What is the second interpretation of made-ness in this passage?
A) Need for an efficient cause
B) Need for a constitutive part
C) Being created in time
D) Being composite

Key Takeaway
Composite quiddities are considered 'made' because their very concept entails need for constitutive parts, unlike simple quiddities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very essence is composed of parts that you did not give yourself—your body, your intellect, your desires—how can you claim self-sufficiency? Does this not demand that you acknowledge your total dependence on the One who composed you? Will you live today as if you are the maker of your own identity?
?
The passage argues that humanity is humanity regardless of whether the Maker's act is considered. But you know that without His constant act of sustaining, you would cease to exist. How does this truth confront your moments of pride and forgetfulness? What specific habit can you change to remember that you are not self-made?
?
You share with all creation the need for an efficient cause—your existence is a gift. But unlike simple quiddities, you are composite, needing parts that can be taken away. How does the fragility of your composition press you to turn to the Eternal One who alone has no parts and no need?

Two Meanings of Maj'uliyya and the Distinction Between Composite and Simple Essences
Definition of maj'uliyya with two interpretations and distinction between composite and simple essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of 'being made' (maj'uliyya) and why composite essences intrinsically need another while simple essences do not.

Core Idea
Composite essences need another in their very concept; simple essences only need another through existence.

This passage from Iji's Mawaqif clarifies what scholars mean when they say essences are 'made' (maj'ula). Some limit it to needing an efficient cause; others extend it to needing any other, including a part. The key distinction emerges between composite and simple essences: composites need their parts by definition, while simples do not.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the two definitions of maj'uliyya and the examples given.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the terms: احتياج, فاعل موجد, جزء مقوم.
2
Identify the two meanings and the distinction
The passage first gives a narrow meaning (need for efficient cause) then a broader one (need for any other). It then says composite essences inherently need their parts, while simple essences do not.
The broader meaning includes both efficient cause and constitutive parts.
3
Reflect on why simple essences are different
Ask yourself: why does a simple essence not have intrinsic need? The answer is that its concept does not include parts. Both types share need arising from existence, but only composites need another in their essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأرادوا) يعني هؤلاء النافين ) . (بالمجعولية الاحتياج إلى (الفاعل الموجد، وهذا كلام حق لا مرية فيه؛ لأن الاحتياج من لوازم الوجود دون الماهية ) وقال بعضهم : وقد أرادوا بالمجعولية الاحتياج إلى الغير سواء كان فاعلاً موجداً أو جزءاً مقوماً ، (أنها ) أي المجعولية بهذا المعنى ( تلحق الماهية المركبة لذاتها، مع قطع النظر عن وجودها، (فإن الاحتياج إلى جزئها الداخل في قوامها ( يلحقها لنفس مفهومها من حيث هو هو (قطعا)، فأينما وجدت الماهية المركبة كانت متصفة بالاحتياج إلى الغير، بخلاف البسيطة؛ إذ ليس لها هذا الاحتياج اللازم للماهية وإن اشتركتا في الاحتياج اللازم للوجود .
(And they intended — meaning these negators — by 'being made' (المجعولية) the need for the efficient cause (الفاعل الموجد), and this is a true statement with no doubt, because need is a concomitant of existence, not of essence. And some said: they intended by 'being made' the need for another, whether it be an efficient cause or a constitutive part. That is, being made in this sense attaches to the composite essence (الماهية المركبة) in itself, regardless of its existence; for the need for its part that enters into its constitution attaches to it for its very concept per se absolutely. So wherever the composite essence is found, it is qualified by need for another, unlike the simple essence (البسيطة), for it does not have this need that is necessary for essence, although they share in the need necessary for existence.)
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'being made' (المجعولية) as understood by two groups of thinkers. First, some negators of essences being made mean by it the need for an efficient cause (الفاعل الموجد). The author affirms this is correct, because need is a concomitant of existence, not of essence itself. Second, others extend the meaning to include need for any other, whether a cause or a constitutive part (جزء مقوم). The passage then explains that with this broader meaning, being made attaches to composite essences (الماهية المركبة) in their very nature, independent of existence, because they need their constituent parts. In contrast, simple essences (البسيطة) do not have this intrinsic need for another, though both composite and simple essences share the need that comes with existence. Thus, the distinction hinges on whether the need is for an internal part (composite) or only for existence.

Key Terms
ماهية (essence)
مجعولية (being made)
مركبة (composite)
بسيطة (simple)
احتياج (need)
فاعل موجد (efficient cause)
جزء مقوم (constitutive part)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all essences, whether composite or simple, need another in the same way.
Correction: Composite essences need their constitutive parts by definition, independently of existence, whereas simple essences do not have this intrinsic need; they only need another due to existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between composite and simple essences regarding need for another?
A) Both have the same need for another.
B) Composite essences need another per se; simple essences only need another due to existence.
C) Simple essences need another per se; composite essences do not.
D) Neither composite nor simple essences need another.

Key Takeaway
Composite essences inherently need their parts, while simple essences only need a cause for their existence, not for their essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a composite of body and soul. In your daily life, do you truly feel your dependence on your constitutive parts, or do you take them for granted? How would acknowledging this need change your gratitude to the One who composed you?
?
The lesson teaches that even simple essences need a cause for existence. You are far from simple. Yet do you live as if you are self-sufficient? Examine your reliance on Allah—is it a theoretical belief or a lived reality that shapes your actions?
?
The passage distinguishes between need inherent in essence (for composites) and need from existence (for all). Which type of need do you most often ignore? Are you seeking to fulfill your essential needs (like purpose, identity) through worldly means, or are you turning to the Creator who alone can satisfy them?

The Two Interpretations of Majʿūla and the Refutation of Independence
Explains the two meanings of majʿūla and that quiddity is not independent of the agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two senses in which quiddity can be said to be 'made' (majʿūla) and why no rational person, except the Mu'tazila, claims quiddity is independent of the agent.

Core Idea
Quiddity's being-made means it needs something else, whether existence or a constituent part.

This passage from Sharḥ al-Mawāqif addresses a key debate in Islamic theology: in what sense can quiddities (essences) be considered 'made'? The author first clarifies a preliminary point about composite quiddities, then reports two interpretations of the claim that quiddity is made absolutely. He then strongly denies that any rational person holds quiddity to be independent of the agent, except for the Mu'tazila who attribute a kind of self-subsistence to possible non-existents. Let's unpack this carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense terminology: majʿūliyya, iḥtiyāj, māhiyya, etc.
2
Understand the preliminary point
The passage begins with a note about composite quiddity: its occurrence to a simple quiddity is inconceivable. This sets the stage for the discussion on being-made.
This preliminary is affirmed as correct without ambiguity.
3
Grasp the two interpretations of 'quiddity is made'
Some scholars say quiddity is made absolutely. The commentator interprets that they mean 'need' (iḥtiyāj) is incidental to quiddity in a general sense, whether for a creating agent or a constituent part. This is the first interpretation. The second is implied: others deny being-made in a specific sense.
The passage affirms this interpretation as truthful.
4
Identify the Mu'tazila position
The passage explicitly says no rational person claims quiddity is independent of the agent, except the Mu'tazila. They hold that possible non-existents are fixed essences independent of the agent's influence.
This is a sectarian exception to the consensus.
5
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on why this matters: the issue of quiddity's being-made is tied to causality and divine action. Understanding these nuances prevents misunderstandings about divine attribute of creation.
This lesson anticipates further arguments on whether quiddity is created or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للماهية المركبة في حد ذاتها مع قطع النظر عن وجودها لا يتصور عروضه للماهية البسيطة، وهذا أيضاً كلام صواب لا شبهة فيه. وقال بعضهم: الماهية مجعولة مطلقاً سواء كانت مركبة أو بسيطة ، ( وقد أرادوا عروض المجعولية لها في الجملة ) أي أرادوا أن الاحتياج عارض لها أعم من أن يكون عروضه لنفس الماهية أو للوجود، وأعم من أن يكون إلى الفاعل الموجد أو الجزء المقوم. وهذا أيضاً كلام صدق لا شك فيه . ( وأن عاقلا) عطف على أن هذه المسألة، أي واعلم أن عاقلاً (لم يقل بأن الماهية الممكنة مستغنية في تقررها وثبوتها في الخارج عن الفاعل) الموجد، كما يتبادر إليه الوهم من قولهم : الماهية غير مجعولة (إلا ما ينسب إلى المعتزلة) من أن المعدومات الممكنة ذوات متقررة ثابتة في أنفسها من غير تأثير للفاعل فيها، وإنما تأثيره في اتصافها بالوجود
For the composite quiddity in its essence, with disregard of its existence, its occurrence to the simple quiddity is not conceivable. This too is correct speech with no doubt in it. And some said: the quiddity is made absolutely, whether composite or simple, (and they intended the occurrence of being-made to it in general), meaning they intended that need is incidental to it, whether its occurrence is to the quiddity itself or to existence, and whether it is to the creating agent or the constituent part. This too is truthful speech with no doubt in it. (And that a rational person) is coordinated with 'that this issue', i.e., know that a rational person (did not say that the possible quiddity is independent in its determination and its subsistence in the external world from the agent) the creator, as might occur to the illusion from their saying: the quiddity is not made, (except what is attributed to the Mu'tazila) that possible non-existents are determined essences subsisting in themselves without any effect of the agent in them, and that His effect is only in their being qualified with existence.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the author's understanding of the claim that quiddities are made or not made. It begins by stating a preliminary: for a composite quiddity considered in itself apart from existence, its occurrence to a simple quiddity is not conceivable. This is considered correct. Then it reports that some scholars said quiddity is made absolutely. The commentator interprets their intention: they meant that the attribute of being-made (al-majʿūliyya) occurs to quiddity in a general sense, meaning that need (al-iḥtiyāj) is incidental to it. This need can be to the quiddity itself or to its existence, and it can be need for an efficient cause or for a constituent part. This second statement is also affirmed as true. The passage then adds that no rational person has claimed that a possible quiddity is independent in its determination and external existence from the agent, except for the Mu'tazila, who hold that possible non-existents are fixed essences in themselves without the agent's influence; the agent only affects their being qualified with existence. This sets up the debate.

Key Terms
مجعولية (majʿūliyya) – being made
احتياج (iḥtiyāj) – need
ماهية (māhiyya) – quiddity/essence
فاعل (fāʿil) – agent/efficient cause
جزء مقوم (juzʾ muqawwim) – constituent part
معتزلة (Muʿtazila) – the Mu'tazilites

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that denying quiddity is made implies it is independent of the agent.
Correction: The passage clarifies that no rational person holds quiddity independent, except the Mu'tazila in a specific sense.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'al-māhiyya majʿūla' according to the scholars who say it is made absolutely?
A) It means quiddity is created by an agent.
B) It means quiddity is dependent on a constituent part.
C) It means need is incidental to quiddity in a general sense.
D) It means quiddity is identical to its existence.

Key Takeaway
The claim that quiddity is 'made' means it is dependent either on an efficient cause or a constituent part, and no one (except the Mu'tazila) says it is independent of the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you truly recognize that you are dependent on Allah not only for your continued existence but also for your very essence? In what areas of your life do you subtly act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that your entire being is sustained by Him moment by moment? How would your daily decisions change if you lived with constant awareness of this total dependence?
?
The Mu'tazila thought that essences are fixed independently of Allah, limiting His role to adding existence. In what ways do you treat your own abilities, knowledge, or personality as if they are 'fixed' independently of Allah? What would it look like to fully internalize that your very nature is being made by Him at every instant?
?
This lesson teaches that even the need for a constituent part is a form of being-made. Have you ever considered that even the 'parts' of your character or circumstances are given by Allah? How can you cultivate gratitude for the very structure of your being, not just the external blessings?

The Critique of Specifying Majʿūliyya
Critique of specificity in investigating quiddity's attachables.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that limiting the investigation of quiddity's attachables to majʿūliyya is not beneficial.

Core Idea
The investigation of quiddity's attachables is general; singling out majʿūliyya lacks merit.

The commentator begins his critique by questioning the author's approach. He points out that the question of whether an attribute is a necessary concomitant of quiddity itself or of its existence is a broad inquiry that applies to many attributes. Therefore, focusing exclusively on majʿūliyya is not particularly useful.

Guided Path
1
Read the first critique
Read the passage carefully and note the commentator's opening statement that the exposition 'has distance'.
2
Identify the generality principle
The commentator says the investigation of what attaches to quiddity applies to many attachables. Understand that this is not limited to majʿūliyya.
Think of other attributes like universality, particularity, etc.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that there is not much benefit in specifying this investigation to majʿūliyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا تقرير ما حرره المصنف وفيه بعد ؛ لأن البحث عما يلحق الماهية أنه من لوازمها من حيث هي أو من لوازم وجودها الخارجي أو الذهني جارٍ في كثير من ،لواحقها، فليس لتخصيص هذا البحث بالمجعولية كثير فائدة وأيضاً كما أن الماهية الممكنة محتاجة إلى الفاعل في وجودها الخارجي، كذلك محتاجة إليه في وجودها ،الذهني، فالمجعولية بمعنى الاحتياج إلى الفاعل من لوازم الماهية الممكنة مطلقاً، فإنها أينما وجدت كانت متصفة بهذا الاحتياج سواء كان اتصافها به بيناً أو غير بين. وإن فسر المجعولية بأنها الاحتياج إلى الفاعل في الوجود الخارجي، كان الكلام صحيحاً، والتقييد تكلفاً، وأبعد أن معنى قولهم : الماهية غير مجعولة أن المجعولية ذلك ما قاله الإمام الرازي من من لیست نفس الماهية ولا داخلة فيها
This is an exposition of what the author wrote, but it has distance, because investigating what attaches to quiddity — whether it is from its necessary concomitants as such or from the concomitants of its external or mental existence — applies to many of its attachables, so there is not much benefit in specifying this investigation to being-made. Also, just as the possible quiddity needs the agent in its external existence, so too it needs Him in its mental existence. So being-made in the sense of need for the agent is a necessary attribute of the possible quiddity absolutely, for wherever it is found, it is qualified with this need, whether its qualification with it is evident or not. And if being-made is interpreted as the need for the agent in external existence, then the statement is correct, and the restriction is a forced one. And more remote is that the meaning of their saying 'the quiddity is not made' is that being-made is that which Imam al-Razi said: it is not the quiddity itself nor internal to it.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the commentator's critique of the author's exposition. The commentator begins by saying 'this exposition has distance' (i.e., it is not accurate). The first reason: the investigation of what attaches to quiddity — whether it is a necessary concomitant of quiddity as such or of its existence (external or mental) — is a general investigation that applies to many attachables of quiddity. Therefore, singling out the attribute of being-made (majʿūliyya) for this investigation is not particularly beneficial. The second reason: just as a possible quiddity needs an agent for its external existence, it also needs an agent for its mental existence. Thus, if being-made means need for the agent, then it is a necessary attribute of every possible quiddity absolutely, wherever it exists. Consequently, the statement that 'quiddity is not made' cannot mean that it is independent of the agent. Then the commentator considers an interpretation: if being-made is interpreted specifically as need for the agent in external existence, then the statement is correct, and the restriction is a forced one. Then it mentions a more remote interpretation attributed to Imam al-Razi: that the meaning of 'quiddity is not made' is that majʿūliyya is not the quiddity itself nor part of it. This is considered even more remote.

Key Terms
لازم (lāzim)
وجود ذهني (wujūd dhihnī)
وجود خارجي (wujūd khārijī)
لواحق (lawāḥiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the debate about quiddity's attachables is exclusively about majʿūliyya.
Correction: The commentator shows that this investigation applies to many attributes, so it is not exclusive to majʿūliyya.

Checkpoint
According to the critique, why is it not beneficial to specify the investigation of quiddity's attachables to majʿūliyya?
A) Because majʿūliyya is not a real attribute.
B) Because the investigation is about existence, not quiddity.
C) Because the investigation of what attaches to quiddity is general and applies to many attachables.
D) Because the author's exposition is completely false.

Key Takeaway
The investigation of quiddity's attachables is a general inquiry; limiting it to majʿūliyya is not beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own study of Islamic theology, do you ever fixate on a single concept—like causality or being-made—while neglecting the broader framework of quiddity and existence? How might this passage challenge you to step back and see the bigger picture?
?
The commentator criticizes the author's approach for lacking benefit. When you learn a new theological idea, do you ever ask yourself whether your focus is truly beneficial or merely habitual? Will you commit to regularly evaluating the priorities of your study?

Need for Agent in Mental Existence and Interpretations
Argument for need in mental existence and analysis of interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that possible quiddities need an agent in both external and mental existence, and how different interpretations of majʿūliyya affect this understanding.

Core Idea
Possible quiddities need an agent absolutely; majʿūliyya as need is a necessary concomitant.

The commentator extends his critique by pointing out that the need for an agent applies not only to external existence but also to mental existence. Therefore, if being-made means need for an agent, then it is a necessary attribute of all possible quiddities. He then considers interpretations of majʿūliyya and critiques one attributed to Imam al-Razi.

Guided Path
1
Read the second critique
Read the passage starting from 'Also, just as the possible quiddity...' and note the argument about mental existence.
2
Understand the dual need
The commentator asserts that quiddities need an agent in both external and mental existence. This makes majʿūliyya (as need) a necessary concomitant regardless of where the quiddity exists.
This broadens the scope beyond external existence.
3
Consider the interpretations
The passage presents two interpretations: (1) interpreting majʿūliyya as need only in external existence (which is correct but forced), and (2) the view of Imam al-Razi that majʿūliyya is not the quiddity itself nor part of it. The latter is considered more remote.

?

The Correct Meaning of 'Quiddity is Not Made'
States the correct meaning and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of the phrase 'quiddity is not made' and how it refutes an objection by al-Razi.

Core Idea
Quiddity in itself is not made, but being-made remains a necessary concomitant.

This passage resolves a common confusion about the relationship between quiddity (essence) and making (jaʿl). The correct meaning is that quiddity considered in itself is not an object of making or effecting. The commentator then shows how this interpretation refutes an argument by al-Razi, using an analogy from geometry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall meaning. Notice the key terms and the structure: first the correct meaning, then the refutation.
Pay attention to the triangle analogy at the end.
2
Understand the correct meaning
The passage states that 'quiddity is not made' means that in its own essence, no making or effecting attaches to it. The example of blackness helps illustrate this: when you consider blackness without considering a maker, you are not making it an object of making.
The distinction between considering quiddity alone and with a maker is crucial.
3
Grasp the refutation of al-Razi
Al-Razi had argued that if a quiddity can be imagined without being made, then being-made is not a necessary attribute. The commentator refutes this by showing that imagining without a property does not prove that property is not necessary—using the triangle example: imagining a triangle without equal angles does not prove that equality is not necessary for it.
This distinguishes between 'evident' (badīhī) and 'necessary' (lāzim).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصواب أن يقال : معنى قولهم الماهية ليست مجعولة أنها في حد أنفسها لا يتعلق بها جعل جاعل ولا تأثير مؤثر ، فإنك إذا لاحظت ماهية السواد ولم تلاحظ موجد بديهية أو نظرية، كما سيأتي . وفيه إشارة إلى الرد على ما ذكره المصنف بقوله: فلو تصور إنسان غير مجعول إلخ بأن اللازم منه أن لا تكون مجعوليته بينة الثبوت له، ولا يلزم منه أن لا تكون لازمة له، كما لا يلزم من تصور المثلث بدون تساوي الزوايا أن لا يكون المتساوي لازماً له
And the correct thing is to say: the meaning of their saying 'the quiddity is not made' is that in the limit of themselves, no making of a maker attaches to them nor effect of an effecter. For if you observe the quiddity of blackness and do not observe a maker, [it is] intuitive or discursive, as will come. And in it is an indication of the refutation of what the author mentioned in his saying: 'for if a person imagines something not made, etc.' in that the necessary consequence is that its being-made is not evidently established for it, and it does not follow from that that it is not necessary for it, just as it does not follow from imagining a triangle without the equality of angles that equality is not necessary for it.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage provides the correct interpretation of the statement 'quiddity is not made'. The correct meaning is that quiddity considered in itself is not an object of making or effecting. The example given is the quiddity of blackness: if you consider it without considering a maker, whether intuitively or discursively (as will be explained later), you understand that it is not made. Then the passage indicates that this is a refutation of an argument by the author (al-muṣannif). The author had argued that if one can imagine a quiddity without being made, that would show that being-made is not a necessary concomitant of quiddity. The commentator responds: the fact that one can imagine quiddity without being made only means that being-made is not evidently established for it (i.e., it is not a primary attribute), but it does not mean it is not a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of it. The analogy: one can imagine a triangle without the property that its angles are equal (tasāwī al-zawāyā, referring to the theorem about triangle angles summing to 180°, but here 'equality of angles' might refer to the equality of base angles in an isosceles triangle? The text says 'تساوي الزوايا' which generally means equality of angles, but in context it likely refers to a necessary property of a triangle, such as the sum of angles being equal to two right angles. The point: imagining the triangle without that property does not prove that the property is not necessary for the triangle. Similarly, imagining quiddity without being-made does not prove that being-made is not necessary for it.

Key Terms
جعل جاعل (jaʿl jāʿil) – making of a maker
تأثير مؤثر (taʾthīr muʾaththir) – effect of an effecter
بديهية (badīhiyya) – intuitive (self-evident)
نظرية (naẓariyya) – discursive (theoretical)
لازم (lāzim) – necessary concomitant

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a property is not self-evident (badīhī) when considering an essence, then it cannot be a necessary property of that essence.
Correction: The passage shows that a property can be necessary (lāzim) even if it is not self-evident. Imagining the essence without the property does not negate necessity, as shown by the triangle example.

Checkpoint
According to the correct interpretation, what does it mean to say 'quiddity is not made'?
A) It means quiddity has no maker.
B) It means quiddity in itself is not subject to making or effecting.
C) It means quiddity is identical to its existence.
D) It means quiddity is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The correct meaning of 'quiddity is not made' is that quiddity considered in itself is not an object of making, but this does not negate that being-made is a necessary concomitant, as illustrated by the triangle analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a necessary truth about your relationship with God—like your absolute dependence on Him—simply because it didn't feel obvious or intuitive? What one step will you take today to act on that truth even when you don't feel it?
?
The passage shows that what is necessary is not always self-evident. Consider a time you ignored a clear command of Allah because your heart felt indifferent. How will you remind yourself that obedience is necessary regardless of your feelings? Write down one specific action you will take this week to obey a truth you know but don't feel.
?
If you were to imagine your own existence without any trace of God's making, it would be a fantasy. Yet you often live as if you are self-sufficient. What one habit or thought pattern reveals that you forget being-made is a necessary attribute of everything you are? Commit to breaking that illusion today.

The Correct Meaning of 'Essence is Not Made'
Defines 'essence is not made' and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct meaning of the statement 'the essence is not made' and why the objection that it implies non-necessity is flawed.

Core Idea
Essences in themselves are not made, but that doesn't mean they are not necessarily made in reality.

What does it mean when philosophers say an essence (mahiyya) is not made? Does it mean essences are independent of God? This lesson clarifies the precise meaning of that statement and shows how an objection based on a misunderstanding is refuted using a simple analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the two-part structure: definition followed by refutation.
The passage is concise but dense. Read carefully.
2
Identify the correct definition
Find where the passage defines what 'essence is not made' means. It says 'in their own limits, no maker's making and no agent's effect pertains to them.' This is the key point.
Essences in themselves are not the direct objects of making.
3
Understand the refutation
The passage refutes an objection by showing that not being evidently made does not mean being necessarily made is false. The triangle analogy illustrates this: imagining a triangle without equal angles does not negate that equality is necessary for it in reality.
Distinguish between what is evident and what is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصواب أن يقال : معنى قولهم الماهية ليست مجعولة أنها في حد أنفسها لا يتعلق بها جعل جاعل ولا تأثير مؤثر ، فإنك إذا لاحظت ماهية السواد ولم تلاحظ موجد بديهية أو نظرية، كما سيأتي . وفيه إشارة إلى الرد على ما ذكره المصنف بقوله: فلو تصور إنسان غير مجعول إلخ بأن اللازم منه أن لا تكون مجعوليته بينة الثبوت له، ولا يلزم منه أن لا تكون لازمة له، كما لا يلزم من تصور المثلث بدون تساوي الزوايا أن لا يكون المتساوي لازماً له نفس الأمر.
And the correct view is to say: The meaning of their statement 'the essence is not made' is that in their own limits, no maker's making and no agent's effect pertains to them. For if you observe the essence of blackness without observing a creator, [it is] either self-evident or theoretical, as will come. And in it is an indication of the refutation of what the author mentioned in his statement: 'For if one imagines a human being as not made, etc.,' namely that what follows from it is that its being-made is not evident as established for it, and it does not follow from it that it is not necessary for it, just as it does not follow from imagining a triangle without equal angles that equality is not necessary for it in reality.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage provides the correct definition of the statement 'the essence is not made.' It begins by stating that essences in themselves are not subject to any maker's making or any agent's effect. Then it illustrates: if you consider the essence of blackness without considering a creator, the fact that it is not made is either self-evident or theoretical. The passage then refutes an objection from the author (al-Isfahani?), who said that if one imagines a human being as not made, then its being-made is not evident. The reply is that not being evident does not mean it is not necessary; just as imagining a triangle without equal angles does not mean that equality is not necessary for it in reality. Thus the objection fails.

C) It means the essence is a necessary existent.

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that essences in themselves are not subject to making, but this does not imply they are not necessarily made in reality.

Causality and Created Things

The Meaning of 'Essence is Not Made' as Negation of Composition
Clarifying the meaning of 'essence is not made' and the debate over its interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'essence is not made' means the essence itself is not the object of making, and why this is interpreted as a pure negation (salb) rather than a positive description.

Core Idea
Essences are not themselves made; they are not the direct product of creation.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a foundational principle. He explains that when scholars say 'the essence is made,' they simply mean it needs a creator. But the more precise statement 'essences are not made' refers to the negation of composition—it does not mean the essence never exists, but that the essence in itself is not the direct product of making. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the objections raised.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two meanings: 'essence is made' vs. 'essence is not made'.
2
Identify the interpretation of 'ab'ad'
The text says one interpretation is 'more distant' (ab'ad). Find why it is considered less direct and what objection is raised.
3
Understand the objection about redundancy
The objection states that this judgment is already known from another statement, so mentioning it is redundant. See how the text responds.
4
Grasp the negation (salb) interpretation
The passage concludes that 'not made' should be interpreted as negation (salb), not as a positive description. Understand why this is the preferred understanding.
This sets the stage for the rest of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أن معنى قولهم إلخ) يعني أن معنى قولهم : إنها مجعولة ظاهر، وهو الاحتياج إلى الموجد لا يحتاج إلى التعرض ومعنى قولهم الماهيات غير مجعولة أنها ليست نفسها ولا جزءها، وإنما كان أبعد لاشتراكه مع ما قاله المصنف في أنه ليس للتخصيص كثير فائدة، يرد عليه أن هذا الحكم قد علم من قولهم : وهي مغايرة لما عداها بأبلغ بيان فالتعرض له مستدرك ولأنه لا وجه حينئذ لمذهب التفصيل. وما قيل من أنه على هذا ينبغي أن يحمل قولهم : غير مجعولة على السلب ففيه أنه على جميع الوجوه المذكورة محمولة على السلب كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( ولا تأثير (مؤثر) أشار بالعطف إلى أن النزاع ليس في الجعل اللغوي، فإنه يستعمل بمعنى الخلق والصيرورة والتصيير، ومعنى طفق . قوله : (إن المجعولية ليست نفس الماهية الخ) فقولهم الماهية غير مجعولة ينبغي أن يحمل حينئذ على السلب لا العدول كما هو ظاهر العبارة؛ لأن الماهية من حيث هي ليست غير معها مفهوماً سواها، لم يعقل هناك جعل، إذ لا مغايرة بين الماهية ونفسها حتى يتصور توسط جعل جاعل بينهما، فتكون إحداهما مجعولة تلك الأخرى. وكذا لا يتصور تأثير الفاعل في الوجود بمعني جعل الوجود وجوداً ، بل تأثيره في الماهية باعتبار الوجود، بمعنى أنه يجعلها متصفة بالوجود، لا بمعنى أنه يجعل اتصافها موجوداً متحققاً في الخارج، فإن الصباغ مثلاً إذا صبغ ثوباً، فإنه لا يجعل الثوب ثوباً، ولا الصبغ صبغا، بل يجعل الثوب متصفا بالصبغ في الخارج وإن لم يجعل اتصافه به موجوداً ثابتاً في الخارج، فليست الماهيات في أنفسها مجعولةً، ولا وجوداتها أيضاً في أنفسها مجعولة، بل الماهيات في كونها موجودة مجعولة وهذا المعنى مما لا قوله : (إذ لا مغايرة إلخ) فيه بحث؛ لأن هذا إنما يفيد عدم تعلق الجعل بالسواد بمعنى جعل شيء شيئاً؛ ولا يفيد نفي تعلق الجعل به بأن يكون نفسه أثر الفاعل وتابعاً للجعل، ومعنى التأثير استتباع المؤثر الأثر، لا ما يتبادر إلى الوهم، أعني إيجاد الأثر. قوله : ( وكذا إلخ ) هذه المقدمة لا دخل لها في بيان أنها ليست بمجعولة، بل توطئة لبيان معنى الجعل، ودفع لما مر من أنه إذا لم تكن ماهية ما مجعولة انتفى المجعولية بالكلية؛ لأن كل ما يفرض تعلق الجعل به من الوجود والموصوفية فهو ماهية في نفسه قوله: (بمعنى جعل الوجود وجوداً) وكذا في الاتصاف بمعنى جعل الاتصاف اتصافاً. قوله : (بل تأثيره إلخ) فالأثر هي الماهية باعتبار الوجود فيتصور توسط الجعل بينهما، بأن يقال : جعل الماهية موجودة، وليس الأثر الاتصاف حتى يرد أنكم قد اعترفتم يكون الاتصاف أثر الفاعل بنفسه، فلم لا تقولون الماهيات كلها كذلك ؟ وأن الأثر هو الأمر الخارجي، والاتصاف ليس كذلك. قوله: (لا) بمعنى أنه يجعل إلخ) فإن الاتصاف إنما يكون موجوداً إذا كان الخارج ظرفاً لوجوده، وفيما نحن فيه الخارج ظرف لنفسه قوله : ( فإن الصباغ إلخ ) تصوير للمعقول بالمحسوس لإيضاحه . قوله : ( وهذا المعنى إلخ فيه بحث؛ لأن ما ذكره إنما يصح إذا كان الاتصاف بالوجود حقيقياً بأن يكون الوجود أمراً زائداً على الماهية تتصف الماهية به سواء كان الوجود موجوداً بنفسه أو معدوماً. وقد عرفت بطلانه بناء على ما هو المشهور من أن ثبوت شيء لشيء فرع لثبوت المثبت له، إلا أن يقال باستثناء الوجود عنه، كما ذهب إليه الإمام، أو يقال بالاستلزام دون الفرعية، كما ذهب إليه المحقق الدواني. أما إذا كان انتزاعياً محضاً؛ ولا يكون في الخارج إلا الماهية ، فلا معنى لقوله بأنه يجعلها متصفة بالوجود. مجعولة أيضاً، على معنى أن اللامجعولية ليست نفسها ولا داخلة فيها. ووجه الأبعدية مع استوائهما في انتفاء وجه تخصيص هذا البحث بالمجعولية؛ أنه على هذا كان معلوماً في أول بحث الماهية، فلا وجه لذكره ثانياً كما هو دأبهم .
His statement: 'That the meaning of their statement, etc.' means that the meaning of their statement 'it is made' is apparent, namely the need for the creator, which does not need to be addressed. And the meaning of their statement 'essences are not made' is that they are not themselves nor a part of it [i.e., the being-made]. And it was only more distant (ab'ad) because it shares with what the author said in that there is not much benefit in specification. It is objected to him that this judgment has been learned from their statement 'and they are different from what is other than them' with the most eloquent expression, so addressing it is redundant, and because there is then no scope for the doctrine of detail (madhhab al-tafsil). And what is said that on this basis their statement 'not made' should be interpreted as negation (salb), then it is that in all the mentioned aspects it is interpreted as negation, as is not hidden. His statement: 'And no effect of an agent' — he indicated by the conjunction that the dispute is not about linguistic making (al-ja'l al-lughawi), for it is used in the meaning of creation, making, and rendering, and the meaning of 'tafiqa' (began). His statement: 'Indeed, being-made is not the essence itself, etc.' — so their statement 'the essence is not made' should then be interpreted as negation, not as the more common (al-'udul), as is apparent from the expression; because the essence, inasmuch as it is, is not along with it a concept other than it; no making is conceived there, since there is no distinction between the essence and itself such that a maker's making could be imagined between them, so that one of them would be made of the other. Likewise, the agent's effect is not conceived in existence in the sense of making existence an existence; rather his effect is on the essence with respect to existence, in the sense that he makes it qualified by existence, not in the sense that he makes its qualification existent and realized in the external. For example, if a dyer dyes a garment, he does not make the garment a garment, nor the dye dye, but he makes the garment qualified by the dye in the external, even if he does not make its qualification by it existent and fixed in the external. So essences in themselves are not made, nor are their existences in themselves made; rather essences in their being existent are made. And this meaning is something which... His statement: 'Since there is no distinction, etc.' — there is discussion in it; because this only proves the non-attachment of making to blackness in the sense of making one thing another; but it does not prove the negation of the attachment of making to it by its being itself the effect of the agent and subsequent to the making. And the meaning of effect (al-ta'thir) is the agent's bringing about of the effect, not what comes to mind, namely the bringing into existence of the effect. His statement: 'And likewise, etc.' — this premise has no role in explaining that it is not made; rather it is a preparation for explaining the meaning of making, and for repelling what has passed that if a certain essence is not made, then being-made is negated entirely; because everything to which making is supposed to attach, from existence and being-qualified, is itself an essence. His statement: 'In the sense of making existence an existence' — and likewise in qualification, in the sense of making qualification a qualification. His statement: 'Rather his effect, etc.' — so the effect is the essence with respect to existence, so the intermediation of making between them is conceived, such that it is said: He made the essence existent. And the effect is not the qualification, so that it might be objected that you have admitted that the qualification is the effect of the agent itself, so why do you not say that all essences are likewise? And that the effect is the external thing, and the qualification is not so. His statement: 'No, in the sense that he makes, etc.' — for the qualification is only existent when the external is the locus of its existence, and in our case the external is the locus of itself. His statement: 'For the dyer, etc.' — is a depiction of the intelligible by the sensible to clarify it. His statement: 'And this meaning, etc.' — there is discussion in it; because what he mentioned is only valid if the qualification by existence is real (haqiqi), such that existence is something additional to the essence, the essence is qualified by it, whether existence is existent by itself or non-existent. And you have known its falsehood based on what is well-known that the subsistence of a thing for a thing is branch of the subsistence of the subject of attribution, unless it is said that existence is excepted from this, as the Imam held, or it is said by way of implication (al-istilzam) without branch-subordination, as al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani held. But if it is purely derivative (intiza'i), and there is nothing in the external except the essence, then there is no meaning to his saying that he makes it qualified by existence. Also, it is not made, in the sense that the non-being-made (al-lamaj'uliyya) is not itself nor part of it. And the reason for its being more distant (al-ab'adiyya), while they are equal in the negation of a specific aspect for this investigation on being-made, is that on this it would have been known at the beginning of the investigation of essence, so there is no reason to mention it a second time, as is their custom.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This lengthy passage delves into the precise meaning of 'the essence is not made.' It begins by clarifying that the meaning of 'the essence is made' is simply the need for a creator, while 'not made' means that the essence itself is not the act of making nor a part of it. The claim that this interpretation is 'more distant' (ab'ad) is examined and objected to. The passage then discusses that the dispute is not about linguistic making but about ontological making. It argues that since there is no distinction between the essence and itself, no making can be conceived that would make the essence itself. The agent's effect is on the essence with respect to existence: he makes the essence existent, not the existence itself. The analogy of the dyer illustrates this: the dyer makes the garment dyed, not the garment or the dye. Therefore, essences in themselves are not made, nor are their existences; rather what is made is the essence in its being existent. Subsequent objections are addressed: the objection that if essences are not made, then nothing is made, is answered by stating that the effect is the essence with respect to existence, and making is conceived between them. The passage concludes with a discussion on the nature of existence and qualification, noting that if existence is purely derivative (intiza'i), then the statement that the agent makes the essence qualified by existence has no meaning.

Key Terms
الماهية (essence)
مجعولة (made)
سلب (negation)
أبعد (more distant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'essence is not made' means the essence never exists or is not created at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it means the essence in itself is not the direct object of making; the essence in its existence is made. The negation is of composition, not of existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'essences are not made' (الماهيات غير مجعولة) mean?
A) Essences do not exist.
B) Essences are not themselves nor a part of the making.
C) Essences are created but without any composition.
D) Essences are made only in their essence, not in existence.

Key Takeaway
When scholars say 'the essence is not made,' they mean the essence itself is not the direct object of making; it is a negation of composition, not of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own essence is not 'made' in the sense of being composed, do you ever act as if your identity is something you have assembled yourself? How does recognizing that your very self is a direct creation of Allah—not a product of your own making—change the way you view your strengths and weaknesses?
?
The passage emphasizes that 'essence is made' means need for a creator. When you attribute success in your life to your own efforts, do you pause to acknowledge that the essence of those efforts itself is dependent on Allah? What one habit can you adopt to make this reliance more present in your daily thoughts?
?
The objection in the passage questions whether clarifying these meanings is redundant. Reflect: Are there areas of your faith where you assume the meaning is obvious and skip the depth? What would happen if you stopped and specified exactly what you mean when you say 'I depend on Allah'?
