قوله : ( معلل بالصورة (الحالة قال المحقق في شرح الإشارات الهيولى إنما تصير هذه الهيولى بعينها لأجل صورة تعينها لا من حيث إنها هذه الصورة بل من حيث إنها صورة ما . وتفصيله ما قاله الإمام في المباحث المشرقية المؤثر في وجود الهيولى المعينة هو وجود المفارق وهو شيء معين الذات مثل تعين ذات الهيولى المعينة وأما الصورة فإنها كما عرفت شرائط لوصول تأثير المفارق والحاجة إلى الصورة ليست من حيث هي تلك الصورة، بل من حيث إنها صورة ما . والمعلول المعين الشخصي وإن كان يستدعي علة معينة شخصية، ولكن لا يستدعي أن تكون شرائط التأثير أموراً بأعيانها. انتهى. وبما نقلنا ظهر أن الصورة المطلقة شريكة فاعل الهيولى المعينة وأنها معتبرة في جانب الفاعل وليست مخصصة للهيولى يتعين دون آخر لأن الصورة المطلقة لا دخل لها في التخصيص، وكلامنا في العلة المخصصة، بل مخصصها بتعين دون آخر ذاتها وأن هيولى كل فلك وهيولى العناصر نوعها منحصر في فرد فاندفع إيراد بعض الفضلاء بالنظر إلى نفس الهيولى وأما بالنظر إلى تعدد أشخاصها باعتبار تبعضها، وحصصها باعتبار أشخاص الأجسام العنصرية وباعتبار أشخاص خوارج المراكز والتداوير والكواكب، فيسيجيء أنه باعتبار العوارض المكتنفة بها، وكذا اندفع ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره بقوله : ومن هاهنا يظهر جواز إلخ لأن علية الصورة من حيث ذاتها لا من حيث حلولها، ولأنها ليست مخصصة والكلام في المخصص.
العناصر
شخصاً، وقد يقال مرادهم باتحاد هيولى العناصر شخصاً أنه شخص واحد لا انفصال في ذاته وإنما هو من خارج وبسببه يصير أشخاصاً متعددة وربما يدعى أن مرادهم بالاتحاد الاتحاد النوعي وزيادة التشخص تصرف من الناقل بمقتضى فهمه يدل عليه تصريحهم بتعدد أشخاصها بسبب القرب والبعد من الفلك، كما مر آنفاً .
قوله : (بل تعينه عندهم بصورته فيه بحث؛ لأن هذا مخالف للمشهور، وسيأتي أيضاً في موقف الجوهر وهو أن الهيولى محتاجة إلى الصورة في بقائها، والصورة محتاجة إليها في تعينها، وقد يجاب : بأن لا تنافي بين الاحتياجين فيجوز أن يكون احتياج الهيولى إلى الصورة في البقاء
والتشخص معاً، ولا محذور في احتياج كل منهما إلى ذات الأخرى في التشخص، كما صرح الإمام في شرح الإشارات. قيل : والتحقيق أن تشخص الصورة بكون الهيولي المعينة من حيث قابلة لتشخصها، وتشخص الهيولى بالصورة المطلقة من حيث هي فاعلة لتشخصها، ونحن نقول : سيشير الشارح إلى بطلان هذا التحقيق في موقف الجوهر حيث قال: قلنا : الواحد
هي
His saying: (i.e., it is, etc.) i.e., by something from the necessary and its concomitant. As for the first, it is due to his saying: 'rather its determination' etc. As for the second, because their doctrine is that the elemental individuals share in hayula, and that the individuals of the partial spheres from the centers, epicycles, and planets share in the hayula of the universal sphere. The pronoun in 'bi-hi' is not referred only to the concomitant to avoid repulsion from it, nor only to the necessary to avoid needing to mention the concomitant afterwards. His saying: (i.e., its determination being caused by its essence and its restriction to one individual) is a reference to the fact that his intention is not to assert the combination, as is apparent from his expression, but not asserting it is by virtue of not asserting the part of the first, as indicated by his saying: 'rather its determination according to them is by its form', so it does not contradict what is well-known from them of saying the unity of the hayula of the elements. Second Observation - Twelfth Topic: Determination if it is caused by the essence. One individual, rather its determination according to them is by its form; because the individuation of hayula is caused according to them by the form inhering in it, not by the essence of hayula. And from here appears the permissibility of the individuation of an essence by what inheres in it, and they built their argument on the impermissibility of it) And if the individuation of the recipient by what inheres in it were necessary... It would suffice then to say: If its individuation is by its essence, they do not say it, so understand, for it is a place where feet slip. His saying: (caused by the form (inhering) al-Muhaqqiq said in Sharh al-Isharat: Hayula only becomes this particular hayula because of a form determining it, not in that it is this form, but in that it is a form. Its detail is what the Imam said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: The effective cause in the existence of the determined hayula is the existence of the separate being, and it is something determined in essence like the determination of the essence of the determined hayula. As for the form, it is, as you have known, conditions for the arrival of the influence of the separate being, and the need for a form is not in it being that particular form, but in it being a form. And the determined personal effect, even if it requires a determined personal cause, does not require that the conditions of influence be specific things. End. And from what we have transmitted, it appears that the absolute form is a partner of the agent of the determined hayula, and it is considered on the side of the agent, and it is not a specifier of hayula to become determined rather than another, because the absolute form has no role in specification. Our discussion is about the specifying cause. Rather, its specifier to determination rather than another is its essence, and that the hayula of each sphere and the hayula of the elements have their species confined to one individual. Thus the objection of some virtuous one with respect to the hayula itself is repelled. As for with respect to the multiplicity of its individuals through its division and its shares through the individuals of elemental bodies and through the individuals of the centers, epicycles, and planets, it will come that it is through the surrounding accidents. Similarly, what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned by his saying: 'And from here appears the permissibility, etc.' is repelled, because the causality of the form is from its essence, not from its inherence, and because it is not a specifier, and the discussion is about the specifier. The elements. An individual, and it might be said that their intention by the unity of the hayula of the elements as an individual is that it is one individual with no separation in its essence, and it is only from outside and by it it becomes multiple individuals. Perhaps it is claimed that their intention by unity is specific unity, and the addition of individuation is an interpretation of the transmitter according to his understanding. This is indicated by their explicit statement of the multiplicity of its individuals due to nearness and distance from the sphere, as has passed recently. His saying: (rather its determination according to them is by its form) contains discussion; because this contradicts the famous, and it will also come in the station of substance: namely that hayula is in need of form for its survival, and form is in need of it for its determination. It might be answered: There is no contradiction between the two needs, so it is possible that hayula's need of form is for survival and individuation together, and there is no problem in each of them needing the other's essence for individuation, as the Imam stated in Sharh al-Isharat. It was said: The verification is that the individuation of the form is by the determined hayula being receptive to its individuation, and the individuation of hayula is by the absolute form as it is an agent for its individuation. And we say: The commentator will indicate the falsity of this verification in the station of substance where he said: We said: the one is...
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the determination (ta'ayyun) of the recipient (qabil) and the role of form (surah) in individuating hayula (prime matter). It begins by explaining a prior statement: the determination of an essence with multiple individuals is caused either by something from the necessary (malzum) or its concomitant (lazim). The commentator clarifies that the philosophers do not hold that the determination of the recipient is by its essence, because they believe hayula is determined by the form inhering in it. The passage then cites al-Muhaqqiq (Nasir al-Din al-Tusi) and Imam al-Razi to argue that hayula becomes this particular hayula not because of the specific form, but because of form in general, which serves as a condition for the influence of the separate being (al-mufariq). The absolute form is not a specifier (mukhasis); the true specifier is the separate being's essence or the essence of hayula itself. The refutation of an objection by 'some virtuous one' is presented, showing that the form is a partner in the agent side and not a specifier. The passage also addresses the unity of the hayula of the elements, discussing whether it is numerically one or specifically one. Finally, a caution is given against a famous view that form is determined by hayula and vice versa, with a promise of further discussion in the station of substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the role of the absolute form in the individuation of hayula?
A) It is the specifying cause (mukhasis).
B) It is a condition for the influence of the separate being.
C) It is the effect of hayula's essence.
D) It is irrelevant to individuation.

Key Takeaway
The form (surah) is a condition for the influence of the separate being on hayula, not the specifying cause of hayula's individuation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the workings of the universe, do you habitually attribute effects to their immediate physical forms (e.g., fire burning), forgetting that the true effective cause is the separate being (God)? How can you train yourself to see forms as mere conditions and direct your gratitude to the true Agent?
?
This lesson teaches that form is not a specifier of your worth; your essence (soul) is what gives you identity. In what areas of your life have you allowed external forms—like appearance, wealth, or status—to define who you are? What concrete step can you take today to realign your self-perception with the truth that your essence is the specifier?
?
The lesson emphasizes that the form's role is generic, not specific. In your worship, do you sometimes focus on the outward form (e.g., exact recitation) to the neglect of the inner condition (sincerity)? How can you make your heart the true specifier of your deeds, ensuring that the form remains a condition and not an end in itself?

7 min
Unity of Hayula and Mutual Individuation
Commentator discusses the unity of the hayula of the elements and the mutual individuation of form and hayula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether the hayula of the elements is numerically one or specifically one, and the mutual need between form and hayula for individuation.

Core Idea
Hayula's unity is debated; form and hayula mutually need each other for individuation.

This lesson addresses two remaining issues: the nature of the unity of the hayula of the elements (whether it is one in number or species) and the mutual need between form and hayula for individuation. The passage discusses different interpretations and hints at a future refutation of a certain verification.

Guided Path
1
Read the discussion on unity
The passage presents the view that the hayula of the elements is one individual with no internal separation, and that multiplicity arises from external causes. Alternatively, some say it is specific unity.
The passage notes that the philosophers explicitly mention multiplicity due to distance from the sphere.
2
Understand the objection
A famous view holds that form is determined by hayula and hayula by form, implying mutual individuation. The passage suggests this might be acceptable but will be refuted later.
Imam al-Razi in Sharh al-Isharat allows mutual need.
3
Note the upcoming refutation
The commentator promises to show the falsity of the verification that form individuates hayula and hayula individuates form, in the station of substance.
This points to a later discussion.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the determination (ta'ayyun) of the recipient (qabil) and the role of form (surah) in individuating hayula (prime matter). It begins by explaining a prior statement: the determination of an essence with multiple individuals is caused either by something from the necessary (malzum) or its concomitant (lazim). The commentator clarifies that the philosophers do not hold that the determination of the recipient is by its essence, because they believe hayula is determined by the form inhering in it. The passage then cites al-Muhaqqiq (Nasir al-Din al-Tusi) and Imam al-Razi to argue that hayula becomes this particular hayula not because of the specific form, but because of form in general, which serves as a condition for the influence of the separate being (al-mufariq). The absolute form is not a specifier (mukhasis); the true specifier is the separate being's essence or the essence of hayula itself. The refutation of an objection by 'some virtuous one' is presented, showing that the form is a partner in the agent side and not a specifier. The passage also addresses the unity of the hayula of the elements, discussing whether it is numerically one or specifically one. Finally, a caution is given against a famous view that form is determined by hayula and vice versa, with a promise of further discussion in the station of substance.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The unity of the hayula of the elements must be numerical unity.
Correction: The passage shows that the philosophers may have intended specific unity, as multiplicity is caused by external factors.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one interpretation of the philosophers' statement about the unity of the hayula of the elements?
A) It is numerically one individual with no internal separation.
B) It is specific unity (the same species).
C) It is an aggregate of multiple individuals.
D) It is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The unity of the hayula of the elements is interpreted in two ways: numerically one or specifically one, and the mutual need between form and hayula for individuation is acknowledged but will be refuted later.

Causality and Created Things
advanced
12 min
Infinite regress argument against determination by a receiver
Refuting infinite regress from determinator of matter being another receiver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that individuation of matter is through another receiver leads to an infinite regress, and how the argument distinguishes between individual and specializational individuation.

Core Idea
If matter's individuation required another receiver, an infinite chain of receivers would ensue, which is impossible.

This passage presents a critical argument in kalam against the idea that the individuation of a material substance (matter) is determined by another receiver. The author shows that such a view leads to either circularity or an infinite regress, both of which are rationally unacceptable. He then addresses a subtle objection and provides a detailed answer distinguishing between two types of individuation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get an overview of the argument. Notice the technical terms like تسلسل (infinite regress) and دور (circularity).
The passage is dense; you may need to read it twice.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage enumerates three ways a receiver could be individuated: by its essence, by what inheres in it, or by another receiver. Note the consequences: confinement, circularity, or infinite regress.
These correspond to the three branches of the argument.
3
Understand the objection and response
An objection claims that determination can occur through successive accidents (preparednesses) without regress. The response shows this does not solve the problem, because the accidents themselves require explanation.
The objection is considered but rejected.
4
Grasp the detailed answer
The answer distinguishes between individual individuation (by essence) and specializational individuation (by accidents from the agent or prior preparednesses). This clarifies the scope of the argument.
This is the key to resolving the confusion.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself why the infinite regress argument is considered decisive in kalam. How does it protect the principle of sufficient reason?
Consider the implications for understanding matter and form.

The circularity you claimed, and if its individuation were through another receiver, it would necessitate infinite regress; because we transfer the discussion to the individuation of that other receiver. The result is that if your proof were sound that the multiplicity of individuals of a specific essence is only due to its receiver, then infinite regress of receivers would be necessary, and the composition of one body from them is absurd. (The answer) to the objection of some virtuous people (that its determination, i.e., the determination of the receiver, is caused by accidents that attach to it through successive preparednesses to infinity) such that each previous preparedness prepares for the next, and these preparednesses are not simultaneous but successive, and such regress is permissible according to them, (does not avail) as the predicate of 'the answer'. And we say that it does not avail because when they allowed its determination (i.e., the determination of the receiver) by what inheres in it, for the reference of what they mentioned is that the cause of the individuation of the receiver are things inhering in it prior to that individuation, and concomitant with another individuation caused by other things prior to the other individuation, and so on. His statement: (Because we transfer the discussion etc.) by saying: If its individuation is by its essence, confinement is necessary, and that entails confinement of the first receiver, which entails confinement of the essence to one individual. If by what inheres in it, circularity is necessary. If by another receiver, we transfer the discussion, and so on. His statement: (That its determination etc.) is a detailed answer: If the mentioned disjunction concerns its individual individuation, we choose that the cause of its individuation is its own essence and it is confined to one individual as you know its detail. If it concerns its specializational individuation occurring within individuals of elemental bodies and individuals of partial celestial spheres, we say: The particularizer of that individuation is accidents that attach to that receiver, either from the side of the agent only as in individuals of partial celestial spheres, as will come in the celestial matters, or by virtue of prior accidents that serve as preparations for the attachment of these accidents, necessitating the specialization of the receiver and the individuation of its portions. Those accidents are not individuators of the essence of the receiver, rather it is individuated by its essence as you know. And then the answer of the author is repelled, because you allowed the individuation of matter by accidents inhering in it, so allow that for the essence; for here it is not individuation of the receiver by what inheres in it, but rather the individuation of its parts by what inheres in itself. So ponder, for this station is among the subtleties.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the claim that the individuation of matter (or any receiver) is through another receiver. The opponent's claim leads to circularity or infinite regress. The commentator explains: if individuation is by essence, the essence is confined to one individual; if by what inheres, circularity results; if by another receiver, we regress infinitely, which is impossible. The response to an objection: the objector says that determination can be through successive accidents (preparednesses) that are not simultaneous but successive, and such regress is permissible. The commentator counters that this does not help because these accidents themselves would need to be explained, leading to regress. Then a detailed answer distinguishes between individual individuation (which is by essence and confined) and specializational individuation (which occurs within individuals of elemental bodies and celestial spheres). The latter is determined by accidents from the agent or preceding accidents that prepare, but these accidents do not individuate the essence itself; the essence is already individuated by itself. Thus the author's point that if you allow individuation of matter by accidents, you should allow it for essence, is repelled, because here we are dealing with parts of the receiver, not the receiver itself.

Critique of the Cause of Individuation
A critical note on the efficient cause of individuation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the efficient cause of individuation must be a single concrete individual, and why neither absolute form nor specific form can serve as that cause.

Core Idea
The cause of individuation must be one individual, not a universal form.

In this short but dense passage, the commentator takes issue with a proposed explanation of individuation. He argues that the efficient cause must be a single concrete individual, and that neither an absolute form nor a specific form can serve as that cause. He then invokes Ibn Sina's insight to ground the matter's unity and notes that the author's phrase avoids circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow: a statement, two rejections, a reference to Ibn Sina, and a concluding remark.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the rejection of absolute form
The commentator states that the efficient cause of individuation must be one individual. Absolute form (a universal) is not one individual, so it cannot be the cause.
This is a key point about causality: causes must be concrete particulars.
3
Understand the rejection of specific form
If one says the cause is a specific form, that also fails because a succession of specific forms would not preserve the matter's individuation. Ibn Sina held that matter's unity is preserved by the specific matter of the form, not by personal unity.
This avoids the problem of impossible succession (tawārud al-ṣuwar).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالشخص لا بد أن تكون علته الفاعلية واحدة بالشخص، والصورة المطلقة ليست كذلك. فحينئذ يشكل كلام المصنف هاهنا لأن علة تشخص الهيولى لا يجوز أن تكون صورة مطلقة فتعين أن تكون صورة معينة، وهو أيضاً باطل؛ إذ لا شك أن توارد الصور الشخصية لا يبطل تشخص الهيولى، كيف وقد صرح الشيخ الرئيس أن الوحدة الشخصية للمادة مستحفظة بالمادة النوعية للصورة لا بالوحدة الشخصية فيلزم التوارد المستحيل فتأمل. قوله: (ومن هاهنا يظهر إلخ) نقل عنه رحمة الله: فيه إشارة إلى أنه ليس الدور في الواقع ولعل وجهه ما أشرنا إليه سابقاً.
By individual, the efficient cause must be one individual, and the absolute form is not so. Therefore the author's speech here is problematic, because the cause of individuation of matter cannot be an absolute form, so it must be a specific form, and that is also false; for there is no doubt that the succession of specific forms does not nullify the individuation of matter, how so when the Chief Master stated that the personal unity of matter is preserved by the specific matter of the form, not by personal unity, so impossible succession would follow, so ponder. His statement: (And from here appears etc.) transmitted from him, may Allah have mercy on him: It contains an indication that there is no circularity in reality, and perhaps its aspect is what we indicated earlier.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This short passage is a critical note. It argues that the efficient cause of individuation must be one individual (i.e., a specific entity), but the absolute form is not one individual; thus it cannot be the cause. If one says it must be a specific form, that is also false because a succession of specific forms would not preserve the matter's individuation; Ibn Sina (the Chief Master) stated that the personal unity of matter is preserved by the specific matter of the form (i.e., the form's specific type), not by personal unity, otherwise impossible succession (of forms in one matter) would result. The commentator then remarks that the author's phrase 'and from here appears' indicates that there is no actual circularity, possibly referring to an earlier point.

Objection to Infinite Regress and the Response by the Separate Cause
Objection and answer regarding infinite regress in determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite regress arises in determination by essence and how the separate cause (al-mubayin) with a specific relation stops the regress.

Core Idea
Infinite regress in determination is avoided by a separate cause with a specific relation.

This passage presents a classic philosophical objection and its response concerning the determination of individuals. The objector claims that if determination is caused by the essence, it leads to an infinite regress of accidental attributes, making receptive matter unnecessary. The response introduces the separate cause (al-mubayin) that individuates through a specific relation, thereby stopping the regress and explaining multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its two parts
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that the passage has two clear sections: an objection starting with 'فلم' and a response beginning with 'هذا وقد يجاب'.
The objection uses a rhetorical question to challenge the need for receptive matter.
2
Analyze the objection: why infinite regress?
The objector argues that if determination is caused by the essence, then the accidental attributes (which determine the essence) would themselves require determination, leading to an infinite chain. Therefore, receptive matter is unnecessary.
The key point is that the essence alone cannot stop the regress.
3
Understand the response: the separate cause stops the regress
The response grants the logical possibility of succession but introduces the separate cause (al-mubayin) that has a specific relation to each individual. This relation directly produces individuation, bypassing the need for an infinite chain. Multiplicity arises from multiple separate agents.
The separate cause is external to the essence and independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الثاني عشر : التعين إن علل بالماهية لا نهاية له اتجه لنا أن نقول : (فلم) لا يجوز تعين الماهيات بصفاتها العارضة لها كذلك ) أي على سبيل التعاقب إلى ما لا يتناهى فلا حاجة حينئذ في تعدد أفراد الماهية النوعية إلى القابل والمادة. هذا وقد يجاب عن أصل الدليل أيضا بجواز أن يكون للمباين نسبة مخصوصة بها تقتضي تشخصاً معيناً، وإذا تعدد الفاعل المباين تعدد أفراد الماهية أيضاً.
The Second Observatory - The Twelfth Aim: Determination - If it is caused by the essence, it has no end. It follows for us to say: "Why is it not permissible (for the) determination of essences by their accidental attributes to be likewise?" i.e., on the basis of succession to infinity. Then there would be no need in the multiplicity of individuals of the specific essence for the receptive matter. And this has been answered from the original proof also by the permissibility that the separate cause (al-mubayin) has a specific relation which necessitates a specific individuation, and if the separate agent is multiple, the individuals of the essence become multiple.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer pair. The objector argues that if the determination of individuals is caused by the essence (mahiyya), then there would be an infinite regress, because the essence is shared and the accidental attributes would cause determination in an endless succession. Therefore, there is no need for a receptive matter (qabil) to explain multiplicity. The answer (the response) grants this but introduces the notion of a separate cause (al-mubayin) that has a specific relation to each individual, thus producing a particular individuation (tashakhkhush) directly. If the separate agents are multiple, the individuals of the essence become multiple without requiring receptive matter. This shows that infinite regress is avoided by appealing to the independent efficient cause (al-fa'il al-mubayin) and its specific relation.

Proof That Determination is Not Existential
Proof by disjunction that determination is not existential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument demonstrating that determination cannot be an existential attribute, and why the proof only applies to individuals that share a kind.

Core Idea
If determination were existential, it would need a cause—and every possible cause leads to absurdity.

In this passage, the author advances a sophisticated proof that determination (al-ta'yin) is not an existential attribute. By assuming the opposite and tracing the necessary causal consequences, the argument shows that all potential explanations collapse into contradictions. This lesson will walk you through each step of the proof and clarify its scope.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim and its context
Read the passage to grasp the main claim: that determination is not existential. Notice that the argument is presented as a proof by contradiction, assuming determination is existential and then deriving impossible consequences.
The proof only applies to determinations where multiple individuals share a kind.
2
Follow the disjunction of possible causes
If determination were existential, it would require a cause. The passage considers the cause could be the essence (mahiyya) or the receptive matter (qabil). Examine each branch carefully.
The essence as cause leads to confinement of the kind to one individual.
3
Analyze the regress and circularity options
If the cause is the receptive matter, its own determination must be explained. Three possibilities arise: by its essence (confinement), by another receptive matter (infinite regress), or by the received form (circularity). Each is absurd.
Understand terms: qabil (receptive matter), maqbul (received form), tasalsul (regress), dawr (circularity).
4
Conclude and note the scope limitation
Since all possibilities are invalid, the premise that determination is existential must be false. However, this only refutes existential determination for individuals sharing a kind, not for unique individuals.
This nuance is important to avoid overgeneralizing the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من جعل هذا الاعتراض ( دليلاً على أن التعين ليس وجودياً) فقال : لو كان تعين الشخص الذي له ما يشاركه في نوعه وجودياً لكان له علة، فعلته إن كانت الماهية انحصر نوعها في شخصها، وإن كانت القابل، فتعين القابل إن كان بماهيته انحصر نوعه في شخص، وإن كان بقابل آخر لزم التسلسل، وإن كان بالمقبول لزم الدور، والكل باطل ولا يجوز أن تكون العلة أمراً مبايناً، فلا يكون التعين أمراً وجودياً.
And among them are those who made this objection a proof that determination is not existential, saying: If the determination of an individual that has what shares its kind were existential, it would have a cause. If its cause is the essence, then its kind would be confined to that individual; if it is the receptive matter, then the determination of the receptive matter, if it is by its essence, its kind would be confined to its individual; if by another receptive matter, an infinite regress would follow; if by the received form, circularity would follow. All are invalid. And it is not permissible that the cause be a separate entity. Therefore determination is not existential.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) that determination (al-ta'yin) is not an existential attribute (wujudi). The argument proceeds by disjunction: suppose determination is wujudi, then it must have a cause. That cause can either be the essence (mahiyya), in which case the kind is confined to one individual, which is not true; or it can be the receptive matter (al-qabil). In the case of the receptive matter, we have a regress: the determination of the receptive matter itself must be explained. If that determination is by its own essence, then again confinement; if by another receptive matter, infinite regress; if by the received form (al-maqbul), circularity (dawr). All these are absurd. Moreover, it is not allowed that the cause be a separate entity (mubayin). Therefore determination cannot be existential; it must be non-existential (adami). The commentator later notes that this proof only applies to determinations of individuals that share a kind, not to those whose kind is confined to one individual.

تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof applies to all determinations, including those of unique individuals.
Correction: The proof explicitly considers 'the determination of an individual that has what shares its kind'. It does not apply to individuals whose kind has no other members.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why can't determination be existential?
A) Because all possible causes lead to absurdity.
B) Because the separate cause cannot be a cause.
C) Because determination has no cause.
D) Because the essence is the only possible cause.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that assuming determination is existential leads inevitably to absurdity, establishing that it must be non-existential (adami).

?
The proof relies on the impossibility of infinite regress and circularity. Can you identify any belief you hold that, if traced back, relies on an infinite chain or circular reasoning? Will you commit to seeking a foundation in your faith that is solid and ends with Allah?

Necessity Precedes Existence: Three Ranks
Explains why the effect's necessity must precede its existence, leading to three ranks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of an effect must precede its existence and how this yields three logical ranks in causal analysis.

Core Idea
Necessity is the logical foundation of existence.

This lesson explores a fundamental principle in Islamic causal philosophy: an effect cannot exist without a prior necessity derived from its cause. The commentator shows that this chain of necessity precedes existence by three distinct ranks. We will unpack this reasoning and see how it structures our understanding of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: wujūb (necessity) and wujūd (existence).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Trace the causal flow
The passage argues that the effect's necessity comes from the cause's existence, and the cause's existence is posterior to its own necessity. Follow the logical sequence.
3
Identify the three ranks
The commentator explicitly states that existence is posterior to necessity by three ranks. Identify: (1) necessity of the cause, (2) existence of the cause, (3) necessity of the effect, then existence of the effect.
These are logical priorities, not temporal stages.
4
Reflect on logical priority
Consider why these ranks are logical rather than temporal. This distinction is crucial for understanding causal necessity in Islamic philosophy.
Think of it as a dependency chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما لم يجب المعلول عن يوجد لما ستعرفه من أن الممكن الموجود لا بد له من وجوب سابق على وجوده مستفاد من علته. وما لم تجب العلة لا المعلول عنها، وذلك لأن وجوب المعلول مستفاد من وجود العلة قطعاً، ووجودها متأخر عن وجوبها، فإن الشيء ما لم يجب وجوده إما لذاته أو لغيره لم يوجد. قوله: ( فيكون وجوده متأخراً عن وجوبهما بمراتب أي بثلاث مراتب، كما دل عليه السياق، وصرح به في حاشية التجريد.
And the effect is not necessitated unless it exists, [otherwise] because you will know that an existing contingent must have a necessity prior to its existence derived from its cause. And the cause is not necessitated [to cause] the effect, because indeed the necessity of the effect is derived from the existence of the cause necessarily, and its existence is posterior to its necessity. For a thing, if its existence is not necessitated—either by itself or by another—it does not exist. His statement: 'so its existence is posterior to their necessity by ranks, i.e., by three ranks, as indicated by the context, and as stated explicitly in the gloss on al-Tajrid.'
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a proof that necessity (wujūb) must precede existence (wujūd) in a causal chain, and it introduces the concept of three ranks. The main text (matn) argues that a contingent thing (mumkin) cannot exist without a prior necessity that comes from its cause. Similarly, the cause itself must have its own necessity prior to causing. The commentator then explains that the existence of the effect is posterior to the necessities of both the cause and the effect by three ranks: (1) the necessity of the cause, (2) the existence of the cause, (3) the necessity of the effect, and then the existence of the effect. This is a standard doctrine in Islamic philosophy regarding the priority of necessity over existence.

Key Terms
المعلول
العلة
الوجوب
الوجود
مراتب
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three ranks are temporal stages in time.
Correction: The ranks are logical priorities in the analysis of causation, not temporal phases. They indicate dependency, not chronological order.

Checkpoint
How many ranks separate the necessity of the cause from the existence of the effect, according to the text?
A) Two ranks
B) Three ranks
C) Four ranks
D) One rank

Key Takeaway
The existence of an effect logically depends on three prior ranks of necessity and existence starting from its cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The text states that nothing exists unless its existence is necessitated. Have you ever attributed something in your life to random chance or luck? How can you replace that thought with the conviction that everything happens by divine necessity and wisdom?
?

The First Proposition: Imkan Necessitates the Contingent to a Cause
Al-Ansari states the first proposition and clarifies key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proposition of the fourth section: that imkan (possibility) is what necessitates the contingent to need a cause, and the clarification of key terms such as muhwij and sabab.

Core Idea
Imkan is the reason the contingent needs a cause, not the cause itself.

In this passage, al-Ansari introduces the first investigation concerning the contingent: the claim by the philosophers that imkan necessitates the contingent to a cause. He clarifies the meaning of 'absolutely' and explains that the 'necessitator' and 'cause' are used in a broad sense. This lesson establishes the foundational proposition for the subsequent arguments about causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the passage
Identify the main proposition: 'imkan necessitates the contingent to a cause'.
Note that this is the first investigation of the fourth section.
2
Clarify the word 'absolutely'
The commentator explains that 'absolutely' means whether imkan is an accident or identical to the essence.
3
Understand the terms muhwij and sabab
The commentator explains that 'muhwij' is broader than 'illa' (cause) and 'sabab' is broader than 'mu'aththir' (efficient cause). This is the philosophers' usage.
This clarifies the scope of the proposition.
4
Reflect on the role of imkan
Imkan is the cause of the need for a cause, not the cause that produces the contingent.
This is a common point of confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع: في أبحاث الممكن لذاته وهي ( أيضاً ( أربعة : أحدها قال الحكماء : الإمكان محوج للممكن ) إلى السبب ) أي الإمكان علة احتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر ( وفي قوله : ( مطلقاً ) أي سواء كان عارضاً أو نفس الماهية. قوله: (أي الإمكان الخ) لما كان المحوج أعم من أن يكون علة أو جزءاً أو شرطاً لها، والسبب أعم من أن يكون مؤثراً أو لا، فسرهما بما هو مراد الحكماء منهما . قوله : ( فإن الممكن إلخ لما كان الحكم بأن الدعوى ضرورية نظرياً استدل عليه، وحاصله، أن من تصور الممكن بالوجه الذي هو مناط الحكم أعني التساوي والاحتياج إلى المؤثر والنسبة بينهما حصل له الحكم من غير توقف على شيء فهو أولى، وإن كان تصور طرفيه نظرياً، وبما ذكرنا اندفع ما قيل أن معنى الممكن ما لا يقتضي ذاته وجوده وعدمه اقتضاء تاماً، وهو لا يستلزم تساوي الطرفين عنده إلا بعد نفي أن لا يكون أحد طرفيه أولى بالنظر إلى ذاته كافية في الوقوع فيكون ثبوت الاحتياج للممكن المعرف بالتعريف المذكور نظرياً؛ لأن غاية ما ذكر أن يكون تصور الموضوع بالوجه الذي هو مناط الحكم نظرياً، وذلك لا يضر بداهته على أن
The fourth objective: On investigations of the contingent in itself. They are also four. The first: The philosophers said: Imkan (possibility) necessitates the contingent to the cause, i.e., imkan is the cause of the contingent's need for the efficient cause. And in his statement: 'absolutely', i.e., whether it is an accident or identical to the essence. His statement: 'i.e., imkan, etc.' Since the necessitator (al-muhwij) is more general than being a cause or a part or a condition for it, and the cause (al-sabab) is more general than being an efficient cause or not, he interpreted them according to what the philosophers intended. His statement: 'For the contingent (al-mumkin), etc.' Since the judgment that the claim is necessary, being theoretical, he argued for it. Its summary: Whoever conceives of the contingent in the aspect that is the basis of the judgment, namely the equivalence and the need for an efficient cause and the relation between them, the judgment is obtained without dependence on anything else, so it is more appropriate, even if the conception of its two sides is theoretical. And by what we mentioned, the objection is repelled that the meaning of the contingent is that its essence does not necessitate its existence or non-existence with complete necessitation, and it does not entail the equality of the two sides according to him except after negating that one of its sides is more appropriate in view of its essence sufficient for occurrence, so the establishment of need for the contingent defined by the mentioned definition would be theoretical; because the utmost that is mentioned is that the conception of the subject in the aspect that is the basis of the judgment is theoretical, and that does not harm its self-evidence.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth section of the work, which examines the contingent (al-mumkin) in itself. It states that there are four investigations (abhas) in this section, and begins the first: the philosophers' claim that imkan (possibility) is what necessitates the contingent to need a cause (sabab) or efficient cause (mu'aththir). The commentator clarifies that 'imkan is the cause of the need'—it is not the cause that produces the contingent, but the reason why it requires a cause. He then comments on the word 'absolutely' (mutlaqan), which likely appears in the matn, explaining that it means whether imkan is an accident or identical to the essence. Next, he explains the philosophers' usage of 'muhwij' (necessitator) and 'sabab' (cause), showing that they are general terms. Finally, he addresses a potential objection: the judgment that imkan necessitates need might be considered theoretical, not self-evident. He argues that it is actually self-evident once the contingent is correctly conceived as having equal relation to existence and non-existence and needing an efficient cause. Even if the conception of the subject is theoretical, the judgment itself is immediate. The passage ends abruptly with 'on the condition that' (ala an), likely continuing onto the next page.

The Self-Evident Nature of the Claim that Imkan Necessitates a Cause
Al-Ansari defends the self-evidence of the first proposition against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers argue that the judgment 'imkan necessitates a cause' is self-evident, and how they rebut the objection that it is theoretical.

Core Idea
The judgment that the contingent needs a cause is immediate once its essence is properly conceived.

After stating the proposition, the author addresses a potential objection: isn't the claim that imkan necessitates a cause a theoretical judgment? He argues that it is actually self-evident because once one conceives of the contingent as having equal possibility and needing an efficient cause, the judgment follows without further reasoning. Even if the conceptualization itself is theoretical, the judgment remains immediate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the structure
Look for the claim, the objection, and the response.
The passage begins with 'قوله : ( فإن الممكن إلخ'.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that the definition of mumkin does not imply equality of sides without further reasoning, so the judgment would be theoretical.
This challenges the self-evidence of the judgment.
3
Analyze the response
The response is that whoever conceives of the contingent with the relevant attributes (equality and need) obtains the judgment immediately; even if the conception is theoretical, the judgment is direct.
The key is distinguishing conception of subject from self-evidence of judgment.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument establishes a foundational principle in kalam without circular reasoning.

The Objection of Majority Disagreement and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani responds that Muslims and all millis affirm God's particularization without a cause, which actually supports the principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani refutes the objection that most rational people allow contingent events without a cause, by showing that religious communities implicitly affirm a cause for the world's existence.

Core Idea
Objectors who deny causality still affirm it for God's creation.

This passage addresses a common objection: if most rational people allow events without a cause, then the necessity of a cause is not universal. Al-Jurjani responds by pointing out that even those objectors—Muslims and all followers of revealed religions—affirm a cause in the case of God's creation of the world. This refutes the objection by exposing inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parentheses in the Arabic; they indicate inserted commentary.
2
Identify the objection
The objector claims that most rational people (al-ʿuqalāʾ) allow the inclination of one side of a contingent without a cause in many instances. They argue that such people would not deny a primary truth, so the principle that every contingent needs a cause is not universally agreed upon.
3
Identify the reply
Al-Jurjani replies that Muslims and all millis (adherents of a revealed religion) judge the opposite: they affirm that God particularized the world at its time without an external particularizing cause. This means they do not allow inclination without a cause in this case; they implicitly accept that God's will is the cause.
The key phrase is 'حكموا بخلافه' meaning 'they judged the opposite' of the objector's claim.
4
Understand the logical force
The reply exposes inconsistency: objectors deny causality in some cases but affirm it for God's creation. Thus, they do not truly reject the principle; they simply apply it differently. The objection therefore fails to refute the necessity of a cause.
5
Reflect on the subtlety
Note that the objection is not simply contradicted; it is shown that the objectors' own practice (in theological matters) confirms the principle. This is a powerful rhetorical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قيل: أكثر العقلاء قالوا بخلافه)، حيث جوزوا رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن لا عن سبب مرجح في مواضع كثيرة، ولا شك أن أكثر العقلاء لا يقدمون على إنكار الحكم البديهي ( فالمسلمون ) بل المليون قاطبة حكموا بخلافه ) في تخصيص الله العالم بوقته ) الذي أوجده فيه بلا مرجح مخصص.
And if it is said: Most rational people have said the opposite, for they allowed the inclination of one side of the contingent without a determining cause in many instances, and there is no doubt that most rational people do not proceed to deny a primary truth. (So the Muslims) — rather, all the millis — (judged the opposite) in the particularization of God of the world at its time, which He brought into existence without a particularizing determining factor.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents another objection and answer. The objector claims that most rational people disagree with the judgment, as they allow the inclination of one side of a contingent without a determining cause in many cases, and most rational people would not deny a primary truth. The answer: on the contrary, Muslims — and indeed all millis (adherents of a revealed religion) — judge the opposite in the particularization of God's creation of the world at its specific time, which He brought into existence without any particularizing determining factor. That is, they affirm that God's act of creating the world at a particular time has no external determining factor, thus they themselves assume a determining factor (God's will) but deny it at the same time? Actually, the author's point is that even those who disagree implicitly accept a determining factor (God's will) for the world's existence, thereby acknowledging the principle that a contingent needs a cause. The text is brief but suggests that the objection is refuted by showing that all religious people accept that God's act is without a cause? Wait: the text says 'حكموا بخلافه في تخصيص الله العالم بوقته الذي أوجده فيه بلا مرجح مخصص' meaning they judged the opposite (i.e., that the world can exist without a determining factor?) No, the objector says most rational people allow inclination without a cause, but the answer says Muslims and millis judge the opposite (i.e., they do not allow inclination without a cause) because they affirm that God particularized the world at its time without a particularizing determining factor? That seems contradictory. Actually, the translation is tricky. The phrase 'حكموا بخلافه' — here 'خلافه' likely refers to the objector's claim. The objector says most rational people allow inclination without a cause; the answer says Muslims and millis judge the opposite, i.e., they do not allow it, and they do so in the case of God's creation of the world at a specific time without a particularizing factor. That is, they accept that God's will is the determining factor, but they deny that there is any external cause, so they are implicitly agreeing that a contingent (the world) needs a cause (God's will). The point is that they do not deny the principle entirely. The commentary should explain that the objection fails because even those who object in some cases accept the principle in the case of God, so they are inconsistent.

The Argument from Animal Instinct and the Reply
Al-Jurjani argues from animal instinct and replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani uses animal instinct as evidence for the need of a determinant and why this argument is refuted by distinguishing occurrence from possibility.

Core Idea
Animals' instinct proves need for a cause, but only for new events, not for possibility.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani presents a clever argument from animal behavior to support the need for a determinant. He then replies to it, clarifying the limits of this instinct. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Argument
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a claim, an example, and a rebuttal.
Pay attention to the terms 'imkan' (possibility) and 'huduth' (occurrence).
2
Analyze the Core Reasoning
Understand that the argument claims animals instinctively judge that two equal possibilities need a determinant. The example of wooden images illustrates this: animals imagine a sound and infer a determinant causes it.
The key is that the animals 'takhayyala' (imagined) a preponderance.
3
Examine the Reply
The reply denies the instinct—it is not about possibility but about the actual occurrence of sound. Animals react because the sound is new, not because it is possible. This distinction weakens the argument.
The reply shifts the focus from imkan to huduth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(بل) الحكم بالاحتياج إلى المتساويين إلى المرجح (مركوز في طباع البهائم ) أيضاً ، ( ولذلك ) تراها ( تنفر من صور الخشب ) فإنه لما كان وجود الصوت وعدمه متساويين بالنسبة إلى ذات الصوت تخيلت البهائم من رجحان وجوده على عدمه أن هناك مرجحاً رجحه عليه فنفرت وهربت منه (قلنا : ذلك أي نفورها لحدوثه لا لإمكانه )
(Rather) the judgment that the two equal [terms] are in need of a determinant is ingrained in the nature of beasts as well. And therefore you see them shying away from wooden images. For when the existence and non-existence of sound were equal in relation to the essence of sound, the beasts imagined from its existence being preponderated over its non-existence that there is a determinant that preponderated it, so they shied and fled from it. (We reply: That—meaning their shying away—is due to its occurrence, not due to its possibility.)
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents a proof and a rebuttal. The author first asserts that the need of two equal sides for a determinant is ingrained even in animals, citing their aversion to wooden images. The reasoning is that since for a contingent sound existence and non-existence are equal, the animals perceive a determinant causing preponderance. However, the response denies this interpretation: the aversion comes from the sound's newness (huduth), not its possibility (imkan). Thus the animals do not recognize the principle of a determinant for possibility but rather the need for an originator for an event.

Key Terms
إمكان
مرجح
بهائم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals' instinct proves the need for a determinant for possibility.
Correction: It actually proves only the need for an originator for an event (occurrence).

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the argument from animals' fear?
A) The animals are reacting to the possibility of sound.
B) The animals are reacting to the occurrence of sound.
C) The animals are not rational.
D) The argument is not based on instinct.

Key Takeaway
Animals' instinct does not prove the need for a determinant for possible existents, but only for actual occurrences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that God must create because of some inherent need in His nature, confusing the contingency of possible things with the temporality of events? This lesson shows that instinct only points to a cause for new events, not for possibility itself. How does that challenge your understanding of God's absolute freedom and will?
?
When you witness a natural phenomenon, does your mind automatically seek a secondary cause, or do you pause to recognize that only God is the Creator of the event itself? This lesson distinguishes between the cause of occurrence and the cause of possibility. What would change if you began to see every event as a direct act of God without reliance on secondary causes?
?
The animals' instinct leads them to flee from wooden images, but they do not reflect on the metaphysical grounding. You have intellect to go beyond instinct. Are you content with superficial causality, or will you strive to internalize that only God is the true originator of all occurrences? What concrete step can you take today to strengthen your tawhid in this regard?

The Objection from Widespread Disagreement and the Reply
Al-Jurjani enumerates groups who seem to allow preponderance without a determinant and explains that they are forced into it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection from widespread disagreement does not undermine the principle of determinism, and how the author distinguishes between entailment and explicit commitment.

Core Idea
No rational thinker explicitly denies that preponderance requires a cause; only their doctrines entail it.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani addresses a powerful objection: many rational people seem to allow preponderance without a determinant. He responds by showing that none of them actually assert the principle's falsehood; rather, their positions imply it, but they consistently reject that implication. This distinction between entailment (luzum) and commitment (iltizam) is crucial for defending the causal principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the groups
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Make a mental list of all the groups mentioned: Muslims, milliyun, Ash'arites, Mu'tazila, and philosophers. Notice the examples given for each group.
The objector uses these examples to argue that many rational people reject the need for a determinant.
2
Understand the objector's claim
The objector claims that these groups allow preponderance of one of the two sides of a contingent without a determining cause in many cases. This seems to contradict the self-evident principle that every contingent requires a determinant.
3
Grasp the author's reply
The author responds by making a key distinction: these groups do not explicitly say that preponderance occurs without a determinant. Rather, their doctrines entail (lazim) that consequence, but they themselves do not commit to it (iltizam). Instead, they try to contrive answers to avoid the implication.
The fact that they bother to contrive answers shows that even they recognize the falsity of the claim.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this reply strengthens the principle of determinism. The author argues that no rational person actually denies the need for a cause; only some positions imply it, and their proponents reject that implication. This upholds the self-evident nature of the principle.
The objection is defeated by showing that the alleged counterexamples are not genuine denials.

And we will detail those answers, strong and weak, in their proper places from what will come to you in the book.)

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector lists many groups (Muslims, milliyun, Ash'arites, Mu'tazila, philosophers) whose doctrines seem to allow preponderance without a determinant. The author responds by stating that none of these groups explicitly say such a thing; rather, their doctrines entail it (lazim), but they do not commit to it (iltizam). Instead, they try to construct answers to avoid the consequence. The fact that they bother to contrive answers shows that they instinctively recognize the falsity of the claim, otherwise they would not bother or would boldly embrace its consequence.

The Implicit Admission of Causality by Its Deniers
Response to objections: no rational person admits occurrence without a preponderant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even the groups whose positions appear to deny the need for a cause (Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, philosophers) do not explicitly claim an uncaused event, and their efforts to answer objections show the principle is universally acknowledged.

Core Idea
No rational person admits an event without a cause.

This passage addresses objections from three groups—Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and philosophers—whose theories seem to violate the principle that every possible requires a preponderant cause. The author responds by showing that none of them explicitly say that one of two equals occurs without a cause, and their attempts to defend themselves prove the principle is deeply rooted in reason.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups mentioned
Read the passage and list the three groups: Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and philosophers. Note the specific cases where each group appears to deny the need for a preponderant cause.
The Ash'aris are mentioned in connection with divine rulings, the Mu'tazila with power and attributes, and the philosophers with celestial motions and planetary positions.
2
Understand the author's response
The author asserts that none of these groups explicitly claim that an event occurs without a cause, even though their positions may imply it. They seek to answer the objection with arguments, whether strong or weak.
This shows that the principle is ingrained in their minds; otherwise, they would not bother to defend themselves.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the fact that these groups attempt to answer the objection is evidence for the universality of the causal principle. How does this strengthen the author's overall argument?

Commentary
This passage is a response to objections raised by various groups (Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and philosophers) against the principle that every occurrence of one of two equally possible alternatives requires a preponderant cause (murajjih). The author first lists specific cases where these groups appear to deny this principle: the Ash'aris in their theory of divine purpose and ruling on acts; the Mu'tazila in their view on power and attributes; and the philosophers in their account of celestial motions, planetary positions, and other particular specifications. The response (qulna) points out that none of these rational thinkers explicitly claim that one of two equals occurs without a preponderant. Rather, their positions imply such a necessity in some judgments, but they do not accept it and instead seek to answer the objection with arguments, whether strong or weak. The fact that they attempt to find answers shows that the absurdity of an uncaused event is deeply rooted in their minds; otherwise they would not bother to defend themselves. The author promises to detail these answers later in the book. The key argument is that the principle is universally acknowledged even by those whose theories seem to contradict it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

6 min
The Possible Essence Necessitates Equality
First path of second proof: argument from equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possible essence necessitates equality of existence and non-existence, and why occurrence without an external preponderant would contradict that equality.

Core Idea
The possible is equally disposed to existence and non-existence.

This passage presents the core of the second proof for the necessity of a cause. It begins by stating that a possible essence, by itself, is equally related to existence and non-existence. If one side occurs without any external preference, that side would become preferred—contradicting the assumption of equality. This sets up the need for a preponderant.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument passage
Read the first part of the source text where the argument is presented: 'The possible essence necessitates equality... so if one occurs without preponderant... that side would be preponderant... contradicting the hypothesis.'
Notice the hypothesis is that the two sides are equal relative to the possible.
2
Understand 'equality'
Understand that 'equality' (tasawi) means the possible is not inclined to existence more than non-existence or vice versa. This is the meaning of imkan.
The passage says 'equality of existence and non-existence in relation to it.'
3
Identify the contradiction
If one side occurs without a preponderant, that side would become 'more appropriate' (awla) and thus not equal. This contradicts the hypothesis. Ask: why does occurrence without preference make it preponderant? Because if no external factor tips the balance, the fact of occurrence itself shows the side has an intrinsic preference.
This is the key logical move.

The second methodology regarding its proof: the demonstration upon it, and in it are paths. The first: the possible essence (necessitates equality, i.e., equality of existence and non-existence in relation to it. (So if one of them occurs without the preponderant from outside, that occurring side would be preponderant and more appropriate with respect to it than the other side, so it would not be equal to it, and that contradicts the hypothesis which is their equality with respect to the essence of the possible and is opposite to it. We said: Only the essence's volition of it, i.e., of that occurring side, contradicts it, i.e., the hypothesis which is equality, because the meaning of equality of the two sides is that the essence of the possible does not necessitate this nor that, so its opposite is the essence's volition of one of them, not its occurrence, i.e., not the occurrence of one of them without a cause, as the opponent who says by coincidence claims, and that one His saying: (In relation to it) i.e., to the possible essence; he restricted it with that because if it were necessitating absolutely, its existence and non-existence would be impossible. His saying: (And more appropriate with respect to it) i.e., in relation to it due to the assumption of no preponderant, not because of it. His saying: (Because the meaning of equality, etc.) in it there is discussion because what he mentioned is the meaning of possibility and its consequent equality: that one of the two sides is not more appropriate with an appropriateness sufficient for occurrence. So if the occurrence of the two sides is assumed without the meaning of the preponderant from outside, one of the two sides would be more appropriate in relation to its essence without doubt, so it would be contrary to equality in the mentioned meaning, so consider. His saying: (The opponent who says by coincidence) i.e., the occurrence of the possible however it happens; he is Democritus as will come, so his saying: 'And that one of the two equals' is an explanatory conjunction for it. So that it becomes a criticism of the first methodology as well, rather it negates the contradiction. If you say: We do not accept that the possible necessitates equality because it is possible for one of the two sides to have priority without reaching the limit of necessity. I say: That will be invalidated. And if it is granted, we say: Priority, if it does not reach the limit of necessity, then with it the side that has priority may occur and may not occur, so equality of existence and non-existence with respect to the two times of priority is realized, and its verification will come in the third research of the conclusion. And this suffices for what we are in. If you say: It is possible that the essence of the possible by itself necessitates priority of one of the two sides without reaching the limit of necessity, and through that priority and preponderance it necessitates the necessity of that side, and it does not follow that the possible is necessary by essence, because the necessary is that whose existence is necessary when considered without regard to anything else, and here necessity has occurred with regard to something else, which is the preponderance arising from the essence as such. I say: The essence, with the priority attributed to it, if it is necessitating the necessity of existence, it is a source of the impossibility of the separation of existence from it absolutely, and we do not mean by necessity except this. And the consideration of the intermediary only invalidates the necessity if it were not attributed to it, as is not hidden. His saying: 'We said: Only it contradicts it, etc.' It should not be said that the explainer did not claim contradiction but rather contrary to the hypothesis, for we say: That follows from his speech, and therefore the commentator said in his exposition of his speech: 'and opposite to it', and that his saying 'contradicts the hypothesis' means it opposes it. His saying: (As the opponent who says by coincidence claims) i.e., the occurrence of one of the two sides of the possible by way of coincidence without a cause. And by the opponent are meant those who deny the need of the possible for a necessitator, like Democritus and his followers who say that the existence of the heavens is by coincidence, and they have various sophisms.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the first path (al-tariq al-awwal) of the second methodology (al-manhaj al-thani) for proving the necessity of a cause for the possible. The argument begins: the possible essence (al-mahiya al-mumkina) necessitates equality—that is, it is equally related to existence and non-existence. If one side occurs without an external preponderant (murajjih min kharaj), then that occurring side would be preponderant and more appropriate (awla), thus not equal, contradicting the hypothesis. The answer (qulna) clarifies that the contradiction arises specifically from the essence's volition (iqtida' al-dhat) of that side, not from mere occurrence (hussul) without a cause. The meaning of equality is that the essence does not necessitate either side, so its opposite is necessitating one side. The author then engages with objections: the first objection denies that the possible necessitates equality, suggesting a priority (ula) that does not reach necessity. The reply: such priority still allows the side to not occur, preserving equality in terms of occurrence time. A second objection proposes that the essence generates a priority that indirectly leads to necessity. The reply: if the essence with its priority necessitates existence, then existence becomes inseparable from the essence, which is exactly the meaning of necessity (wujub); the intermediary does not break the causal chain if it is attributed to the essence. The commentary also discusses the opponent who claims coincidence (ittifaq), identified as Democritus, who held that the heavens exist by chance. The author notes that this argument also critiques the first methodology. The passage is a thorough dialectical examination of the principle that the possible requires an external cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That occurrence itself, without the essence's inclination, contradicts equality.
Correction: It is the essence's volition (iqtida') of that side that contradicts equality, not mere occurrence. The author later clarifies: 'Only the essence's volition of it... not its occurrence.'

Checkpoint
What does the possible essence necessitate according to the first path?
A) Necessity of existence
B) Equality of existence and non-existence
C) Priority of one side over the other
D) The need for an external preponderant

Key Takeaway
The possible essence is inherently indifferent to existence and non-existence; any occurrence without an external cause would imply a preference that violates that indifference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own existence is equally possible as non-existence, what does that imply about your constant dependence on the One who tipped the balance in your favor? Have you thanked Him today as if He had no obligation to bring you into being?
?
When you look at the world around you—your health, your wealth, your relationships—do you assume they simply occurred without a cause, or do you actively trace each blessing back to the External Preponderant? Where is your heart really resting?
?
The possible essence is utterly indifferent, yet you are here, alive and accountable. What if you were not? Does the reality that you could have just as easily not existed move you to surrender your illusion of self-sufficiency right now?

Answer: The Essence's Volition, Not Occurrence, Contradicts Equality
Answer to the objection and further dialectical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise answer to the objection: what contradicts equality is the essence's volition (iqtida') of a side, not its mere occurrence, and you will grasp the analysis of priority and indirect necessity.

Core Idea
Equality is violated by the essence's innate inclination, not by external occurrence.

Building on the previous argument, this lesson presents the answer to the objection. The author clarifies that the contradiction is not caused by the mere occurrence of a side, but by the essence's volition or inclination (iqtida') towards that side. This distinction is crucial for defending the need for an external cause. The passage also addresses objections about priority and indirect necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read from 'We said: Only the essence's volition of it... contradicts the hypothesis' up to the discussion of priority.
Notice the Arabic structure: 'innama yunaqiduhu iqtida' al-dhat lahu... la husuluhu.'
2
Understand the distinction
The key is to distinguish between the essence's internal inclination (iqtida') and the external occurrence (husul). Only the former directly contradicts equality. Occurrence alone, without the essence being the source, is not contradictory.
This is a subtle point: the opponent might claim that occurrence itself violates equality, but the author shows it is only the essence's own determination.
3
Analyze the priority objection
An objector says: maybe the possible has a priority (ula) that is not full necessity. The response: such priority still allows the other side to occur, preserving equality in terms of occurrence times.
The argument uses time analysis: if one side is more likely, it may still not occur, so equality of possibility remains.
4
Examine the indirect necessity objection
Another objection proposes that the essence generates a priority that indirectly leads to necessity through an intermediary. The answer: if the priority is from the essence, then the necessity is still ultimately from the essence, making the essence necessary by itself—which is absurd.
This refutes the attempt to avoid external cause by making the essence the source of its own necessity.
