Answer to the Equality Objection
Sanusi responds to the claim that one of two equals can occur without a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'arī response to the equality objection reinforces the necessity of a preponderant by distinguishing between essence inclination and occurrence.

Core Idea
Equality of two sides does not forbid the essence from inclining toward one side.

This passage tackles a crucial objection to the Ash'arī argument for a cause of contingent existence. The opponent claims that if two possibilities are equal, one cannot occur without a preponderant making it preferred. The response clarifies what equality truly means, preserving the need for an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening lines: if one of two equals occurs without an external preponderant, it becomes preferred, contradicting the assumption of equality.
This objection challenges the need for a preponderant.
2
Follow Sanusi's reply
Sanusi says the occurrence does not contradict equality; it indicates that the essence has an inclination (اقتضاء الذات) toward that side.
The reply shifts focus from occurrence to inclination.
3
Understand the precise meaning of equality
Equality means the essence does not necessitate either side. Its opposite is inclination, not mere occurrence.
This prevents the confusion between essence and event.
4
Consider the commentator's discussion
The commentator notes that if equality means no side is sufficiently entitled, then occurrence implies preference, which conflicts with that equality. This highlights the nuance.
The discussion refines the definition of equality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقع أحدهما لا المرجح من خارج ( كان ) ذلك الطرف الواقع ( راجحاً) وأولى بها من الطرف الآخر، فلايكون مساوياً له وهو خلاف المفروض الذي هو تساويهما بالنسبة إلى ماهية الممكن ومناقض له قلنا: إنما يناقضه أي المفروض الذي هو التساوي ( اقتضاء الذات له أي لذلك الطرف الواقع، لأن معنى تساوي الطرفين أن ذات الممكن لا تقتضي هذا ولا ذاك، فنقيضه اقتضاء الذات أحدهما، ( لا حصوله ) أي لا حصول أحدهما ( لا) ،لعلة كما يزعمه الخصم القائل بالاتفاق، وأن أحد . قوله : (بالقياس إليها أي إلى الماهية الممكنة، قيد بذلك لأنها لو كانت مقتضية مطلقاً لامتنع وجودها وعدمها . قوله : ( وأولى بها ) أي بالقياس إليها لفرض عدم المرجح لا بسببها . قوله : ( لأن معنى تساوي (الخ فيه بحث لأن ما ذكره معنى الإمكان ومقتضاه التساوي أن لا يكون أحد الطرفين أولى به أولوية كافية في الوقوع فإذا فرض وقوع الطرفين لا بمعنى المرجح من خارج كان أحد الطرفين أولى بالقياس إلى ذاته بلا شبهة فيكون منافيا للتساوي بالمعنى المذكور فتدبر. قوله : ( القائل بالاتفاق أي بوقوع الممكن كيف ما اتفق هو ديمقراطيس على ما سيجيء، فقوله : وإن أحد المتساويين عطف تفسيري له .
When one of the two [sides] occurs without an external preponderant, that occurring side becomes preferred and more entitled to it than the other side, so it is not equal to it, and this contradicts the assumed equality of the two sides with respect to the essence of the contingent and is opposed to it. We say: It only contradicts it—i.e., the assumption which is equality—[in that it implies] the essence's inclination towards that occurring side, because the meaning of the equality of the two sides is that the essence of the contingent does not necessitate this nor that, so its opposite is the essence's inclination towards one of them, not its occurrence—i.e., not the occurrence of one of them—without a cause, as the opponent who claims coincidence claims, and that one of the two equals [occurs by chance]. His statement: 'in relation to it' means in relation to the contingent essence; he restricted it because if it were necessitating absolutely, its existence and non-existence would be impossible. His statement: 'and more entitled to it' means in relation to it, due to the assumption of the absence of an external preponderant, not because of it. His statement: 'because the meaning of equality...' contains discussion, because what he mentioned is the meaning of contingency and its consequence is equality, namely that neither of the two sides is more entitled to it with an entitlement sufficient for occurrence. So if occurrence is assumed, [then] without an external preponderant, one of the two sides becomes more entitled in relation to itself, without doubt, and this would conflict with equality in the mentioned sense, so reflect. His statement: 'the opponent who claims coincidence' means the occurrence of the contingent however it happens; he is Democritus, as will come, so his statement: 'and that one of the two equals' is a explicative apposition to it.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents and answers an objection against the Ash'arī argument that the contingent requires a preponderant (مُرَجِّح) for its existence. The objection claims: if the two sides (existence and non-existence) are truly equal with respect to the contingent's essence, then if one side occurs without any external preponderant, that side becomes preferred (راجح) and more entitled to occur—thus it is no longer equal, contradicting the initial assumption. The answer clarifies that the occurrence of one side does not contradict equality of the essence; rather it indicates that the essence has an inclination (اقتضاء الذات) toward that side. The commentator explains that equality means the essence does not necessitate either side, so its opposite is an inclination of the essence, not the mere occurrence. He then explains the glosses: 'in relation to it' restricts the equality to the essence, because if the essence necessitated absolutely, existence and non-existence would be impossible. The 'entitlement' is relative to the essence under the assumption of no external preponderant. A further note questions the meaning of equality, noting that if equality means no side is sufficiently entitled, then the occurrence of one side implies a relative preference, conflicting with that equality. Finally, the opponent is identified as Democritus, who believes in pure coincidence.

Key Terms
مرجح
تساوي
اقتضاء الذات
اتفاق
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two possibilities are equal, neither can occur without an external preponderant contradicting the equality.
Correction: Equality of the essence means it does not necessitate either side, but occurrence can still happen due to an inclination (which itself requires a cause).

Checkpoint
What does the answer say the occurrence of one side without an external preponderant implies?
A) That the two sides are still equal
B) That the essence has an inclination toward that side
C) That the side occurred by necessity
D) That the contingent is impossible

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arī response shows that equality of two sides does not prevent occurrence, but occurrence implies a causal inclination, preserving the need for a preponderant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the so-called 'random' or 'coincidental' events in your life, do you actually believe they happen without any cause? If not, what does your belief in a cause—even if unseen—demand of you in terms of gratitude or accountability today?
?
Consider a moment when you felt torn between two choices, each seemingly equal. Did you assume that whichever choice you made was simply a 'chance' decision, or did you recognize that your essence—your desires, fears, or values—was actually inclining you? How does Sanusi's distinction between occurrence and essence's inclination challenge you to take responsibility for your choices?
?
If every event has a cause, and that cause ultimately traces back to Allah's will, then what are you attributing to 'luck' or 'fortune' that you should instead attribute to Him? Write down one specific area of your life where you have been acting as if things 'just happen,' and commit to changing your perspective starting now.

Inherent Preference and Its Implications
Sanusi refutes objections to the Ash'arī position on contingency and causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that the contingent's essence could have an inherent preference that does not reach necessity, and why that still entails the need for an external cause or leads to self-necessity.

Core Idea
A preference without necessity still implies equality; a self-sufficient preference leads to self-necessity.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a sophisticated objection: perhaps the contingent's essence has a built-in preference for one state without that preference being necessitating. He shows that such a preference would still result in the same problem of needing an external cause. He then tackles a further objection that the preference itself could be the cause of necessity, and demonstrates that this amounts to making the essence self-necessitating, which contradicts its contingent nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Try to grasp the flow of objection and response.
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail initially.
2
Understand the first objection and refutation
The objector says the contingent might have a preference that is not necessitating. Sanusi responds that such a preference still leaves the possibility of both sides, so equality holds relative to that preference.
This preserves the need for an external cause to break the equality.
3
Understand the second objection and refutation
The objector then suggests the essence itself could necessitate a preference, which then necessitates existence. Sanusi counters that if the essence with the preference necessitates existence, it is a source of impossibility of non-existence, which is exactly what necessity means, making the essence self-necessitating.
The presence of an intermediary (the preference) does not negate necessity if the intermediary is rooted in the essence.
4
Identify the opponent
Sanusi identifies the opponent as Democritus and his followers, who deny the need for a cause and attribute existence to coincidence.
This roots the discussion in a historical theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: لا نسلم اقتضاء الممكن للتساوي لجواز أولوية أحد الطرفين من غير أن يصل إلى حد الوجوب، قلت: سيبطل ذلك، ولو سلم :قلنا الأولوية إذا لم تصل إلى حد الوجوب فمعها قديقع الطرف الأولى وقد لا يقع فيتحقق تساوي الوجود والعدم بالنسبة إلى وقتي الأولوية وسيجيء تحقيقه اني ثالث أبحاث الخاتمة، وهذا القدر يكفي فيما نحن فيه فإن قلت : يجوز أن تقتضي ذات الممكن بانفرادها أولوية أحد الطرفين من غير أن يصل إلى حد الوجوب، وبواسطة تلك الأولوية والرجحان يقتضي وجوب ذلك الطرف، ولا يلزم كون الممكن واجباً بالذات؛ لأن الواجب هو الذي يجب وجوده إذا التفت إليه من غير التفات إلى غيره وهاهنا قد وجب الوجوب مع الالتفات إلى الغير وهو الرجحان الناشئ عن الذات من حيث هي قلت الذات مع الأولوية المستنده إليه إذا كان : مقتضيا لوجوب الوجود كان مبدأ لاستحالة انفكاك الوجود عنه قطعاً، ولا نعني بالوجوب إلا هذا، واعتبار الواسطة إنما يقدح في الوجوب لو لم تكن مستندة إليه كما لا يخفى . قوله : قلنا إنما يناقضه الخ لا يقال المعلل لم يدع التناقض بل خلاف المفروض لأنا نقول : يلزم من كلامه ذلك، ولذلك قال الشارح في تقرير :كلامه ومناقض له على أن قوله : يناقض المفروض معناه يخالفه . قوله : (كما يزعم الخصم القائل (بالاتفاق) أي بوقوع أحد طرفي الممكن بطريق الاتفاق من غير علة والمراد بالخصم هم المنكرون لاحتياج الممكن إلى الموجب كديمقراطيس وأتباعه القائلين بأن وجود السموات بطريق الاتفاق ولهم شبه شتى .
If you say: We do not grant the contingent's requirement of equality because of the possibility of one side having a preference does not reach the degree of necessity. I say: That will be nullified, and even if granted, we say: The preference, when it does not reach the degree of necessity, with it the preferred side may occur or may not occur, so equality of existence and non-existence is realized with respect to the two times of preference, and its verification will come in the third investigation of the conclusion, and this much suffices for what we are in. Then if you say: It is possible that the essence of the contingent by itself necessitates a preference for one side without reaching the degree of necessity, and by means of that preference and preponderance it necessitates the necessity of that side, and it does not follow that the contingent is necessary by itself; because the necessary is that whose existence is necessitated when considered without consideration of anything else, and here the necessity is necessitated with consideration of something else, namely the preponderance arising from the essence as such. I say: The essence with the preference attributed to it, when it is necessitating the necessity of existence, is a source for the impossibility of the separation of existence from it absolutely, and we do not mean by necessity except this, and the consideration of the intermediary only impugns the necessity if it were not attributed to it, as is not hidden. His statement: 'We say: It only contradicts it etc.' It is not said that the argumentator did not claim contradiction but rather contrary to the assumption, because we say: That follows from his statement, and therefore the commentator said in formulating his statement: 'and opposed to it' although his statement 'contradicts the assumption' means it opposes it. His statement: 'as the opponent who claims coincidence claims' means the occurrence of one of the two sides of the contingent by way of coincidence without a cause, and by 'opponent' is meant those who deny the contingent's need for a necessitator, like Democritus and his followers who say that the existence of the heavens is by coincidence, and they have various sophisms.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of objections against the Ash'arī position. It addresses the objection that the contingent's essence might have an inherent preference (أولوية) for one side without reaching necessity (وجوب). The response argues that if the preference does not necessitate, then both existence and non-existence remain possible, which still implies equality relative to that preference. The objector then proposes that the essence, by itself, could necessitate a preference, and through that preference necessitate the actual existence, thus avoiding the need for an external cause. The answer refutes this by stating that if the essence-with-preference necessitates existence, it is a source of impossibility of non-existence, which is the definition of necessity; the mere presence of an intermediary (the preference) does not remove necessity unless the intermediary is not traced back to the essence. The commentary then clarifies that the earlier 'contradiction' claim is actually about opposition to the assumption. Finally, the opponent is identified again as Democritus and his followers, who attribute existence to pure coincidence without a cause.

اقتضاء الذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A preference in the essence without an external cause would still avoid the need for a creator.
Correction: The response shows that such a preference either still leaves equality (requiring external cause) or leads to the essence being self-necessitating, which contradicts contingency.

Checkpoint
According to the response, if the essence with an inherent preference necessitates existence, what does that entail?
A) The contingent becomes impossible
B) The contingent remains contingent
C) The essence becomes a source of impossibility of non-existence
D) The essence needs an external cause to necessitate

Key Takeaway
An inherent preference in the contingent essence cannot both avoid an external cause and avoid self-necessitation; it either fails to break equality or makes the essence self-necessitating.

?

Al-Razi's Second Method for the Need of a Cause
Sanusi presents al-Razi's argument that the contingent's preponderance is existential and requires a locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that the preponderance of existence in a contingent is an existential attribute requiring a locus, which must be the efficient cause.

Core Idea
A contingent's preponderance is a real quality that cannot float free—it needs a cause.

This lesson presents a sophisticated argument from Imam al-Razi demonstrating that contingent beings need an efficient cause. Instead of looking at the contingent itself, al-Razi focuses on the 'preponderance' (tarjīḥ) that brings it into existence. He shows that this preponderance is an existential attribute, and like all such attributes, it must inhere in a subject. That subject cannot be the contingent itself, so it must be something else—the efficient cause. This argument ties the need for a cause directly to the contingent's contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get an overview of al-Razi's argument.
Notice the logical flow: preponderance → existential attribute → needs locus → locus is cause.
2
Understand the concept of preponderance
Al-Razi says the contingent must have a preponderance of existence over non-existence reaching necessity. This preponderance is an existential attribute because it occurs after non-existence.
He gives examples: motion after rest, knowledge after ignorance.
3
Follow the logical steps
Trace why the preponderance needs a locus, why that locus cannot be the contingent itself, and why it must be the efficient cause.
The key step: if the locus were the contingent, the effect would exist before the preponderance that precedes it—absurd.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument links contingency to causality. The commentator clarifies that for temporal contingents, the preponderance is temporal; for eternal contingents, it is eternal.
This avoids the problem of the eternal cause being a locus for temporal events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطريق) (الثاني) واختاره الإمام الرازي ( في المحصل والأربعين ) لا بد (للممكن) قبل الوجود أن يترجح طرف) أي يترجح طرف وجوده على عدمه بحيث يجب، لما سيأتي . ( و ) ذلك ( الترجح ) الواصل إلى حد الوجوب صفة وجودية ) ، لأنه حصل بعد ما لم يكن، فلو جاز أن لا يكون وجودياً لجاز أن لا تكون حركة بعد السكون، والعلم الحاصل بعد عدمه وجودي، وإذا كان الترجح أمراً وجودياً، فله محل موجود لامتناع قيامه بذاته أو بمعدوم آخر، (وليس) ذلك المحل ( هو الأثر ) أي الممكن ، وإلا كان الأثر ( موجوداً قبله ) أي قبل الترجح السابق على وجوده فيكون الممكن موجوداً قبل وجوده بمرتبتين، هذا خلف. فلا من شيء آخر موجود يقوم به الترجح فهو المؤثر، قلنا: لا نسلم أن يترجح وجوده قبل الوجود وما سيأتي من أنه لا بد أن يترجح، بد هناك من الممكن يجب أن . قوله : ( لا بد للممكن (الخ لإمكانه وحاصله : أن الممكن لإمكانه يحتاج إلى الترجح المحتاج إلى المؤثر فيكون لإمكانه محتاجاً إلى المؤثر. قوله : ( لأنه حصل بعد ما لم يكن أي في الممكنات ،الحادثة فتكون وجودية في الممكنات القديمة ؛ لما مر من أن الاتصاف بالصفة التي من شأنها الوجود في الخارج فرع وجودها . قوله : (فهو المؤثر) أي المحل هو المؤثر فإن كان الترجح حادثاً كان المؤثر حادثاً ولو باعتبار بعض أجزائه أو شروطه، وإن كان قديماً يكون مؤثره قديماً، فلا يلزم كون المؤثر القديم محلاً للحوادث .
The second method, and it was chosen by Imam al-Razi in al-Muhassal and al-Arba'in: The contingent, before existence, must have a side preponderated, i.e., the side of its existence preponderates over its non-existence such that it becomes necessary, as will come. And that preponderance reaching the degree of necessity is an existential attribute, because it occurs after not having been. For if it were permissible for it not to be existential, it would be permissible for motion not to occur after rest, and knowledge occurring after its non-existence is existential. And when the preponderance is an existential thing, it has an existent locus, because it is impossible for it to subsist by itself or by another non-existent. And that locus is not the effect, i.e., the contingent, otherwise the effect would exist before it, i.e., before the preponderance that precedes its existence, so the contingent would be existent before its existence by two degrees; this is absurd. Therefore, there must be another existent thing in which the preponderance subsists, and it is the efficient cause. We say: we do not grant that its existence preponderates before existence, and it will come that it must preponderate, but there, from the contingent, it must... His statement: 'The contingent must etc.' is due to its contingency, and its summary: the contingent, due to its contingency, needs the preponderance which needs the efficient cause, so due to its contingency it needs the efficient cause. His statement: 'because it occurs after not having been' means in temporal contingents, so it is existential in temporal contingents; as for eternal contingents, according to what has passed: being qualified by an attribute that is supposed to exist externally is a branch of its existence. His statement: 'it is the efficient cause' means the locus is the efficient cause. For if the preponderance is temporal, the efficient cause is temporal, even if in terms of some of its parts or conditions; and if it is eternal, its efficient cause is eternal, so it does not require the eternal efficient cause to be a locus for temporal events.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents a second method (طريق ثاني) attributed to Imam al-Razi from his works al-Muhassal and al-Arba'in, demonstrating that the contingent needs an efficient cause (مؤثر). The argument proceeds stepwise: 1) Before existence, the contingent must have a 'preponderance' (ترجح) of existence over non-existence reaching the degree of necessity (وجوب). 2) This preponderance is an existential attribute (صفة وجودية) because it occurs after non-existence; otherwise, similar temporal phenomena like motion or knowledge would not be existential. 3) Being existential, it requires a subsistent locus (محل موجود) since it cannot subsist by itself or in a non-existent. 4) This locus cannot be the contingent itself (the effect, أثر), because that would imply the effect exists before the preponderance that precedes its existence, leading to an absurdity (the contingent existing two steps before its existence). 5) Therefore, the locus must be another existent, which is the efficient cause (مؤثر). The commentator adds that this argument shows the contingent's need for a cause is rooted in its contingency (إمكان). He clarifies that the argument applies to temporal contingents; for eternal contingents (like the attributes of God in Ash'arī thought), the preponderance is not temporal. He also explains that if the preponderance is temporal, the cause is temporal; if eternal, the cause is eternal, so attributing a temporal preponderance to an eternal cause does not entail that the eternal cause is a locus for temporal events.

Key Terms
طريق ثاني
ترجح
صفة وجودية
محل
مؤثر
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The preponderance itself requires an external cause.
Correction: The argument shows that the preponderance, as an existential attribute, needs a locus (subject) to inhere in, not a separate cause. The locus becomes identified as the efficient cause.

Checkpoint
Why can the locus of the preponderance not be the contingent itself?
A) Because the contingent is non-existent before existence
B) Because that would make the effect exist before the preponderance, leading to an absurdity
C) Because the preponderance is a temporal attribute
D) Because the contingent does not need a cause

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's second method shows that the contingent needs an efficient cause because its preponderance of existence is an existential attribute that cannot inhere in the contingent itself.

?
The argument shows that the preponderance of existence cannot inhere in the created thing itself. In what areas of your life do you act as if you are the locus of your own strength, wisdom, or righteousness? How can you consciously redirect reliance onto the Creator?
?
If the preponderance is an existential attribute that occurs after non-existence, then every moment of your existence is a fresh gift. What specific habit will you adopt today to remind yourself that you are not your own source, but entirely dependent?

The Second Method's Logical Flaw: Necessary vs Desired
Sanusi distinguishes between necessary consequence and desired claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key criticism that al-Razi's second method only proves the need for a cause, not that contingency is the cause of that need.

Core Idea
Proving need is not proving the cause of need.

Sanusi begins his critique by identifying a mismatch between what the method necessarily yields and what it aims to prove. This is a classic logical distinction between the necessary consequence (lazim) and the desired claim (matlub). Let's see how this applies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Sanusi states the method and immediately points out a logical gap.
The passage is a single complex sentence; unpack it slowly.
2
Identify lazim and matlub
Find where Sanusi says what the method necessarily yields (lazim) and what it is supposed to prove (matlub). Understand the difference.
The lazim is that the contingent needs a cause; the matlub is that contingency is the cause of that need.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for the overall argument about causality? Think about how this critique undermines al-Razi's method.
This is a common tactic in kalam: testing whether a proof actually proves what it claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الطريق الثاني فيه نظر؛ لأن اللازم من هذا الطريق أن الممكن محتاج إلى المؤثر، وأما علة الاحتياج هو الإمكان فلا، فالمطلوب غير لازم واللازم غير مطلوب. قوله : (لأنه حصل بعد ما لم يكن الخ فإن قلت : هذا إنما يتم في ترجح الحادث، كما يدل عليه قوله لجاز أن لا تكون الحركة بعد السكون الخ، فلا يجري الدليل في الصفات القديمة الممكنة على رأي الأشاعرة مع أن المدعى عام قلت لو سلم فلا قائل بالفصل، فعلية : الإمكان في الحادث تستلزم العلية في غيره بطريق الأولى وفيه ما فيه . قوله: (فهو المؤثر) فيه بحث إذ لو صح هذا الدليل لزم كون الباري تعالى محلاً للحوادث، وهي ترجحات الحوادث الحادثة، ولو بني على رأي الفلاسفة كان العقل العاشر محلاً لها مع أنهم لا يقولون به أيضاً. قوله : ( وهو المتنازع فيه إن قلت بل المتنازع فيه هاهنا أخص مما ذكره، لأن النزاع هاهنا في أن علة الاحتياج هي الإمكان أو غيره لا أن الممكن هل يحتاج إلى علة أم لا، قلت : من جملة الخصوم في كون الإمكان علة للاحتياج القائلون بالاتفاق، كما سبق الآن . وسيأتي، فالنزاع معهم في نفس الاحتياج إلى العلة مالاً .
His statement: 'The second method: there is consideration in it, because what is necessary from this method is that the contingent needs the efficient cause, but the cause of the need is contingency, not [the method's conclusion], so the required [conclusion] is not necessary and what is necessary is not required. His statement: 'because it occurs after not having been etc.' If you say: This only applies to the preponderance of the temporal, as his statement 'for if it were permissible for motion not to occur after rest etc.' indicates, so the proof does not apply to eternal possible attributes according to the Ash'arī view, even though the claim is general. I say: Even if granted, no one claims a distinction, so the causality of contingency in the temporal necessitates causality in others by primary inference, but there is something in it. His statement: 'it is the efficient cause' contains discussion, because if this proof were sound, it would entail that the Most High Creator is a locus for temporal events, namely the preponderances of temporal events; and if based on the philosophers' view, the Tenth Intellect would be a locus for them, even though they also do not say it. His statement: 'and it is the point of contention' - if you say: Rather, the point of contention here is more specific than what he mentioned, because the contention here is about whether the cause of the need is contingency or something else, not about whether the contingent needs a cause or not. I say: Among the opponents regarding contingency being the cause of the need are the proponents of coincidence, as already occurred and will come, so the contention with them is about the very need for a cause.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage contains critical notes on al-Razi's second method. First, it is argued that the method fails to prove the intended conclusion: it only shows that the contingent needs an efficient cause (لازم), but the desired claim (مطلوب) is that the cause of this need is contingency itself (الإمكان). Thus, the method's necessary consequence is not the required thesis. Second, the objection that the proof only applies to temporal contingents (because it uses the example of motion after rest) but not to eternal possible attributes (like God's attributes according to Ash'aris) is addressed: even if granted, no one makes a distinction, and the causality of contingency in the temporal implies it for others by priority (بطريق الأولى), though this is debatable. Third, the conclusion that the locus is the efficient cause is criticized because it would imply that God (or the Tenth Intellect in philosophy) becomes a locus for temporal preponderances, which is problematic. Finally, the commentator clarifies the real point of contention: it is not whether the contingent needs a cause, but whether contingency is the cause of that need. The opponents who deny contingency as the cause include the proponents of coincidence (الاتفاق), who deny the need for a cause altogether, so the debate with them is about the very need for a cause.

Key Terms
طريق ثاني
لازم
مطلوب
علة الاحتياج
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Razi's second method proves that contingency is the cause of the need for a cause.
Correction: The method only proves that the contingent needs a cause, not that contingency itself is the cause of that need.

Checkpoint
What is the main criticism of al-Razi's second method according to this passage?
A) It assumes the existence of the contingent before existence
B) It conflates the necessary consequence with the desired claim
C) It relies on the books of the philosophers
D) It does not mention Democritus

Key Takeaway
The method fails because it confuses the necessary consequence with the desired claim: it proves need but not the ground of need.

?
In your own life, where might you be confusing the mere proof of need with proof of the cause of that need? What would it cost you to admit that gap?
?
Are you willing to accept that some of your most cherished beliefs might be supported by arguments that confuse the necessary consequence with the desired claim? What would change if you faced that honestly today?

Addressing Objections and Clarifying the Real Contention
Sanusi responds to objections about scope and locus, and redefines the contention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the responses to objections about the method's scope and implications, and the real point of contention with opponents.

Core Idea
The real debate is about the cause of need, not the need itself.

After the initial logical critique, Sanusi addresses further objections that might be raised against his criticism. He also clarifies what the actual disagreement is about, distinguishing between those who deny contingency as a cause and those who deny the need for a cause altogether.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in full
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting each objection and response. The passage has several layers of debate.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Follow the objections and replies
Identify the three main points: scope objection (temporal vs eternal), implication objection (God as locus), and clarification of contention.
Notice how Sanusi uses 'if you say' and 'I say' to structure the debate.
3
Understand the final clarification
Sanusi ends by specifying that the real contention is about whether contingency is the cause of the need, and some opponents even deny the need for a cause.
This sets the stage for further discussion about the proponents of coincidence (ittifaq).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الطريق الثاني فيه نظر؛ لأن اللازم من هذا الطريق أن الممكن محتاج إلى المؤثر، وأما علة الاحتياج هو الإمكان فلا، فالمطلوب غير لازم واللازم غير مطلوب. قوله : (لأنه حصل بعد ما لم يكن الخ فإن قلت : هذا إنما يتم في ترجح الحادث، كما يدل عليه قوله لجاز أن لا تكون الحركة بعد السكون الخ، فلا يجري الدليل في الصفات القديمة الممكنة على رأي الأشاعرة مع أن المدعى عام قلت لو سلم فلا قائل بالفصل، فعلية : الإمكان في الحادث تستلزم العلية في غيره بطريق الأولى وفيه ما فيه . قوله: (فهو المؤثر) فيه بحث إذ لو صح هذا الدليل لزم كون الباري تعالى محلاً للحوادث، وهي ترجحات الحوادث الحادثة، ولو بني على رأي الفلاسفة كان العقل العاشر محلاً لها مع أنهم لا يقولون به أيضاً. قوله : ( وهو المتنازع فيه إن قلت بل المتنازع فيه هاهنا أخص مما ذكره، لأن النزاع هاهنا في أن علة الاحتياج هي الإمكان أو غيره لا أن الممكن هل يحتاج إلى علة أم لا، قلت : من جملة الخصوم في كون الإمكان علة للاحتياج القائلون بالاتفاق، كما سبق الآن . وسيأتي، فالنزاع معهم في نفس الاحتياج إلى العلة مالاً .
His statement: 'The second method: there is consideration in it, because what is necessary from this method is that the contingent needs the efficient cause, but the cause of the need is contingency, not [the method's conclusion], so the required [conclusion] is not necessary and what is necessary is not required. His statement: 'because it occurs after not having been etc.' If you say: This only applies to the preponderance of the temporal, as his statement 'for if it were permissible for motion not to occur after rest etc.' indicates, so the proof does not apply to eternal possible attributes according to the Ash'arī view, even though the claim is general. I say: Even if granted, no one claims a distinction, so the causality of contingency in the temporal necessitates causality in others by primary inference, but there is something in it. His statement: 'it is the efficient cause' contains discussion, because if this proof were sound, it would entail that the Most High Creator is a locus for temporal events, namely the preponderances of temporal events; and if based on the philosophers' view, the Tenth Intellect would be a locus for them, even though they also do not say it. His statement: 'and it is the point of contention' - if you say: Rather, the point of contention here is more specific than what he mentioned, because the contention here is about whether the cause of the need is contingency or something else, not about whether the contingent needs a cause or not. I say: Among the opponents regarding contingency being the cause of the need are the proponents of coincidence, as already occurred and will come, so the contention with them is about the very need for a cause.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage contains critical notes on al-Razi's second method. First, it is argued that the method fails to prove the intended conclusion: it only shows that the contingent needs an efficient cause (لازم), but the desired claim (مطلوب) is that the cause of this need is contingency itself (الإمكان). Thus, the method's necessary consequence is not the required thesis. Second, the objection that the proof only applies to temporal contingents (because it uses the example of motion after rest) but not to eternal possible attributes (like God's attributes according to Ash'aris) is addressed: even if granted, no one makes a distinction, and the causality of contingency in the temporal implies it for others by priority (بطريق الأولى), though this is debatable. Third, the conclusion that the locus is the efficient cause is criticized because it would imply that God (or the Tenth Intellect in philosophy) becomes a locus for temporal preponderances, which is problematic. Finally, the commentator clarifies the real point of contention: it is not whether the contingent needs a cause, but whether contingency is the cause of that need. The opponents who deny contingency as the cause include the proponents of coincidence (الاتفاق), who deny the need for a cause altogether, so the debate with them is about the very need for a cause.

الصفات القديمة الممكنة
المؤثر
محل للحوادث
اتفاق

?

How does this lesson challenge you to examine your own assumptions about causality and the need for a cause? When you reflect on this, does it shake your confidence in the rational foundations of tawheed? How does this push you to strengthen your conviction, and what will you do to ensure your faith is built on solid grounds?
?

What step will you take this week to learn how to respond to a specific objection about causality or the existence of God?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
The Second Argument: The Contingent Requires a Preponderance and a Cause
Sanusi presents the second argument from Imam al-Razi that the contingent requires a preponderance which is existential, necessitating a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's argument that the contingent, due to its contingency, requires a preponderance (tarajjuḥ) which is an existential attribute, necessitating an external cause.

Core Idea
A contingent's need for a cause arises from the existential preponderance of existence over non-existence.

Imam al-Razi presents a powerful argument: before a contingent exists, one of its two possibilities must become preponderant. This preponderance cannot be a mere mental consideration; it must be real and existential. After laying this foundation, the argument proceeds to show that this existential preponderance must reside in an external cause, not in the contingent itself. In this lesson, we will trace this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the flow of the argument from the preponderance to the cause.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'because' and 'so'.
2
Identify the key claim: preponderance is existential
The argument starts by claiming that the preponderance that brings the contingent into existence is an existential attribute. Find the reasoning: it comes after not being, so it cannot be nonexistent.
Contrast with the idea that preponderance might be a mere relation.
3
Understand the need for a locus
Since the preponderance is existential, it must inhere in a locus (maḥall) that is itself existent. Why can't it subsist by itself or in a non-existent?
4
See why the locus cannot be the possible itself
If the preponderance were in the possible, the possible would have to exist before its own existence—an absurd consequence. Therefore, the locus must be something else, which is the cause.
This is the contradiction argument.
5
Connect to the conclusion: the cause must exist
The argument concludes that the locus of the preponderance is the effecter (muʾaththir), i.e., the cause. The commentary explains the implications for temporal vs. eternal causes.
This links the need for a preponderance to the need for a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني اختاره الإمام الرازي لا بد للممكن قبل الوجود أن يترجح طرف أي يترجح طرف وجوده على عدمه بحيث يجب، لما سيأتي. وذلك الترجح الواصل إلى حد الوجوب صفة وجودية، لأنه حصل بعد ما لم يكن، فلو جاز أن لا يكون وجودياً لجاز أن لا تكون حركة بعد السكون، والعلم الحاصل بعد عدمه وجودي، وإذا كان الترجح أمراً وجودياً، فله محل موجود لامتناع قيامه بذاته أو بمعدوم آخر، وليس ذلك المحل هو الأثر أي الممكن، وإلا كان الأثر موجوداً قبله أي قبل الترجح السابق على وجوده فيكون الممكن موجوداً قبل وجوده بمرتبتين، هذا خلف. فلا من شيء آخر موجود يقوم به الترجح فهو المؤثر. قوله: ( لا بد للممكن (الخ لإمكانه وحاصله : أن الممكن لإمكانه يحتاج إلى الترجح المحتاج إلى المؤثر فيكون لإمكانه محتاجاً إلى المؤثر. قوله : ( لأنه حصل بعدما لم يكن أي في الممكنات الحادثة فتكون وجودية في الممكنات القديمة؛ لما مر من أن الاتصاف بالصفة التي من شأنها الوجود في الخارج فرع وجودها. قوله : (فهو المؤثر) أي المحل هو المؤثر فإن كان الترجح حادثاً كان المؤثر حادثاً ولو باعتبار بعض أجزائه أو شروطه، وإن كان قديماً يكون مؤثره قديماً، فلا يلزم كون المؤثر القديم محلاً للحوادث.
The second path: Imam al-Razi chose that for the contingent, before existence, one side must become preponderant, i.e., the side of its existence over its non-existence must become preponderant to the point of necessity, as will come. And that preponderance reaching the limit of necessity is an existential attribute, because it occurs after it was not. For if it were not existential, then it would be permissible for motion not to occur after rest, and the knowledge that comes after its non-existence is existential. And if the preponderance is an existential thing, then it has an existent locus, because it is impossible for it to subsist by itself or by another non-existent. And that locus is not the effect, i.e., the possible; otherwise the effect would be existent before it, i.e., before the preponderance that precedes its existence, so the possible would be existent before its existence by two degrees—this is a contradiction. So it must be another existent thing that the preponderance subsists in, and that is the effecter. His saying: '(there is no alternative for the possible... )' due to its contingency, and its summary is that the possible, due to its contingency, needs the preponderance, which needs the effecter; so by its contingency it is in need of the effecter. His saying: 'because it comes after it was not' means in the temporal possibles, so they are existential in the eternal possibles, because it has passed that being qualified by an attribute whose nature is to exist externally is a branch of its existence. His saying: 'so it is the effecter' means the locus is the effecter; if the preponderance is temporal, the effecter is temporal, even if by virtue of some of its parts or conditions; and if it is eternal, its effecter is eternal, so it does not necessitate that the eternal effecter is a locus for temporal events.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument from Imam al-Razi for the necessity of a cause for contingent beings. The author first states the argument: a contingent, before coming into existence, must have one of its two possibilities (existence or non-existence) become preponderant (tarajjuḥ) to the point of necessity. This preponderance is an existential attribute because it occurs after not having been; otherwise, if it were nonexistent, we could not explain the occurrence of motion after rest, which is a real change. Since it is existential, it requires an existent locus (maḥall) to inhere in, because an attribute cannot subsist by itself or in a nonexistent. This locus cannot be the possible (the effect) itself, because that would imply the effect exists before its own existence by two degrees (the preponderance and then the existence), which is absurd. Therefore, the locus must be something else, which is the cause (al-muʾaththir). The commentary then explains each part: the summary emphasizes that the possible needs the preponderance due to its contingency, and the preponderance needs an effecter; thus the need for a cause is rooted in contingency. The commentary also clarifies that the argument applies to both temporal and eternal possibles (on the Ashʿarite view that attributes are possible and eternal), and that whether the preponderance is temporal or eternal, the cause is correspondingly temporal or eternal, without making the eternal cause a locus for temporal events (if the preponderance is temporal, the cause is temporal in some aspect, but if eternal, the cause is eternal).

Key Terms
الترجح
الممكن
الوجوب
العلة
المؤثر
صفة وجودية
المحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the preponderance is a relational concept without existential reality, making the argument unnecessary.
Correction: The argument insists that the preponderance is a real existential attribute because it occurs after not being, and thus requires a real locus and cause.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why can the preponderance (tarajjuḥ) not inhere in the possible itself?
A) Because the possible is not yet existent before its existence.
B) Because the possible would then exist before its own existence, leading to a contradiction.
C) Because the possible is a substance and cannot receive attributes.
D) Because the preponderance is a mental consideration only.

Key Takeaway
The contingent's need for a cause is rooted in the existential nature of the preponderance that brings it into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very existence is a contingent possibility that has been preponderated to actuality. Do you live each day as if that preponderance is sustained by a cause outside yourself, or do you fall into the illusion of self-sufficiency? Take one moment today to consciously acknowledge that your next breath is a gift from the Preponderator.
?
The argument forces you to see that the preponderance cannot subsist in you — you are not the cause of your own existence. Is there an area of your life where you are acting as if you are the cause of your own success, goodness, or faith? Write down one such area and commit to attributing it to God today.
?
If the very act of existence requires an external cause, then every change in your heart — every turn from sin to obedience, every increase in certainty — is also a preponderance that requires a cause. Have you been seeking that cause? What would change if you sincerely asked the Causer to give you the preponderance of good over evil in your next decision?
